10030-8{_}701{_}I finally ventured home--not without some fear, however, of the Gobel family--and was delighted to learn that during my absence mother had had an interview with Mr. Gobel, and having settled the difficulty with him, the two families had become friends again, and I may state, incidentally, that they ever after remained so.
10030-8{_}702{_}I have since often met Stephen Gobel, and we have had many a laugh together over our love affair and the affray at the school-house.
10030-8{_}703{_}Mary Hyatt, the innocent cause of the whole difficulty, is now married and living in Chicago.
10030-8{_}704{_}Thus ended my first love scrape.
10030-8{_}705{_}In the winter of 1856-57 my father, in company with a man named J.C. Boles, went to Cleveland, Ohio, and organized a colony of about thirty families, whom they brought to Kansas and located on the Grasshopper.
10030-8{_}706{_}Several of these families still reside there.
10030-8{_}707{_}It was during this winter that father, after his return from Cleveland, caught a severe cold.
10030-8{_}708{_}This, in connection with the wound he had received at Rively's--from which he had never entirely recovered--affected him seriously, and in April, 1857, he died at home from kidney disease.
10030-8{_}709{_}This sad event left my mother and the family in poor circumstances, and I determined to follow the plains for a livelihood for them and myself.
10030-8{_}710{_}I had no difficulty in obtaining work under my old employers, and in May, 1857, I started for Salt Lake City with a herd of beef cattle, in charge of Frank and Bill McCarthy, for General Albert Sidney Johnson's army, which was then being sent across the plains to fight the Mormons.
10030-8{_}711{_}Nothing occurred to interrupt our journey until we reached Plum Creek, on the South Platte river, thirty-five miles west of Old Fort Kearney.
10030-8{_}712{_}We had made a morning drive and had camped for dinner.
10030-8{_}713{_}The wagon-masters and a majority of the men had gone to sleep under the mess wagons; the cattle were being guarded by three men, and the cook was preparing dinner.
10030-8{_}714{_}No one had any idea that Indians were anywhere near us.
10030-8{_}715{_}The first warning we had that they were infesting that part of the country was the firing of shots and the whoops and yells from a party of them, who, catching us napping, gave us a most unwelcome surprise.
10030-8{_}716{_}All the men jumped to their feet and seized their guns.
10030-8{_}717{_}They saw with astonishment the cattle running in every direction, they having been stampeded by the Indians, who had shot and killed the three men who were on day-herd duty, and the red devils were now charging down upon the rest of us.
10030-8{_}718{_}I then thought of mother's fears of my falling into the hands of the Indians, and I had about made up my mind that such was to be my fate; but when I saw how coolly and determinedly the McCarthy brothers were conducting themselves and giving orders to the little band, I became convinced that we would "stand the Indians off," as the saying is.
10030-8{_}719{_}Our men were all well armed with Colt's revolvers and Mississippi yagers, which last, carried a bullet, and two buckshots.
10030-8{_}720{_}The McCarthy boys, at the proper moment, gave orders to fire upon the advancing enemy.
10030-8{_}721{_}The volley checked them, although they returned the compliment, and shot one of our party through the leg.
10030-8{_}722{_}Frank McCarthy then sang out, "Boys, make a break for the slough yonder, and we can then have the bank for a breast-work."
10030-8{_}723{_}[Illustration: KILLING MY FIRST INDIAN.]
10030-8{_}724{_}We made a run for the slough which was only a short distance off, and succeeded in safely reaching it, bringing with us the wounded man.
10030-8{_}725{_}The bank proved to be a very effective breast-work, affording us good protection.
10030-8{_}726{_}We had been there but a short time when Frank McCarthy, seeing that the longer we were corraled the worse it would be for us, said:  "Well, boys, we'll try to make our way back to Fort Kearney by wading in the river and keeping the bank for a breast-work."
10030-8{_}727{_}We all agreed that this was the best plan, and we accordingly proceeded down the river several miles in this way, managing to keep the Indians at a safe distance with our guns, until the slough made a junction with the main Platte river.
10030-8{_}728{_}From there down we found the river at times quite deep, and in order to carry the wounded man along with us we constructed a raft of poles for his accommodation, and in this way he was transported.
10030-8{_}729{_}Occasionally the water would be too deep for us to wade, and we were obliged to put our weapons on the raft and swim.
10030-8{_}730{_}The Indians followed us pretty close, and were continually watching for an opportunity to get a good range and give us a raking fire.
10030-8{_}731{_}Covering ourselves by keeping well under the bank, we pushed ahead as rapidly as possible, and made pretty good progress, the night finding us still on the way and our enemies still on our track.
10030-8{_}732{_}I being the youngest and smallest of the party, became somewhat tired, and without noticing it I had fallen behind the others for some little distance.
10030-8{_}733{_}It was about ten o'clock and we were keeping very quiet and hugging close to the bank, when I happened to look up to the moon-lit sky and saw the plumed head of an Indian peeping over the bank.
10030-8{_}734{_}Instead of hurrying ahead and alarming the men in a quiet way, I instantly aimed my gun at the head and fired.
10030-8{_}735{_}The report rang out sharp and loud on the night air, and was immediately followed by an Indian whoop, and the next moment about six feet of dead Indian came tumbling into the river.
10030-8{_}736{_}I was not only overcome with astonishment, but was badly scared, as I could hardly realize what I had done.
10030-8{_}737{_}I expected to see the whole force of Indians come down upon us.
10030-8{_}738{_}While I was standing thus bewildered, the men, who had heard the shot and the war-whoop and had seen the Indian take a tumble, came rushing back.
10030-8{_}739{_}"Who fired that shot?"
10030-8{_}740{_}cried Frank McCarthy.
10030-8{_}741{_}"I did," replied I, rather proudly, as my confidence returned and I saw the men coming up.
10030-8{_}742{_}"Yes, and little Billy has killed an Indian stone-dead--too dead to skin," said one of the men, who had approached nearer than the rest, and had almost stumbled upon the corpse.
10030-8{_}743{_}From that time forward I became a hero and an Indian killer.
10030-8{_}744{_}This was, of course, the first Indian I had ever shot, and as I was not then more than eleven years of age, my exploit created quite a sensation.
10030-8{_}745{_}The other Indians, upon learning what had happened to their "advance guard," set up a terrible howling, and fired several volleys at us, but without doing any injury, as we were so well protected by the bank.
10030-8{_}746{_}We resumed our journey down the river, and traveled all night long.
10030-8{_}747{_}Just before daylight, Frank McCarthy crawled out over the bank and discovered that we were only five miles from Fort Kearney, which post we reached in safety in about two hours,--shortly after _reveille_--bringing the wounded man with us.
10030-8{_}748{_}It was indeed a relief to us all to feel that once more we were safe.
10030-8{_}749{_}Frank McCarthy immediately reported to the commanding officer and informed him of all that had happened.
10030-8{_}750{_}The commandant at once ordered a company of cavalry and one of infantry to proceed to Plum Creek on a forced march--taking a howitzer with them--to endeavor to recapture the cattle from the Indians.
10030-8{_}751{_}The firm of Russell, Majors & Waddell had a division agent at Kearney, and this agent mounted us on mules so that we could accompany the troops.
10030-8{_}752{_}On reaching the place where the Indians had surprised us, we found the bodies of the three men whom they had killed and scalped, and literally cut into pieces.
10030-8{_}753{_}We of course buried the remains.
10030-8{_}754{_}We caught but few of the cattle; the most of them having been driven off and stampeded with the buffaloes, there being numerous immense herds of the latter in that section of the country at that time.
10030-8{_}755{_}The Indian's trail was discovered running south towards the Republican river, and the troops followed it to the head of Plum Creek, and there abandoned it, returning to Fort Kearney without having seen a single red-skin.
10030-8{_}756{_}The company's agent, seeing that there was no further use for us in that vicinity--as we had lost our cattle and mules--sent us back to Fort Leavenworth.
10030-8{_}757{_}The company, it is proper to state, did not have to stand the loss of the expedition, as the government held itself responsible for such depredations by the Indians.
10030-8{_}758{_}On the day that I got into Leavenworth, sometime in July, I was interviewed for the first time in my life by a newspaper reporter, and the next morning I found my name in print as "the youngest Indian slayer on the plains."
10030-8{_}759{_}I am candid enough to admit that I felt very much elated over this notoriety.
10030-8{_}760{_}Again and again I read with eager interest the long and sensational account of our adventure.
10030-8{_}761{_}My exploit was related in a very graphic manner, and for a long time afterwards I was considerable of a hero.
10030-8{_}762{_}The reporter who had thus set me up, as I then thought, on the highest pinnacle of fame, was John Hutchinson, and I felt very grateful to him.
10030-8{_}763{_}He now lives in Wichita, Kansas.
10030-8{_}764{_}CHAPTER V.  IN BUSINESS.
10030-8{_}765{_}In the summer of 1857, Russell, Majors & Waddell were sending a great many trains across the plains to Salt Lake with supplies for General Johnston's army.
10030-8{_}766{_}Men were in great demand, and the company was paying teamsters forty dollars per month in gold.
10030-8{_}767{_}An old and reliable wagon-master, named Lewis Simpson--who had taken a great fancy to me, and who, by the way, was one of the best wagon-masters that ever ran a bull train--was loading a train for the company, and was about to start out with it for Salt Lake.
10030-8{_}768{_}He asked me to go along as an "extra hand."
10030-8{_}769{_}The high wages that were being paid were a great inducement to me, and the position of an "extra hand" was a pleasant one.
10030-8{_}770{_}All that I would have to do would be to take the place of any man who became sick, and drive his wagon until he recovered.
10030-8{_}771{_}I would have my own mule to ride, and to a certain extent I would be a minor boss.
10030-8{_}772{_}My mother was very much opposed to my taking this long trip, as I would be absent nearly a year, and there was a possibility that something might arise to prevent me from ever coming back, as we could not often tell how the Mormon difficulty would terminate.
10030-8{_}773{_}Then again, owing to the Indians, a journey over the plains in those days was a perilous undertaking.
10030-8{_}774{_}She said that as I had recently returned from the plains, and had had a narrow escape from death at the hands of the Indians, she did not want me to risk my life a second time.
10030-8{_}775{_}I told her that inasmuch as I had determined to follow the plains for an occupation, nothing could now stop me from going on this trip, and if it became necessary I would run away.
10030-8{_}776{_}Seeing that it was impossible to keep me at home, she reluctantly gave her consent, but not until she had called upon Mr. Russell and Mr. Simpson in regard to the matter, and had obtained from the latter gentleman his promise that I should be well taken care of, if we had to winter in the mountains.
10030-8{_}777{_}She did not like the appearance of Simpson, and upon inquiry she learned, to her dismay, that he was a desperate character, and that on nearly every trip he had made across the plains he had killed some one.
10030-8{_}778{_}Such a man, she thought, was not a fit master or companion for her son, and she was very anxious to have me go with some other wagon-master; but I still insisted upon remaining with Simpson.
10030-8{_}779{_}"Madam, I can assure you that Lew.
10030-8{_}780{_}Simpson is one of the most reliable wagon-masters on the plains," said Mr. Russell, "and he has taken a great fancy to Billy.
10030-8{_}781{_}If your boy is bound to go, he can go with no better man.
10030-8{_}782{_}No one will dare to impose on him while he is with Lew.
10030-8{_}783{_}Simpson, whom I will instruct to take good care of the boy.
10030-8{_}784{_}Upon reaching Fort Laramie, Billy can, if he wishes, exchange places with some fresh man coming back on a returning train, and thus come home without making the whole trip."
10030-8{_}785{_}This seemed to satisfy mother, and then she had a long talk with Simpson himself, imploring him not to forget his promise to take good care of her precious boy.
10030-8{_}786{_}He promised everything that she asked.
10030-8{_}787{_}Thus, after much trouble, I became one of the members of Simpson's train.
10030-8{_}788{_}Before taking our departure, I arranged with Russell, Majors & Waddell that when my pay should fall due it should be paid over to mother.
10030-8{_}789{_}As a matter of interest to the general reader, it may be well in this connection to give a brief description of a freight train.
10030-8{_}790{_}The wagons used in those days by Russell, Majors & Waddell were known as the "J. Murphy wagons," made at St. Louis specially for the plains business.
10030-8{_}791{_}They were very large and were strongly built, being capable of carrying seven thousand pounds of freight each.
10030-8{_}792{_}The wagon-boxes were very commodious--being as large as the rooms of an ordinary house--and were covered with two heavy canvas sheets to protect the merchandise from the rain.
10030-8{_}793{_}These wagons were generally sent out from Leavenworth, each loaded with six thousand pounds of freight, and each drawn by several yokes of oxen in charge of one driver.
10030-8{_}794{_}A train consisted of twenty-five wagons, all in charge of one man, who was known as the wagon-master.
10030-8{_}795{_}The second man in command was the assistant wagon-master; then came the "extra hand," next the night herder; and lastly, the cavallard driver, whose duty it was to drive the lame and loose cattle.
10030-8{_}796{_}There were thirty-one men all told in a train.
10030-8{_}797{_}The men did their own cooking, being divided into messes of seven.
10030-8{_}798{_}One man cooked, another brought wood and water, another stood guard, and so on, each having some duty to perform while getting meals.
10030-8{_}799{_}All were heavily armed with Colt's pistols and Mississippi yagers, and every one always had his weapons handy so as to be prepared for any emergency.
10030-8{_}800{_}The wagon-master, in the language of the plains, was called the "bull-wagon boss"; the teamsters were known as "bull-whackers"; and the whole train was denominated a "bull-outfit."
10030-8{_}801{_}Everything at that time was called an "outfit."
10030-8{_}802{_}The men of the plains were always full of droll humor and exciting stories of their own experiences, and many an hour I spent in listening to the recitals of thrilling adventures and hair-breadth escapes.
10030-8{_}803{_}Russell, Majors & Waddell had in their employ two hundred and fifty trains, composed of 6,250 wagons, 75,000 oxen, and about eight thousand men; their business reaching to all the government frontier posts in the north and west, to which they transported supplies, and they also carried freight as far south as New Mexico.
10030-8{_}804{_}[Illustration: A PRAIRIE SCHOONER.]
10030-8{_}805{_}The trail to Salt Lake ran through Kansas to the northwest, crossing the Big Blue river, then over the Big and Little Sandy, coming into Nebraska near the Big Sandy.
10030-8{_}806{_}The next stream of any importance was the Little Blue, along which the trail ran for sixty miles; then crossed a range of sand-hills and struck the Platte river ten miles below Old Fort Kearney; thence the course lay up the South Platte to the old Ash Hollow Crossing, thence eighteen miles across to the North Platte--near the mouth of the Blue Water, where General Harney had his great battle in 1855 with the Sioux and Cheyenne Indians.
10030-8{_}807{_}From this point the North Platte was followed, passing Court House Rock, Chimney Rock and Scott's Bluffs, and then on to Fort Laramie, where the Laramie River was crossed.
10030-8{_}808{_}Still following the North Platte for some considerable distance, the trail crossed this river at old Richard's Bridge, and followed it up to the celebrated Red Buttes--crossing the Willow creeks to the Sweet Water, passing the great Independence Rock and the Devil's gate, up to the Three Crossings of the Sweet Water, thence past the Cold Springs, where, three feet under the sod, on the hottest day of summer, ice can be found; thence to the Hot Springs and the Rocky Ridge, and through the Rocky Mountains and Echo Cañon, and thence on to the Great Salt Lake valley.
10030-8{_}809{_}We had started on our trip with everything in good shape, following the above described trail.
10030-8{_}810{_}During the first week or two out, I became well acquainted with most of the train men, and with one in particular, who became a life-long and intimate friend of mine.
10030-8{_}811{_}His real name was James B. Hickok; he afterwards became famous as "Wild Bill, the Scout of the Plains"--though why he was so called I never could ascertain--and from this time forward I shall refer to him by his popular nickname.
10030-8{_}812{_}He was ten years my senior--a tall, handsome, magnificently built and powerful young fellow, who could out-run, out-jump and out-fight any man in the train.
10030-8{_}813{_}He was generally admitted to be the best man physically, in the employ of Russell, Majors & Waddell; and of his bravery there was not a doubt.
10030-8{_}814{_}General Custer, in his "Life on the Plains," thus speaks of Wild Bill:         *       *       *       *       *  "Among the white scouts were numbered some of the most noted of their class.
10030-8{_}815{_}The most prominent man among them was 'Wild Bill,' whose highly varied career was made the subject of an illustrated sketch in one of the popular monthly periodicals a few years ago.
10030-8{_}816{_}'Wild Bill' was a strange character, just the one which a novelist might gloat over.
10030-8{_}817{_}He was a plains-man in every sense of the word, yet unlike any other of his class.
10030-8{_}818{_}In person he was about six feet and one inch in height, straight as the straightest of the warriors whose implacable foe he was.
10030-8{_}819{_}He had broad shoulders, well-formed chest and limbs, and a face strikingly handsome; a sharp, clear blue eye, which stared you straight in the face when in conversation; a finely shaped nose, inclined to be aquiline; a well-turned mouth, with lips only partially concealed by a handsome moustache.
10030-8{_}820{_}His hair and complexion were those of the perfect blonde.
10030-8{_}821{_}The former was worn in uncut ringlets, falling carelessly over his powerfully formed shoulders.
10030-8{_}822{_}Add to this figure a costume blending the immaculate neatness of the dandy with the extravagant taste and style of the frontiersman, and you have Wild Bill....
10030-8{_}823{_}Whether on foot or on horseback, he was one of the most perfect types of physical manhood I ever saw.
10030-8{_}824{_}"Of his courage there could be no question; it had been brought to the test on too many occasions to admit of a doubt.
10030-8{_}825{_}His skill in the use of the pistol and rifle was unerring; while his deportment was exactly the opposite of what might be expected from a man of his surroundings.
10030-8{_}826{_}It was entirely free from all bluster or bravado.
10030-8{_}827{_}He seldom spoke himself unless requested to do so.
10030-8{_}828{_}His conversation, strange to say, never bordered either on the vulgar or blasphemous.
10030-8{_}829{_}His influence among the frontiersmen was unbounded, his word was law; and many are the personal quarrels and disturbances which he has checked among his comrades by his simple announcement that 'This has gone far enough,'--if need be followed by the ominous warning that when persisted in or renewed the quarreler 'must settle it with me.'
10030-8{_}830{_}"Wild Bill was anything but a quarrelsome man; yet no one but him could enumerate the many conflicts in which he had been engaged, and which had almost always resulted in the death of his adversary.
10030-8{_}831{_}I have a personal knowledge of at least half a dozen men whom he had at various times killed, one of these being at the time a member of my command.
10030-8{_}832{_}Others had been severely wounded, yet he always escaped unhurt.
10030-8{_}833{_}"On the plains every man openly carries his belt with its invariable appendages, knife and revolver--often two of the latter.
10030-8{_}834{_}Wild Bill always carried two handsome ivory-handled revolvers of the large size; he was never seen without them....
10030-8{_}835{_}Yet in all the many affairs of this kind in which Wild Bill has performed a part, and which have come to my knowledge, there was not a single instance in which the verdict of twelve fair-minded men would not have been pronounced in his favor."
10030-8{_}836{_}*       *       *       *       *  [Illustration: WILD BILL.]
10030-8{_}837{_}Such is the faithful picture of Wild Bill as drawn by General Custer, who was a close observer and student of personal character, and under whom Wild Bill served as a scout.
10030-8{_}838{_}The circumstances under which I first made his acquaintance and learned to know him well and to appreciate his manly character and kind-heartedness, were these.
10030-8{_}839{_}One of the teamsters in Lew.
10030-8{_}840{_}Simpson's train was a surly, overbearing fellow, and took particular delight in bullying and tyrannizing over me, and one day while we were at dinner he asked me to do something for him.
10030-8{_}841{_}I did not start at once, and he gave me a slap in the face with the back of his hand,--knocking me off an ox-yoke on which I was sitting, and sending me sprawling on the ground.
10030-8{_}842{_}Jumping to my feet I picked up a camp kettle full of boiling coffee which was setting on the fire, and threw it at him.
10030-8{_}843{_}I hit him in the face, and the hot coffee gave him a severe scalding.
10030-8{_}844{_}He sprang for me with the ferocity of a tiger, and would undoubtedly have torn me to pieces, had it not been for the timely interference of my new-found friend, Wild Bill, who knocked the man down.
10030-8{_}845{_}As soon as he recovered himself, he demanded of Wild Bill what business it was of his that he should "put in his oar."
10030-8{_}846{_}"It's my business to protect that boy, or anybody else, from being unmercifully abused, kicked and cuffed, and I'll whip any man who tries it on," said Wild Bill; "and if you ever again lay a hand on that boy--little Billy there--I'll give you such a pounding that you won't get over it for a month of Sundays."
10030-8{_}847{_}From that time forward Wild Bill was my protector and intimate friend, and the friendship thus begun continued until his death.
10030-8{_}848{_}Nothing transpired on the trip to delay or give us any trouble whatever, until the train struck the South Platte river.
10030-8{_}849{_}One day we camped on the same ground where the Indians had surprised the cattle herd, in charge of the McCarty brothers.
10030-8{_}850{_}It was with difficulty that we discovered any traces of anybody ever having camped there before, the only landmark being the single grave, now covered with grass, in which we had buried the three men who had been killed.
10030-8{_}851{_}The country was alive with buffaloes.
10030-8{_}852{_}Vast herds of these monarchs of the plains were roaming all around us, and we laid over one day for a grand hunt.
10030-8{_}853{_}Besides killing quite a number of buffaloes, and having a day of rare sport, we captured ten or twelve head of cattle, they being a portion of the herd which had been stampeded by the Indians, two months before.
10030-8{_}854{_}The next day we pulled out of camp, and the train was strung out to a considerable length along the road which ran near the foot of the sand-hills, two miles from the river.
10030-8{_}855{_}Between the road and the river we saw a large herd of buffaloes grazing quietly, they having been down to the stream for a drink.
10030-8{_}856{_}Just at this time we observed a party of returning Californians coming from the West.
10030-8{_}857{_}They, too, noticed the buffalo herd, and in another moment they were dashing down upon them, urging their steeds to the greatest speed.
10030-8{_}858{_}The buffalo herd stampeded at once, and broke for the hills; so hotly were they pursued by the hunters that about five hundred of them rushed through our train pell-mell, frightening both men and oxen.
10030-8{_}859{_}Some of the wagons were turned clear round, and many of the terrified oxen attempted to run to the hills, with the heavy wagons attached to them.
10030-8{_}860{_}Others turned around so short that they broke the wagon tongues off.
10030-8{_}861{_}Nearly all the teams got entangled in their gearing, and became wild and unruly, so that the perplexed drivers were unable to manage them.
10030-8{_}862{_}The buffaloes, the cattle, and the drivers, were soon running in every direction, and the excitement upset nearly everybody and everything.
10030-8{_}863{_}Many of the cattle broke their yokes and stampeded.
10030-8{_}864{_}One big buffalo bull became entangled in one of the heavy wagon-chains, and it is a fact that in his desperate efforts to free himself, he not only actually snapped the strong chain in two, but broke the ox-yoke to which it was attached, and the last seen of him he was running towards the hills with it hanging from his horns.
10030-8{_}865{_}A dozen other equally remarkable incidents happened during the short time that the frantic buffaloes were playing havoc with our train, and when they had got through and left us, our outfit was very badly crippled and scattered.
10030-8{_}866{_}This caused us to go into camp and spend a day in replacing the broken tongues, and repairing other damages, and gathering up our scattered ox-teams.
10030-8{_}867{_}The next day we rolled out of camp, and proceeded on our way towards the setting sun.
10030-8{_}868{_}Everything ran along smoothly with us from that point until we came within about eighteen miles of Green river, in the Rocky mountains--where we camped at noon.
10030-8{_}869{_}At this place we had to drive our cattle about a mile and a half to a creek to water them.
10030-8{_}870{_}Simpson, his assistant, George Woods and myself, accompanied by the usual number of guards, drove the cattle over to the creek, and while on our way back to camp, we suddenly observed a party of twenty horsemen rapidly approaching us.
10030-8{_}871{_}We were not yet in view of our wagons, as a rise of ground intervened, and therefore we could not signal the train-men in case of any unexpected danger befalling us.
10030-8{_}872{_}We had no suspicion, however, that we were about to be trapped, as the strangers were white men.
10030-8{_}873{_}When they had come up to us, one of the party, who evidently was the leader, rode out in front and said:  "How are you, Mr.
10030-8{_}874{_}Simpson?"
10030-8{_}875{_}"You've got the best of me, sir," said Simpson, who did not know him.
10030-8{_}876{_}"Well, I rather think I have," coolly replied the stranger, whose words conveyed a double meaning, as we soon learned.
10030-8{_}877{_}We had all come to a halt by this time, and the strange horsemen had surrounded us.
10030-8{_}878{_}They were all armed with double-barreled shot guns, rifles and revolvers.
10030-8{_}879{_}We also were armed with revolvers, but we had had no idea of danger, and these men, much to our surprise, had "got the drop" on us, and had covered us with their weapons, so that we were completely at their mercy.
10030-8{_}880{_}The whole movement of corraling us was done so quietly and quickly that it was accomplished before we knew it.
10030-8{_}881{_}"I'll trouble you for your six shooters, gentlemen," now said the leader.
10030-8{_}882{_}"I'll give 'em to you in a way you don't want," replied Simpson.
10030-8{_}883{_}The next moment three guns were leveled at Simpson.
10030-8{_}884{_}"If you make a move you're a dead man," said the leader.
10030-8{_}885{_}Simpson saw that he was taken at a great disadvantage, and thinking it advisable not to risk the lives of the party by any rash act on his part, he said: "I see now that you have the best of me, but who are you, anyhow?"
10030-8{_}886{_}"I am Joe Smith," was the reply.
10030-8{_}887{_}"What!
10030-8{_}888{_}the leader of the Danites?"
10030-8{_}889{_}asked Simpson.
10030-8{_}890{_}"You are correct," said Smith, for he it was.
10030-8{_}891{_}"Yes," said Simpson, "I know you now; you are a spying scoundrel."
10030-8{_}892{_}Simpson had good reason for calling him this and applying to him a much more opprobrious epithet, for only a short time before this, Joe Smith had visited our train in the disguise of a teamster, and had remained with us two days.
10030-8{_}893{_}He suddenly disappeared, no one knowing where he had gone or why he had come among us.
10030-8{_}894{_}But it was all explained to us now that he had returned with his Mormon Danites.
10030-8{_}895{_}After they had disarmed us, Simpson asked, "Well, Smith, what are you going to do with us?"
10030-8{_}896{_}"Ride back with us and I'll soon show you," said Smith.
10030-8{_}897{_}We had no idea of the surprise which awaited us.
10030-8{_}898{_}As we came upon the top of the ridge, from which we could view our camp, we were astonished to see the remainder of the train men disarmed and stationed in a group and surrounded by another squad of Danites, while other Mormons were searching our wagons for such articles as they wanted.
10030-8{_}899{_}"How is this?"
10030-8{_}900{_}inquired Simpson.
10030-8{_}901{_}"How did you surprise my camp without a struggle?
10030-8{_}902{_}I can't understand it."
10030-8{_}903{_}"Easily enough," said Smith; "your men were all asleep under the wagons, except the cooks, who saw us coming and took us for returning Californians or emigrants, and paid no attention to us until we rode up and surrounded your train.
10030-8{_}904{_}With our arms covering the men, we woke them up, and told them that all they had to do was to walk out and drop their pistols--which they saw was the best thing they could do under circumstances over which they had no control--and you can just bet they did it."
10030-8{_}905{_}"And what do you propose to do with us now?"
10030-8{_}906{_}asked Simpson.
10030-8{_}907{_}"I intend to burn your train," said he; "you are loaded with supplies and ammunition for Sidney Johnson, and as I have no way to convey the stuff to my own people, I'll see that it does not reach the United States troops."
10030-8{_}908{_}"Are you going to turn us adrift here?"
10030-8{_}909{_}asked Simpson, who was anxious to learn what was to become of himself and his men.
10030-8{_}910{_}"No; I hardly am as bad as that.
10030-8{_}911{_}I'll give you enough provisions to last you until you can reach Fort Bridger," replied Smith; "and as soon as your cooks can get the stuff out of the wagons, you can start."
10030-8{_}912{_}"On foot?"
10030-8{_}913{_}was the laconic inquiry of Simpson.
10030-8{_}914{_}"Yes sir," was the equally short reply.
10030-8{_}915{_}"Smith, that's too rough on us men.
10030-8{_}916{_}Put yourself in our place and see how you would like it," said Simpson; "you can well afford to give us at least one wagon and six yokes of oxen to convey us and our clothing and provisions to Fort Bridger.
10030-8{_}917{_}You're a brute if you don't do this."
10030-8{_}918{_}"Well," said Smith, after consulting a minute or two with some of his company, "I'll do that much for you."
10030-8{_}919{_}The cattle and the wagon were brought up according to his orders, and the clothing and provisions were loaded on.
10030-8{_}920{_}"Now you can go," said Smith, after everything had been arranged.
10030-8{_}921{_}"Joe Smith, I think you are a mean coward to set us afloat in a hostile country, without giving us our arms," said Simpson, who had once before asked for the weapons, and had had his request denied.
10030-8{_}922{_}Smith, after further consultation with his comrades, said: "Simpson, you are too brave a man to be turned adrift here without any means of defense.
10030-8{_}923{_}You shall have your revolvers and guns."
10030-8{_}924{_}Our weapons were accordingly handed over to Simpson, and we at once started for Fort Bridger, knowing that it would be useless to attempt the recapture of our train.
10030-8{_}925{_}When we had traveled about two miles we saw the smoke arising from our old camp.
10030-8{_}926{_}The Mormons after taking what goods they wanted and could carry off, had set fire to the wagons, many of which were loaded with bacon, lard, hard-tack, and other provisions, which made a very hot, fierce fire, and the smoke to roll up in dense clouds.
10030-8{_}927{_}Some of the wagons were loaded with ammunition, and it was not long before loud explosions followed in rapid succession.
10030-8{_}928{_}We waited and witnessed the burning of the train, and then pushed on to Fort Bridger.
10030-8{_}929{_}Arriving at this post, we learned that two other trains had been captured and destroyed in the same way, by the Mormons.
10030-8{_}930{_}This made seventy-five wagon loads, or 450,000 pounds of supplies, mostly provisions, which never reached General Johnson's command, to which they had been consigned.
10030-8{_}931{_}CHAPTER VI.
10030-8{_}932{_}HARD TIMES.
10030-8{_}933{_}As it was getting very late in the fall, we were compelled to winter at Fort Bridger; and a long, tedious winter it was.
10030-8{_}934{_}There were a great many troops there, and about four hundred of Russell, Majors & Waddell's employees.
10030-8{_}935{_}These men were all organized into militia companies, which were officered by the wagon-masters.
10030-8{_}936{_}Some lived in tents, others in cabins.
10030-8{_}937{_}It was known that our supplies would run short during the winter, and so all the men at the post were put on three-quarter rations to begin with; before long they were reduced to one-half rations, and finally to one-quarter rations.
10030-8{_}938{_}We were forced to kill our poor worn-out cattle for beef.
10030-8{_}939{_}They were actually so poor that we had to prop them up to shoot them down.
10030-8{_}940{_}At last we fell back on the mules, which were killed and served up in good style.
10030-8{_}941{_}Many a poor, unsuspecting government mule passed in his chips that winter in order to keep the soldiers and bull-whackers from starvation.
10030-8{_}942{_}It was really a serious state of affairs.
10030-8{_}943{_}The wood for the post was obtained from the mountains, but having no longer any cattle or mules to transport it, the men were obliged to haul it themselves.
10030-8{_}944{_}Long lariats were tied to the wagons, and twenty men manning each, they were pulled to and from the mountains.
10030-8{_}945{_}Notwithstanding all these hardships, the men seemed to be contented and to enjoy themselves.
10030-8{_}946{_}The winter finally passed away, and early in the spring, as soon as we could travel, the civil employees of the government, with the teamsters and freighters, started for the Missouri river; the Johnson expedition having been abandoned.
10030-8{_}947{_}On the way down we stopped at Fort Laramie, and there met a supply train bound westward.
10030-8{_}948{_}Of course we all had a square meal once more, consisting of hard tack, bacon, coffee and beans.
10030-8{_}949{_}I can honestly say that I thought it was the best meal I had ever eaten; at least I relished it more than any other, and I think the rest of the party did the same.
10030-8{_}950{_}On leaving Fort Laramie, Simpson was made brigade wagon-master, and was put in charge of two large trains, with about four hundred extra men, who were bound for Fort Leavenworth.
10030-8{_}951{_}When we came to Ash Hollow, instead of taking the usual trail over to the South Platte, Simpson concluded to follow the North Platte down to its junction with the South Platte.
10030-8{_}952{_}The two trains were traveling about fifteen miles apart, when one morning while Simpson was with the rear train, he told his assistant wagon-master, George Woods and myself to saddle up our mules, as he wanted us to go with him and overtake the head train.
10030-8{_}953{_}We started off at about eleven o'clock, and had ridden about seven miles when--while we were on a big plateau, back of Cedar Bluffs--we suddenly discovered a band of Indians coming out of the head of a ravine, half a mile distant, and charging down upon us at full speed.
10030-8{_}954{_}I thought that our end had come this time, sure.
10030-8{_}955{_}Simpson, however, took in the situation in a moment, and knowing that it would be impossible to escape by running our played-out mules, he adopted a bolder and much better plan.
10030-8{_}956{_}He jumped from his own mule, and told us to dismount also.
10030-8{_}957{_}He then shot the three animals, and as they fell to the ground he cut their throats to stop their kicking.
10030-8{_}958{_}He then jerked them into the shape of a triangle, and ordered us inside of the barricade.
10030-8{_}959{_}All this was but the work of a few moments, yet it was not done any too soon, for the Indians had got within three hundred yards of us, and were still advancing, and uttering their demoniacal yells or war-whoops.
10030-8{_}960{_}There were forty of the red-skins and only three of us.
10030-8{_}961{_}We were each armed with a Mississippi yager and two Colt's revolvers.
10030-8{_}962{_}"Get ready for them with your guns, and when they come within fifty yards, aim low, blaze away and bring down your man!"
10030-8{_}963{_}Such was the quick command of Simpson.
10030-8{_}964{_}The words had hardly escaped from his mouth, when the three yagers almost simultaneously belched forth their contents.
10030-8{_}965{_}We then seized our revolvers and opened a lively fire on the enemy, at short range, which checked their advance.
10030-8{_}966{_}Then we looked over our little barricade to ascertain what effect our fire had produced, and were much gratified at seeing three dead Indians and one horse lying on the ground.
10030-8{_}967{_}Only two or three of the Indians, it seemed, had fire-arms.
10030-8{_}968{_}It must be remembered that in those days every Indian did not own a needle gun or a Winchester rifle, as they now do.
10030-8{_}969{_}Their principal weapons were their bows and arrows.
10030-8{_}970{_}Seeing that they could not take our little fortification, or drive us from it, they circled around us several times, shooting their arrows at us.
10030-8{_}971{_}One of the arrows struck George Wood in the left shoulder, inflicting only a slight wound, however, and several lodged in the bodies of the dead mules; otherwise they did us no harm.
10030-8{_}972{_}The Indians finally galloped off to a safe distance, where our bullets could not reach them, and seemed to be holding a council.
10030-8{_}973{_}This was a lucky move for us, for it gave us an opportunity to reload our guns and pistols, and prepare for the next charge of the enemy.
10030-8{_}974{_}During the brief cessation of hostilities, Simpson extracted the arrow from Wood's shoulder, and put an immense quid of tobacco on the wound.
10030-8{_}975{_}Wood was then ready for business again.
10030-8{_}976{_}[Illustration: HOLDING THE FORT.]
10030-8{_}977{_}The Indians did not give us a very long rest, for with, another desperate charge, as if to ride over us, they came dashing towards the mule barricade.
10030-8{_}978{_}We gave them a hot reception from our yagers and revolvers.
10030-8{_}979{_}They could not stand, or understand, the rapidly repeating fire of the revolvers, and we again checked them.
10030-8{_}980{_}They circled around us once more and gave us a few parting shots as they rode off, leaving behind them another dead Indian and a horse.
10030-8{_}981{_}For two hours afterwards they did not seem to be doing anything but holding a council.
10030-8{_}982{_}We made good use of this time by digging up the ground inside the barricade with our knives and throwing the loose earth around and over the mules, and we soon had a very respectable fortification.
10030-8{_}983{_}We were not troubled any more that day, but during the night the cunning rascals tried to burn us out by setting fire to the prairie.
10030-8{_}984{_}The buffalo grass was so short that the fire did not trouble us much, but the smoke concealed the Indians from our view, and they thought that they could approach close to us without being seen.
10030-8{_}985{_}We were aware of this, and kept a sharp look-out, being prepared all the time to receive them.
10030-8{_}986{_}They finally abandoned the idea of surprising us.
10030-8{_}987{_}Next morning, bright and early, they gave us one more grand charge, and again we "stood them off."
10030-8{_}988{_}They then rode away half a mile or so, and formed a circle around us.
10030-8{_}989{_}Each man dismounted and sat down, as if to wait and starve us out.
10030-8{_}990{_}They had evidently seen the advance train pass on the morning of the previous day, and believed that we belonged to that outfit and were trying to overtake it; they had no idea that another train was on its way after us.
10030-8{_}991{_}Our hopes of escape from this unpleasant and perilous situation now depended upon the arrival of the rear train, and when we saw that the Indians were going to besiege us instead of renewing their attacks, we felt rather confident of receiving timely assistance.
10030-8{_}992{_}We had expected that the train would be along late in the afternoon of the previous day, and as the morning wore away we were somewhat anxious and uneasy, at its non-arrival.
10030-8{_}993{_}At last, about ten o'clock, we began to hear in the distance the loud and sharp reports of the big bull-whips, which were handled with great dexterity by the teamsters, and cracked like rifle shots.
10030-8{_}994{_}These were as welcome sounds to us as were the notes of the bag-pipes to the beseiged garrison at Lucknow, when the reinforcements were coming up and the pipers were heard playing, "The Campbells are Coming."
10030-8{_}995{_}In a few moments we saw the lead or head wagon coming slowly over the ridge, which had concealed the train from our view, and soon the whole outfit made its appearance.
10030-8{_}996{_}The Indians observed the approaching train, and assembling in a group they held a short consultation.
10030-8{_}997{_}They then charged upon us once more, for the last time, and as they turned and dashed away over the prairie, we sent our farewell shots rattling after them.
10030-8{_}998{_}The teamsters, seeing the Indians and hearing the shots, came rushing forward to our assistance, but by the time they reached us the red-skins had almost disappeared from view.
10030-8{_}999{_}The teamsters eagerly asked us a hundred questions concerning our fight, admired our fort and praised our pluck.
10030-8{_}1000{_}Simpson's remarkable presence of mind in planning the defense was the general topic of conversation among all the men.
10030-8{_}1001{_}When the teams came up we obtained some water and bandages with which to dress Wood's wound, which had become quite inflamed and painful, and we then put him into one of the wagons.
10030-8{_}1002{_}Simpson and myself obtained a remount, bade good-bye to our dead mules which had served us so well, and after collecting the ornaments and other plunder from the dead Indians, we left their bodies and bones to bleach on the prairie.
10030-8{_}1003{_}The train moved on again and we had no other adventures, except several exciting buffalo hunts on the South Platte, near Plum Creek.
10030-8{_}1004{_}We arrived at Fort Leavenworth about the middle of July, 1858, when I immediately visited home.
10030-8{_}1005{_}I found mother in very poor health, as she was suffering from asthma.
10030-8{_}1006{_}My oldest sister, Martha, had, during my absence, been married to John Crane, and was living at Leavenworth.
10030-8{_}1007{_}During the winter at Fort Bridger I had frequently talked with Wild Bill about my family, and as I had become greatly attached to him I asked him to come and make a visit at our house, which he promised to do.
10030-8{_}1008{_}So one day, shortly after our return from Fort Bridger, he accompanied me home from Leavenworth.
10030-8{_}1009{_}My mother and sisters, who had heard so much about him from me, were delighted to see him and he spent several weeks at our place.
10030-8{_}1010{_}They did everything possible to repay him for his kindness to me.
10030-8{_}1011{_}Ever afterwards, when he was at or near Leavenworth, Wild Bill came out to our house to see the family, whether I was at home or not, and he always received a most cordial reception.
10030-8{_}1012{_}His mother and sisters lived in Illinois, and he used to call our house his home, as he did not have one of his own.
10030-8{_}1013{_}I had been home only about a month, after returning from Fort Bridger, when I again started out with another train, going this time as assistant wagon-master under Buck Bomer.
10030-8{_}1014{_}We went safely through to Fort Laramie, which was our destination, and from there we were ordered to take a load of supplies to a new post called Fort Wallach, which was being established at Cheyenne Pass.
10030-8{_}1015{_}We made this trip and got back to Fort Laramie about November 1st.
10030-8{_}1016{_}I then quit the employ of Russell, Majors & Waddell, and joined a party of trappers who were sent out by the post trader, Mr. Ward, to trap on the streams of the Chugwater and Laramie for beaver, otter, and other fur animals, and also to poison wolves for their pelts.
10030-8{_}1017{_}We were out two months, but as the expedition did not prove very profitable, and was rather dangerous on account of the Indians, we abandoned the enterprise and came into Fort Laramie in the latter part of December.
10030-8{_}1018{_}Being anxious to return to the Missouri river, I joined with two others, named Scott and Charley, who were also desirous of going East on a visit, bought three ponies and a pack-mule, and we started out together.
10030-8{_}1019{_}We made rapid progress on our journey, and nothing worthy of note happened until one afternoon, along the banks of the Little Blue River, we spied a band of Indians hunting on the opposite side of the stream, three miles away.
10030-8{_}1020{_}We did not escape their notice, and they gave us a lively chase for two hours, but they could find no good crossing, and as evening came on we finally got away from them.
10030-8{_}1021{_}We traveled until late in the night; when upon discovering a low, deep ravine which we thought would make a comfortable and safe camping-place, we stopped for a rest.
10030-8{_}1022{_}In searching for a good place to make our beds, I found a hole, and I called to my companions that I had found a fine place for a nest.
10030-8{_}1023{_}One of the party was to stand guard while the others slept.
10030-8{_}1024{_}Scott took the first watch, while Charley and I made a bed in the hole.
10030-8{_}1025{_}While clearing out the place we felt something rough, but as it was dark we could not make out what it was.
10030-8{_}1026{_}At any rate we concluded that it was bones or sticks of wood; we thought perhaps it might be the bones of some animal which had fallen in there and died.
10030-8{_}1027{_}These bones, for such they really proved to be, we pushed one side and then we lay down.
10030-8{_}1028{_}But Charley, being an inveterate smoker, could not resist the temptation of indulging in a smoke before going to sleep.
10030-8{_}1029{_}So he sat up and struck a match to light his old pipe.
10030-8{_}1030{_}Our subterranean bed-chamber was thus illuminated for a moment or two; I sprang to my feet in an instant for a ghastly and horrifying sight was revealed to us.
10030-8{_}1031{_}Eight or ten human skeletons lay scattered upon the ground.
10030-8{_}1032{_}The light of the match died out, but we had seen enough to convince us that we were in a large grave, into which, perhaps, some unfortunate emigrants, who had been killed by the Indians, had been thrown; or, perhaps, seeking refuge there, they had been corraled and then killed on the spot.
10030-8{_}1033{_}If such was the case, they had met the fate of thousands of others, whose friends have never heard of them since they left their eastern homes to seek their fortunes in the Far West.
10030-8{_}1034{_}However, we did not care to investigate this mystery any further, but we hustled out of that chamber of death and informed Scott of our discovery.
10030-8{_}1035{_}Most of the plains-men are very superstitious, and we were no exception to the general rule.
10030-8{_}1036{_}We surely thought that this incident was an evil omen, and that we would be killed if we remained there any longer.
10030-8{_}1037{_}[Illustration: CAMPING IN A SEPULCHRE.]
10030-8{_}1038{_}"Let us dig out of here quicker than we can say Jack Robinson," said Scott; and we began to "dig out" at once.
10030-8{_}1039{_}We saddled our animals and hurriedly pushed forward through the darkness, traveling several miles before we again went into camp.
10030-8{_}1040{_}Next morning it was snowing fiercely, but we proceeded as best we could, and that night we succeeded in reaching Oak Grove ranch, which had been built during the summer.
10030-8{_}1041{_}We here obtained comfortable accommodations and plenty to eat and drink--especially the latter.
10030-8{_}1042{_}Scott and Charley were great lovers and consumers of "tanglefoot," and they soon got gloriously drunk, keeping it up for three days, during which time they gambled with the ranchmen, who got away with all their money; but little they cared for that, as they had their spree.
10030-8{_}1043{_}They finally sobered up, and we resumed our journey, urging our jaded animals as much as they could stand, until we struck Marysville, on the Big Blue.
10030-8{_}1044{_}From this place to Leavenworth we secured first-rate accommodations along the road, as the country had become pretty well settled.
10030-8{_}1045{_}It was in February, 1859, that I got home.
10030-8{_}1046{_}As there was now a good school in the neighborhood, taught by Mr. Divinny, my mother wished me to attend it, and I did so for two months and a half--the longest period of schooling that I ever received at any one time in my life.
10030-8{_}1047{_}As soon as the spring came and the grass began growing, I became uneasy and discontented, and again longed for the free and open life of the plains.
10030-8{_}1048{_}The Pike's Peak gold excitement was then at its height, and everybody was rushing to the new gold diggings.
10030-8{_}1049{_}I caught the gold-fever myself, and joined a party bound for the new town of Auraria, on Cherry Creek, afterwards called Denver, in honor of the then governor of Kansas.
10030-8{_}1050{_}On arriving at Auraria we pushed on to the gold streams in the mountains, passing up through Golden Gate, and over Guy Hill, and thence on to Black Hawk.
10030-8{_}1051{_}We prospected for two months, but as none of us knew anything about mining we met with very poor success, and we finally concluded that prospecting for gold was not our forte.
10030-8{_}1052{_}We accordingly abandoned the enterprise and turned our faces eastward once more.
10030-8{_}1053{_}[Illustration: RAFTING ON THE PLATTE.]
10030-8{_}1054{_}When we struck the Platte River, the happy thought of constructing a small raft--which would float us clear to the Missouri and thence down to Leavenworth--entered our heads, and we accordingly carried out the plan.
10030-8{_}1055{_}Upon the completion of the raft we stocked it with provisions, and "set sail" down the stream.
10030-8{_}1056{_}It was a light craft and a jolly crew, and all was smooth sailing for four or five days.
10030-8{_}1057{_}When we got near old Julesburg, we met with a serious mishap.
10030-8{_}1058{_}Our raft ran into an eddy, and quick as lightning went to pieces, throwing us all into the stream, which was so deep that we had to swim ashore.
10030-8{_}1059{_}We lost everything we had, which greatly discouraged us, and we thereupon abandoned the idea of rafting it any farther.
10030-8{_}1060{_}We then walked over to Julesburg, which was only a few miles distant.
10030-8{_}1061{_}This ranch, which became a somewhat famous spot, had been established by "Old Jules," a Frenchman, who was afterwards killed by the notorious Alf.
10030-8{_}1062{_}Slade.
10030-8{_}1063{_}The great pony express, about which so much has been said and written, was at that time just being started.
10030-8{_}1064{_}The line was being stocked with horses and put into good running condition.
10030-8{_}1065{_}At Julesburg I met Mr. George Chrisman, the leading wagon-master of Russell, Majors & Waddell, who had always been a good friend to me.
10030-8{_}1066{_}He had bought out "Old Jules," and was then the owner of Julesburg ranch, and the agent of the pony express line.
10030-8{_}1067{_}He hired me at once as a pony express rider, but as I was so young he thought I would not be able to stand the fierce riding which was required of the messengers.
10030-8{_}1068{_}He knew, however, that I had been raised in the saddle--that I felt more at home there than in any other place--and as he saw that I was confident that I could stand the racket, and could ride as far and endure it as well as some of the older riders, he gave me a short route of forty-five miles, with the stations fifteen miles apart, and three changes of horses.
10030-8{_}1069{_}I was required to make fifteen miles an hour, including the changes of horses.
10030-8{_}1070{_}I was fortunate in getting well-broken animals, and being so light, I easily made my forty-five miles on time on my first trip out, and ever afterwards.
10030-8{_}1071{_}I wrote to mother and told her how well I liked the exciting life of a pony express rider.
10030-8{_}1072{_}She replied, and begged of me to give it up, as it would surely kill me.
10030-8{_}1073{_}She was right about this, as fifteen miles an hour on horseback would, in a short time, shake any man "all to pieces"; and there were but very few, if any, riders who could stand it for any great length of time.
10030-8{_}1074{_}Nevertheless, I stuck to it for two months, and then, upon receiving a letter informing me that my mother was very sick, I gave it up and went back to the old home in Salt Creek Valley.
10030-8{_}1075{_}CHAPTER VII.
10030-8{_}1076{_}ACCIDENTS AND ESCAPES.
10030-8{_}1077{_}My restless, roaming spirit would not allow me to remain at home very long, and in November, after the recovery of my mother, I went up the Republican River and its tributaries on a trapping expedition in company with Dave Harrington.
10030-8{_}1078{_}Our outfit consisted of one wagon and a yoke of oxen for the transportation of provisions, traps, and other necessaries.
10030-8{_}1079{_}We began trapping near Junction City, Kansas, and then proceeded up the Republican River to the mouth of Prairie Dog Creek, where we found plenty of beavers.
10030-8{_}1080{_}Having seen no signs of Indians thus far, we felt comparatively safe.
10030-8{_}1081{_}We were catching a large number of beavers and were prospering finely, when one of our oxen, having become rather poor, slipped and fell upon the ice, dislocating his hip, so that we had to shoot him to end his misery.
10030-8{_}1082{_}This left us without a team; but we cared little for that, however, as we had made up our minds to remain there till spring, when, and it was decided, that one of us should go to the nearest settlement and get a yoke of oxen with which to haul our wagon into some place of safety where we could leave it.
10030-8{_}1083{_}We would probably have pulled through the winter all right had it not been for a very serious accident which befell me just at that time.
10030-8{_}1084{_}Spying a herd of elk, we started in pursuit of them, and creeping up towards them as slyly as possible, while going around the bend of a sharp bluff or bank of the creek I slipped and broke my leg just above the ankle.
10030-8{_}1085{_}Notwithstanding the great pain I was suffering, Harrington could not help laughing when I urged him to shoot me, as he had the ox, and thus end my misery.
10030-8{_}1086{_}He told me to "brace up," and that he would bring me out "all right."
10030-8{_}1087{_}"I am not much of a surgeon," said he, "but I can fix that leg of yours, even if I haven't got a diploma."
10030-8{_}1088{_}He succeeded in getting me back to camp, which was only a few yards from the creek, and then he set the fracture as well as he knew how, and made me as comfortable as was possible under the circumstances.
10030-8{_}1089{_}We then discussed the situation, which to say the least, looked pretty blue.
10030-8{_}1090{_}Knowing that, owing to our mishaps, we could not do anything more that winter, and as I dreaded the idea of lying there on my back with a broken leg for weeks, and perhaps months, I prevailed upon Harrington to go the nearest settlement--about 125 miles distant--to obtain a yoke of cattle, and then come back for me.
10030-8{_}1091{_}This he consented to do; but before leaving he gathered plenty of wood, and as the ground was covered with snow, I would have no difficulty in getting water if I had a fire.
10030-8{_}1092{_}There was plenty of fresh meat and other provisions in the "dug-out," so that I had no fears of starvation.
10030-8{_}1093{_}The "dugout," which we had built immediately after we had determined to remain there all winter, was a very cosy hole in the ground, covered with poles, grass and sod, with a fireplace in one end.
10030-8{_}1094{_}Harrington thought it would take him twenty days or more to make the round trip; but being well provided for--for this length of time--I urged him to go at once.
10030-8{_}1095{_}Bidding me good-bye he started on foot.
10030-8{_}1096{_}After his departure, each day, as it came and went, seemed to grow longer to me as I lay there helpless and alone.
10030-8{_}1097{_}I made a note of each day, so as to know the time when I might expect him back.
10030-8{_}1098{_}[Illustration: SAVED BY CHIEF RAIN-IN-THE-FACE.]
10030-8{_}1099{_}On the twelfth day after Harrington left me, I was awakened from a sound sleep by some one touching me upon the shoulder.
10030-8{_}1100{_}I looked up and was astonished to see an Indian warrior standing at my side.
10030-8{_}1101{_}His face was hideously daubed with paint, which told me more forcibly than words could have done that he was on the war-path.
10030-8{_}1102{_}He spoke to me in broken English and Sioux mixed, and I understood him to ask what I was doing there, and how many there were with me.
10030-8{_}1103{_}By this time the little dug-out was nearly filled with other Indians, who had been peeping in at the door, and I could hear voices of still more outside as well as the stamping of horses.
10030-8{_}1104{_}I began to think that my time had come, as the saying is, when into the cabin stepped an elderly Indian, whom I readily recognized as old Rain-in-the-Face, a Sioux chief from the vicinity of Fort Laramie.
10030-8{_}1105{_}I rose up as well as I could and showed him my broken leg.
10030-8{_}1106{_}I told him where I had seen him, and asked him if he remembered me.
10030-8{_}1107{_}He replied that he knew me well, and that I used to come to his lodge at Fort Laramie to visit him.
10030-8{_}1108{_}I then managed to make him understand that I was there alone and having broken my leg, I had sent my partner off for a team to take me away.
10030-8{_}1109{_}I asked him if his young men intended to kill me, and he answered, that was what they had proposed to do, but he would see what they had to say.
10030-8{_}1110{_}The Indians then talked among themselves for a few minutes, and upon the conclusion of the consultation, old Rain-in-the-Face turned to me and gave me to understand that as I was yet a "papoose," or a very young man, they would not take my life.
10030-8{_}1111{_}But one of his men, who had no fire-arms, wanted my gun and pistol.
10030-8{_}1112{_}I implored old Rain-in-the-Face to be allowed to keep the weapons, or at least one of them, as I needed something with which to keep the wolves away.
10030-8{_}1113{_}He replied that as his young men were out on the war path, he had induced them to spare my life; but he could not prevent them from taking what ever else they wanted.
10030-8{_}1114{_}They unsaddled their horses as if to remain there for some time, and sure enough they stayed the remainder of the day and all night.
10030-8{_}1115{_}They built a fire in the dug-out and cooked a lot of my provisions, helping themselves to everything as if they owned it.
10030-8{_}1116{_}However, they were polite enough to give me some of the food after they had cooked it.
10030-8{_}1117{_}It was a sumptuous feast that they had, and they seemed to relish it as if it was the best lay-out they had had for many a long day.
10030-8{_}1118{_}They took all my sugar and coffee, and left me only some meat and a small quantity of flour, a little salt and some baking powder.
10030-8{_}1119{_}They also robbed me of such cooking utensils as they wished; then bidding me good-bye, early in the morning, they mounted their ponies and rode off to the south, evidently bent on some murdering and thieving expedition.
10030-8{_}1120{_}I was glad enough to see them leave, as my life had undoubtedly hung by a thread during their presence.
10030-8{_}1121{_}I am confident that had it not been for my youth and the timely recognition and interference of old Rain-in-the-Face they would have killed me without any hesitation or ceremony.
10030-8{_}1122{_}The second day after they had gone it began snowing, and for three long and weary days the snow continued to fall thick and fast.
10030-8{_}1123{_}It blocked the door-way and covered the dug-out to the depth of several feet, so that I became a snowbound prisoner.
10030-8{_}1124{_}My wood was mostly under the snow, and it was with great difficulty that I could get enough to start a fire with.
10030-8{_}1125{_}My prospects were gloomy indeed.
10030-8{_}1126{_}I had just faced death at the hands of the Indians, and now I was in danger of losing my life from starvation and cold.
10030-8{_}1127{_}I knew that the heavy snow would surely delay Harrington on his return; and I feared that he might have perished in the storm, or that some other accident might have befallen him.
10030-8{_}1128{_}Perhaps some wandering band of Indians had run across him and killed him.
10030-8{_}1129{_}I was continually thinking of all these possibilities, and I must say that my outlook seemed desperate.
10030-8{_}1130{_}At last the twentieth day arrived--the day on which Harrington was to return--and I counted the hours from morning till night, but the day passed away with no signs of Harrington.
10030-8{_}1131{_}The wolves made the night hideous with their howls; they gathered around the dug-out; ran over the roof; and pawed and scratched as if trying to get in.
10030-8{_}1132{_}Several days and nights thus wore away, the monotony all the time becoming greater, until at last it became almost unendurable.
10030-8{_}1133{_}Some days I would go without any fire at all, and eat raw frozen meat and melt snow in my mouth for water.
10030-8{_}1134{_}I became almost convinced that Harrington had been caught in the storm and had been buried under the snow, or was lost.
10030-8{_}1135{_}Many a time during that dreary period of uncertainty, I made up my mind that if I ever got out of that place alive, I would abandon the plains and the life of a trapper forever.
10030-8{_}1136{_}I had nearly given up all hopes of leaving the dug-out alive.
10030-8{_}1137{_}It was on the twenty-ninth day, while I was lying thus despondently thinking and wondering, that I heard the cheerful sound of Harrington's voice as he came slowly up the creek, yelling, "whoa!
10030-8{_}1138{_}haw!"
10030-8{_}1139{_}to his cattle.
10030-8{_}1140{_}A criminal on the scaffold, with the noose around his neck, the trap about to be sprung, and receiving a pardon just at the last moment, thus giving him a new lease of life, could not have been more grateful than I was at that time.
10030-8{_}1141{_}It was useless for me to try to force the door open, as the snow had completely blockaded it, and I therefore anxiously awaited Harrington's arrival.
10030-8{_}1142{_}"Hello!
10030-8{_}1143{_}Billy!"
10030-8{_}1144{_}he sang out in a loud voice as he came up, he evidently being uncertain as to my being alive.
10030-8{_}1145{_}"All right, Dave," was my reply.
10030-8{_}1146{_}"Well, old boy, you're alive, are you?"
10030-8{_}1147{_}said he.
10030-8{_}1148{_}"Yes; and that's about all.
10030-8{_}1149{_}I've had a tough siege of it since you've been away, and I came pretty nearly passing in my chips.
10030-8{_}1150{_}I began to think you never would get here, as I was afraid you had been snowed under," said I.
10030-8{_}1151{_}He soon cleared away the snow from the entrance, and opening the door he came in.
10030-8{_}1152{_}I don't think there ever was a more welcome visitor than he was.
10030-8{_}1153{_}I remember that I was so glad to see him that I put my arms around his neck and hugged him for five minutes; never shall I forget faithful Dave Harrington.
10030-8{_}1154{_}"Well, Billy, my boy, I hardly expected to see you alive again," said Harrington, as soon as I had given him an opportunity to draw his breath; "I had a terrible trip of it, and I didn't think I ever would get through.
10030-8{_}1155{_}I was caught in the snow-storm, and was laid up for three days.
10030-8{_}1156{_}The cattle wandered away, and I came within an ace of losing them altogether.
10030-8{_}1157{_}When I got started again the snow was so deep that it prevented me from making much headway.
10030-8{_}1158{_}But as I had left you here I was bound to come through, or die in the attempt."
10030-8{_}1159{_}Again I flung my arms around Dave's neck and gave him a hug that would have done honor to a grizzly bear.
10030-8{_}1160{_}My gratitude was thus much more forcibly expressed than it could have been by words.
10030-8{_}1161{_}Harrington understood this, and seemed to appreciate it.
10030-8{_}1162{_}The tears of joy rolled down my cheeks, and it was impossible for me to restrain them.
10030-8{_}1163{_}When my life had been threatened by the Indians I had not felt half so miserable as when I lay in the dug-out thinking I was destined to die a slow death by starvation and cold.
10030-8{_}1164{_}The Indians would have made short work of it, and would have given me little or no time to think of my fate.
10030-8{_}1165{_}I questioned Harrington as to his trip, and learned all the details.
10030-8{_}1166{_}He had passed through hardships which but few men could have endured.
10030-8{_}1167{_}Noble fellow, that he was.
10030-8{_}1168{_}He had risked his own life to save mine.
10030-8{_}1169{_}After he had finished his story, every word of which I had listened to with eager interest, I related to him my own experiences, in which he became no less interested.
10030-8{_}1170{_}He expressed great astonishment that the Indians had not killed me, and he considered it one of the luckiest and most remarkable escapes he had ever heard of.
10030-8{_}1171{_}It amused me, however, to see him get very angry when I told him that they had taken my gun and pistol and had used up our provisions.
10030-8{_}1172{_}"But never mind, Billy," said he, "we can stand it till the snow goes off, which will not be long, and then we will pull our wagon back to the settlements."
10030-8{_}1173{_}A few days afterwards Harrington gathered up our traps, and cleaned the snow out of the wagon.
10030-8{_}1174{_}Covering it with the sheet which we had used in the dug-out, he made a comfortable bed inside, and helped me into it.
10030-8{_}1175{_}We had been quite successful in trapping, having caught three hundred beavers and one hundred otters, the skins of which Harrington loaded on the wagon.
10030-8{_}1176{_}We then pulled out for the settlements, making good headway, as the snow had nearly disappeared, having been blown or melted away, so that we had no difficulty in finding a road.
10030-8{_}1177{_}On the eighth day out we came to a farmer's house, or ranch, on the Republican River, where we stopped and rested for two days, and then went on to the ranch where Harrington had obtained the yoke of cattle.
10030-8{_}1178{_}We gave the owner of the team twenty-five beaver skins, equal to $60, for the use of the cattle, and he let us have them until we reached Junction City, sending his boy with us to bring them back.
10030-8{_}1179{_}At Junction City we sold our wagon and furs and went with a government mule train to Leavenworth--arriving there in March, 1860.
10030-8{_}1180{_}I was just able to get around on crutches when I got into Leavenworth, and it was several months after that before I entirely recovered the use of my leg.
10030-8{_}1181{_}During the winter I had often talked to Harrington about my mother and sisters, and had invited him to go home with me in the spring.
10030-8{_}1182{_}I now renewed the invitation, which he accepted, and accompanied me home.
10030-8{_}1183{_}When I related to mother my adventures and told her how Harrington had saved my life, she thanked him again and again.
10030-8{_}1184{_}I never saw a more grateful woman than she was.
10030-8{_}1185{_}She asked him to always make his home with us, as she never could reward him sufficiently for what he had done for her darling boy, as she called me.
10030-8{_}1186{_}Harrington concluded to remain with us through the summer and farm mother's land.
10030-8{_}1187{_}But alas!
10030-8{_}1188{_}the uncertainty of life.
10030-8{_}1189{_}The coming of death when least expected was strikingly illustrated in his case.
10030-8{_}1190{_}During the latter part of April he went to a nursery for some trees, and while coming home late at night he caught a severe cold and was taken seriously sick, with lung fever.
10030-8{_}1191{_}Mother did everything in her power for him.
10030-8{_}1192{_}She could not have done more had he been her own son, but notwithstanding her motherly care and attention, and the skill of a physician from Leavenworth, he rapidly grew worse.
10030-8{_}1193{_}It seemed hard, indeed, to think that a great strong man like Harrington, who had braved the storms, and endured the other hardships of the plains all winter long, should, during the warm and beautiful days of spring, when surrounded by friends and the comforts of a good home, be fatally stricken down.
10030-8{_}1194{_}But such was his fate.
10030-8{_}1195{_}He died one week from the day on which he was taken sick.
10030-8{_}1196{_}We all mourned his loss as we would that of a loved son or brother, as he was one of the truest, bravest, and best of friends.
10030-8{_}1197{_}Amid sorrow and tears we laid him away to rest in a picturesque spot on Pilot Knob.
10030-8{_}1198{_}His death cast a gloom over our household, and it was a long time before it was entirely dispelled.
10030-8{_}1199{_}I felt very lonely without Harrington, and I soon wished for a change of scene again.
10030-8{_}1200{_}CHAPTER VIII.
10030-8{_}1201{_}ADVENTURES ON THE OVERLAND ROAD.
10030-8{_}1202{_}As the warm days of summer approached I longed for the cool air of the mountains; and to the mountains I determined to go.
10030-8{_}1203{_}After engaging a man to take care of the farm, I proceeded to Leavenworth and there met my old wagon-master and friend, Lewis Simpson, who was fitting out a train at Atchison and loading it with supplies for the Overland Stage Company, of which Mr. Russell, my old employer, was one of the proprietors.
10030-8{_}1204{_}Simpson was going with this train to Fort Laramie and points further west.
10030-8{_}1205{_}"Come along with me, Billy," said he, "I'll give you a good lay-out.
10030-8{_}1206{_}I want you with me."
10030-8{_}1207{_}"I don't know that I would like to go as far west as that again," replied I, "but I do want to ride the pony express once more; there's some life in that."
10030-8{_}1208{_}"Yes, that's so; but it will soon shake the life out of you," said he.
10030-8{_}1209{_}"However, if that's what you've got your mind set on, you had better come to Atchison with me and see Mr. Russell, who I'm pretty certain, will give you a situation."
10030-8{_}1210{_}I replied that I would do that.
10030-8{_}1211{_}I then went home and informed mother of my intention, and as her health was very poor I had great difficulty in obtaining her consent.
10030-8{_}1212{_}I finally convinced her that as I was of no use on the farm, it would be better and more profitable for me to return to the plains.
10030-8{_}1213{_}So after giving her all the money I had earned by trapping, I bade her good-bye and set out for Atchison.
10030-8{_}1214{_}I met Mr. Russell there and asked him for employment as a pony express-rider; he gave me a letter to Mr. Slade, who was then the stage agent for the division extending from Julesburg to Rocky Ridge.
10030-8{_}1215{_}Slade had his headquarters at Horseshoe Station, thirty-six miles west of Fort Laramie and I made the trip thither in company with Simpson and his train.
10030-8{_}1216{_}Almost the very first person I saw after dismounting from my horse was Slade.
10030-8{_}1217{_}I walked up to him and presented Mr. Russell's letter, which he hastily opened and read.
10030-8{_}1218{_}With a sweeping glance of his eye he took my measure from head to foot, and then said:  "My boy, you are too young for a pony express-rider.
10030-8{_}1219{_}It takes men for that business."
10030-8{_}1220{_}"I rode two months last year on Bill Trotter's division, sir, and filled the bill then; and I think I am better able to ride now," said I.
10030-8{_}1221{_}"What!
10030-8{_}1222{_}are you the boy that was riding there, and was called the youngest rider on the road?"
10030-8{_}1223{_}"I am the same boy," I replied, confident that everything was now all right for me.
10030-8{_}1224{_}"I have heard of you before.
10030-8{_}1225{_}You are a year or so older now, and I think you can stand it.
10030-8{_}1226{_}I'll give you a trial anyhow and if you weaken you can come back to Horseshoe Station and tend stock."
10030-8{_}1227{_}That ended our first interview.
10030-8{_}1228{_}The next day he assigned me to duty on the road from Red Buttes on the North Platte, to the Three Crossings of the Sweetwater--a distance of seventy-six miles--and I began riding at once.
10030-8{_}1229{_}It was a long piece of road, but I was equal to the undertaking; and soon afterwards had an opportunity to exhibit my power of endurance as a pony express rider.
10030-8{_}1230{_}One day when I galloped into Three Crossings, my home station, I found that the rider who was expected to take the trip out on my arrival, had got into a drunken row the night before and had been killed; and that there was no one to fill his place.
10030-8{_}1231{_}I did not hesitate for a moment to undertake an extra ride of eighty-five miles to Rocky Ridge, and I arrived at the latter place on time.
10030-8{_}1232{_}I then turned back and rode to Red Buttes, my starting place, accomplishing on the round trip a distance of 322 miles.
10030-8{_}1233{_}[Illustration: I IMMEDIATELY CHANGED HORSES]  Slade heard of this feat of mine, and one day as he was passing on a coach he sang out to me, "My boy, you're a brick, and no mistake.
10030-8{_}1234{_}That was a good run you made when you rode your own and Miller's routes, and I'll see that you get extra pay for it."
10030-8{_}1235{_}Slade, although rough at times and always a dangerous character--having killed many a man--was always kind to me.
10030-8{_}1236{_}During the two years that I worked for him as pony express-rider and stage-driver, he never spoke an angry word to me.
10030-8{_}1237{_}As I was leaving Horse Creek one day, a party of fifteen Indians "jumped me" in a sand ravine about a mile west of the station.
10030-8{_}1238{_}They fired at me repeatedly, but missed their mark.
10030-8{_}1239{_}I was mounted on a roan California horse--the fleetest steed I had.
10030-8{_}1240{_}Putting spurs and whip to him, and lying flat on his back, I kept straight on for Sweetwater Bridge--eleven miles distant--instead of trying to turn back to Horse Creek.
10030-8{_}1241{_}The Indians came on in hot pursuit, but my horse soon got away from them, and ran into the station two miles ahead of them.
10030-8{_}1242{_}The stock-tender had been killed there that morning, and all the stock had been driven off by the Indians, and as I was therefore unable to change horses, I continued on to Ploutz's Station--twelve miles further--thus making twenty-four miles straight run with one horse.
10030-8{_}1243{_}I told the people at Ploutz's what had happened at Sweetwater Bridge, and with a fresh horse went on and finished the trip without any further adventure.
10030-8{_}1244{_}[Illustration: ATTACK ON STAGE COACH.]
10030-8{_}1245{_}About the middle of September the Indians became very troublesome on the line of the stage road along the Sweetwater.
10030-8{_}1246{_}Between Split Rock and Three Crossings they robbed a stage, killed the driver and two passengers, and badly wounded Lieut.
10030-8{_}1247{_}Flowers, the assistant division agent.
10030-8{_}1248{_}The red-skinned thieves also drove off the stock from the different stations, and were continually lying in wait for the passing stages and pony express-riders, so that we had to take many desperate chances in running the gauntlet.
10030-8{_}1249{_}The Indians had now become so bad and had stolen so much stock that it was decided to stop the pony express for at least six weeks, and to run the stages but occasionally during that period; in fact, it would have been almost impossible to have run the enterprise much longer without restocking the line.
10030-8{_}1250{_}While we were thus nearly all lying idle, a party was organized to go out and search for stolen stock.
10030-8{_}1251{_}This party was composed of stage-drivers, express-riders, stock-tenders, and ranchmen--forty of them altogether--and they were well-armed and well-mounted.
10030-8{_}1252{_}They were mostly men who had undergone all kinds of hardships and braved every danger, and they were ready and anxious to "tackle" any number of Indians.
10030-8{_}1253{_}Wild Bill (who had been driving stage on the road and had recently come down to our division) was elected captain of the company.
10030-8{_}1254{_}It was supposed that the stolen stock had been taken to the head of Powder River and vicinity, and the party, of which I was a member, started out for that section in high hopes of success.
10030-8{_}1255{_}Twenty miles out from Sweetwater Bridge, at the head of Horse Creek, we found an Indian trail running north towards Powder River, and we could see by the tracks that most of the horses had been recently shod and were undoubtedly our stolen stage stock.
