10030-8{_}1256{_}Pushing rapidly forward, we followed this trail to Powder River; thence down this stream to within about forty miles of the spot where old Fort Reno now stands.
10030-8{_}1257{_}Here the trail took a more westerly course along the foot of the mountains, leading eventually to Crazy Woman's Fork--a tributary of Powder River.
10030-8{_}1258{_}At this point we discovered that the party whom we were trailing had been joined by another band of Indians, and, judging from the fresh appearance of the trail, the united body could not have left this spot more than twenty-four hours before.
10030-8{_}1259{_}Being aware that we were now in the heart of the hostile country and that we might at any moment find more Indians than we had "lost," we advanced with more caution than usual, and kept a sharp lookout.
10030-8{_}1260{_}As we were approaching Clear Creek, another tributary of Powder river, we discovered Indians on the opposite side of the creek, some three miles distant; at least we saw horses grazing, which was a sure sign that there were Indians there.
10030-8{_}1261{_}The Indians thinking themselves in comparative safety--never before having been followed so far into their own country by white men--had neglected to put out any scouts.
10030-8{_}1262{_}They had no idea that there were any white men in that part of the country.
10030-8{_}1263{_}We got the lay of their camp, and then held a council to consider and mature a plan for capturing it.
10030-8{_}1264{_}We knew full well that the Indians would outnumber us at least three to one, and perhaps more.
10030-8{_}1265{_}Upon the advice and suggestion of Wild Bill, it was finally decided that we should wait until it was nearly dark, and then, after creeping as close to them as possible, make a dash through their camp, open a general fire on them, and stampede the horses.
10030-8{_}1266{_}This plan, at the proper time, was most successfully executed.
10030-8{_}1267{_}The dash upon the enemy was a complete surprise to them.
10030-8{_}1268{_}They were so overcome with astonishment that they did not know what to make of it.
10030-8{_}1269{_}We could not have astonished them any more if we had dropped down into their camp from the clouds.
10030-8{_}1270{_}They did not recover from the surprise of this sudden charge until after we had ridden pell-mell through their camp and got away with our own horses as well as theirs.
10030-8{_}1271{_}We at once circled the horses around towards the south, and after getting them on the south side of Clear Creek, some twenty of our men--just as the darkness was coming on--rode back and gave the Indians a few parting shots.
10030-8{_}1272{_}We then took up our line of march for Sweetwater Bridge, where we arrived four days afterwards with all of our own horses and about one hundred captured Indian ponies.
10030-8{_}1273{_}The expedition had proved a grand success, and the event was celebrated in the usual manner--by a grand spree.
10030-8{_}1274{_}The only store at Sweetwater Bridge did a rushing business for several days.
10030-8{_}1275{_}The returned stock-hunters drank, and gambled and fought.
10030-8{_}1276{_}The Indian ponies, which had been distributed among the captors, passed from hand to hand at almost every deal of the cards.
10030-8{_}1277{_}There seemed to be no limit to the rioting, and carousing; revelry reigned supreme.
10030-8{_}1278{_}On the third day of the orgie, Slade, who had heard the news, came up to the bridge and took a hand in the "fun," as it was called.
10030-8{_}1279{_}To add some variation and excitement to the occasion, Slade got in to a quarrel with a stage-driver and shot him, killing him almost instantly.
10030-8{_}1280{_}[Illustration: ALF.
10030-8{_}1281{_}SLADE KILLING THE DRIVER.]
10030-8{_}1282{_}The "boys" became so elated as well as "elevated" over their success against the Indians, that most of them were in favor of going back and cleaning out the whole Indian race.
10030-8{_}1283{_}One old driver especially, Dan Smith, was eager to open a war on all the hostile nations, and had the drinking been continued another week he certainly would have undertaken the job, single-handed and alone.
10030-8{_}1284{_}The spree finally came to an end; the men sobered down and abandoned the idea of again invading the hostile country.
10030-8{_}1285{_}The recovered horses were replaced on the road, and the stages and pony express were again running on time.
10030-8{_}1286{_}Slade, having taken a great fancy to me, said: "Billy, I want you to come down to my headquarters, and I'll make you a sort of supernumerary rider, and send you out only when it is necessary."
10030-8{_}1287{_}I accepted the offer, and went with him down to Horseshoe, where I had a comparatively easy time of it.
10030-8{_}1288{_}I had always been fond of hunting, and I now had a good opportunity to gratify my ambition in that direction, as I had plenty of spare time on my hands.
10030-8{_}1289{_}In this connection I will relate one of my bear-hunting adventures.
10030-8{_}1290{_}One day, when I had nothing else to do, I saddled up an extra pony express horse, and arming myself with a good rifle and pair of revolvers, struck out for the foot hills of Laramie Peak for a bear-hunt.
10030-8{_}1291{_}Riding carelessly along, and breathing the cool and bracing autumn air which came down from the mountains, I felt as only a man can feel who is roaming over the prairies of the far West, well armed, and mounted on a fleet and gallant steed.
10030-8{_}1292{_}The perfect freedom which he enjoys is in itself a refreshing stimulant to the mind as well as to the body.
10030-8{_}1293{_}Such indeed were my feelings on this beautiful day, as I rode up the valley of the Horseshoe.
10030-8{_}1294{_}Occasionally I scared up a flock of sage-hens or a jack-rabbit.
10030-8{_}1295{_}Antelopes and deer were almost always in sight in any direction, but as they were not the kind of game I was after, on that day, I passed them by, and kept on towards the higher mountains.
10030-8{_}1296{_}The further I rode the rougher and wilder became the country, and I knew that I was approaching the haunts of the bear.
10030-8{_}1297{_}I did not discover any, however, although I saw plenty of tracks in the snow.
10030-8{_}1298{_}About two o'clock in the afternoon, my horse having become tired, and myself being rather weary, I shot a sage-hen, and dismounting, I unsaddled my horse and tied him to a small tree, where he could easily feed on the mountain grass.
10030-8{_}1299{_}I then built a little fire, and broiling the chicken and seasoning it with salt and pepper, which I had obtained from my saddle-bags, I soon sat down to a "genuine square meal," which I greatly relished.
10030-8{_}1300{_}After resting for a couple of hours, I remounted and resumed my upward trip to the mountains, having made up my mind to camp out that night rather than go back without a bear, which my friends knew I had gone out for.
10030-8{_}1301{_}As the days were growing short, night soon came on, and I looked around for a suitable camping place.
10030-8{_}1302{_}While thus engaged, I scared up a flock of sage-hens, two of which I shot, intending to have one for supper and the other for breakfast.
10030-8{_}1303{_}By this time it was becoming quite dark, and I rode down to one of the little mountain streams, where I found an open place in the timber suitable for a camp.
10030-8{_}1304{_}I dismounted, and after unsaddling my horse and hitching him to a tree, I prepared to start a fire.
10030-8{_}1305{_}Just then I was startled by hearing a horse whinnying further up the stream.
10030-8{_}1306{_}It was quite a surprise to me, and I immediately ran to my animal to keep him from answering, as horses usually do in such cases.
10030-8{_}1307{_}I thought that the strange horse might belong to some roaming band of Indians, as I knew of no white men being in that portion of the country at that time.
10030-8{_}1308{_}I was certain that the owner of the strange horse could not be far distant, and I was very anxious to find out who my neighbor was, before letting him know that I was in his vicinity.
10030-8{_}1309{_}I therefore re-saddled my horse, and leaving him tied so that I could easily reach him I took my gun and started out on a scouting expedition up the stream.
10030-8{_}1310{_}I had gone about four hundred yards when, in a bend of the stream, I discovered ten or fifteen horses grazing.
10030-8{_}1311{_}On the opposite side of the creek a light was shining high up the mountain bank.
10030-8{_}1312{_}Approaching the mysterious spot as cautiously as possible, and when within a few yards of the light--which I discovered came from a dug-out in the mountain side--I heard voices, and soon I was able distinguish the words, as they proved to be in my own language.
10030-8{_}1313{_}Then I knew that the occupants of the dug-out, whence the voices proceeded, were white men.
10030-8{_}1314{_}Thinking that they might be a party of trappers, I boldly walked up to the door and knocked for admission.
10030-8{_}1315{_}The voices instantly ceased, and for a moment a deathlike silence reigned inside.
10030-8{_}1316{_}Then there seemed to follow a kind of hurried whispering--a sort of consultation--and then some one called out: "Who's there?"
10030-8{_}1317{_}"A friend and a white man," I replied.
10030-8{_}1318{_}The door opened, and a big, ugly-looking fellow stepped, forth and said:  "Come in."
10030-8{_}1319{_}I accepted the invitation with some degree of fear and hesitation, which I endeavored to conceal, as I saw that it was too late to back out, and that it would never do to weaken at that point, whether they were friends or foes.
10030-8{_}1320{_}Upon entering the dug-out my eyes fell upon eight as rough and villainous looking men as I ever saw in my life.
10030-8{_}1321{_}Two of them I instantly recognized as teamsters who had been driving in Lew Simpson's train, a few months before, and had been discharged.
10030-8{_}1322{_}They were charged with the murdering and robbing of a ranchman; and having stolen his horses it was supposed that they had left the country.
10030-8{_}1323{_}I gave them no signs of recognition however, deeming it advisable to let them remain in ignorance as to who I was.
10030-8{_}1324{_}It was a hard crowd, and I concluded that the sooner I could get away from them the better it would be for me.
10030-8{_}1325{_}I felt confident that they were a band of horse-thieves.
10030-8{_}1326{_}"Where are you going, young man; and who's with you?"
10030-8{_}1327{_}asked one of the men who appeared to be the leader of the gang.
10030-8{_}1328{_}"I am entirely alone.
10030-8{_}1329{_}I left Horseshoe station this morning for a bear hunt, and not finding any bears, I had determined to camp out for the night and wait till morning," said I; "and just as I was going into camp, a few hundred yards down the creek, I heard one of your horses whinnying, and then I came up to your camp."
10030-8{_}1330{_}[Illustration: THE HORSE THIEVES' DEN.]
10030-8{_}1331{_}I was thus explicit in my statement in order, if possible to satisfy the cut-throats that I was not spying upon them, but that my intrusion was entirely accidental.
10030-8{_}1332{_}"Where's your horse?"
10030-8{_}1333{_}demanded the boss thief.
10030-8{_}1334{_}"I left him down the creek," I answered.
10030-8{_}1335{_}They proposed going after the horse, but I thought that that would never do, as it would leave me without any means of escape, and I accordingly said, in hopes to throw them off the track, "Captain, I'll leave my gun here and go down and get my horse, and come back and stay all night."
10030-8{_}1336{_}I said this in as cheerful and as careless a manner as possible, so as not to arouse their suspicions in any way, or lead them to think that I was aware of their true character.
10030-8{_}1337{_}I hated to part with my gun, but my suggestion of leaving it was a part of the plan of escape which I had arranged.
10030-8{_}1338{_}If they have the gun, thought I, they would surely believe that I intended to come back.
10030-8{_}1339{_}But this little game did not work at all, as one of the desperadoes spoke up and said:  "Jim and I will go down with you after your horse, and you can leave your gun here all the same, as you'll not need it."
10030-8{_}1340{_}"All right," I replied, for I could certainly have said nothing else.
10030-8{_}1341{_}It became evident to me that it would be better to trust myself with two men than with the whole party.
10030-8{_}1342{_}It was apparent that from this time on, I would have to be on the alert for some good opportunity to give them the slip.
10030-8{_}1343{_}"Come along," said one of them, and together we went down the creek, and soon came to the spot where my horse was tied.
10030-8{_}1344{_}One of the men unhitched the animal and said: "I'll lead the horse."
10030-8{_}1345{_}"Very well," said I, "I've got a couple of sage-hens here.
10030-8{_}1346{_}Lead on."
10030-8{_}1347{_}I picked up the sage-hens, which I had killed a few hours before, and followed the man who was leading the horse, while his companion brought up the rear.
10030-8{_}1348{_}The nearer we approached the dug-out the more I dreaded the idea of going back among the villainous cut-throats.
10030-8{_}1349{_}My first plan of escape having failed, I now determined upon another.
10030-8{_}1350{_}[Illustration: MY ESCAPE FROM THE HORSE THIEVES.]
10030-8{_}1351{_}I had both of my revolvers with me, the thieves not having thought it necessary to search me.
10030-8{_}1352{_}It was now quite dark, and I purposely dropped one of the sage-hens, and asked the man behind me to pick it up.
10030-8{_}1353{_}While he was hunting for it on the ground, I quickly pulled out one of my Colt's revolvers and struck him a tremendous blow on the back of the head, knocking him senseless to the ground.
10030-8{_}1354{_}I then instantly wheeled around, and saw that the man ahead who was only a few feet distant, had heard the blow and had turned to see what was the matter, his hand upon his revolver.
10030-8{_}1355{_}We faced each other at about the same instant, but before he could fire, as he tried to do, I shot him dead in his tracks.
10030-8{_}1356{_}Then jumping on my horse, I rode down the creek as fast as possible, through the darkness and over the rough ground and rocks.
10030-8{_}1357{_}The other outlaws in the dug-out, having heard the shot which I had fired, knew there was trouble, and they all came rushing down the creek.
10030-8{_}1358{_}I suppose, by the time they reached the man whom I had knocked down, that he had recovered and hurriedly told them of what had happened.
10030-8{_}1359{_}They did not stay with the man whom I had shot, but came on in hot pursuit of me.
10030-8{_}1360{_}They were not mounted, and were making better time down the rough canõn than I was on horseback.
10030-8{_}1361{_}From time to time I heard them gradually gaining on me.
10030-8{_}1362{_}At last they had come so near that I saw that I must abandon my horse.
10030-8{_}1363{_}So I jumped to the ground, and gave him a hard slap with the butt of one of my revolvers, which started him on down the valley, while I scrambled up the mountain side.
10030-8{_}1364{_}I had not ascended more than forty feet when I heard my pursuers coming closer and closer; I quickly hid behind a large pine tree, and in a few moments they all rushed by me, being led on by the rattling footsteps of my horse, which they heard ahead of them.
10030-8{_}1365{_}Soon I heard them firing at random at the horse, as they no doubt supposed I was still seated on his back.
10030-8{_}1366{_}As soon as they had passed me I climbed further up the steep mountain, and knowing that I had given them the slip, and feeling certain that I could keep out of their way, I at once struck out for Horseshoe station, which was twenty-five miles distant.
10030-8{_}1367{_}I had hard traveling at first, but upon reaching lower and better ground, I made good headway, walking all night and getting into the station just before daylight,--foot-sore, weary, and generally played out.
10030-8{_}1368{_}I immediately waked up the men of the station and told them of my adventure.
10030-8{_}1369{_}Slade himself happened to be there, and he at once organized a party to go out and hunt up the horse-thieves.
10030-8{_}1370{_}Shortly after daylight twenty well-armed stage-drivers, stock-tenders and ranchmen were galloping in the direction of the dug-out.
10030-8{_}1371{_}Of course I went along with the party, notwithstanding I was very tired and had had hardly any rest at all.
10030-8{_}1372{_}We had a brisk ride, and arrived in the immediate vicinity of the thieves' rendezvous at about ten o'clock in the morning.
10030-8{_}1373{_}We approached the dug-out cautiously, but upon getting in close proximity to it we could discover no horses in sight.
10030-8{_}1374{_}We could see the door of the dug-out standing wide open, and we then marched up to the place.
10030-8{_}1375{_}No one was inside, and the general appearance of everything indicated that the place had been deserted--that the birds had flown.
10030-8{_}1376{_}Such, indeed, proved to be the case.
10030-8{_}1377{_}We found a new-made grave, where they had evidently buried the man whom I had shot.
10030-8{_}1378{_}We made a thorough search of the whole vicinity, and finally found their trail going southeast in the direction of Denver.
10030-8{_}1379{_}As it would have been useless to follow them, we rode back to the station; and thus ended my eventful bear-hunt.
10030-8{_}1380{_}We had no more trouble for some time from horse-thieves after that.
10030-8{_}1381{_}During the winter of 1860 and the spring of 1861 I remained at Horseshoe, occasionally riding pony express and taking care of stock.
10030-8{_}1382{_}CHAPTER IX.
10030-8{_}1383{_}FAST DRIVING.
10030-8{_}1384{_}It was in the spring of 1861, while I was at Horseshoe, that the eastern-bound coach came in one day loaded down with passengers and baggage, and stopped for dinner; Horseshoe being a regular dinner station as well as a home station.
10030-8{_}1385{_}The passengers consisted of six Englishmen, and they had been continually grumbling about the slow time that was being made by the stages, saying that the farther they got East the slower they went.
10030-8{_}1386{_}"These blarsted 'eathens don't know hanything habout staging, hany-'ow," remarked one of them.
10030-8{_}1387{_}"Blarst me bloody heyes!
10030-8{_}1388{_}they cawn't stage in this country as we do in Hingland, you know," said another.
10030-8{_}1389{_}Their remarks were overheard by Bob Scott, who was to drive the coach from Horseshoe to Fort Laramie, and he determined to give them satisfaction before they got over his route.
10030-8{_}1390{_}Scott was known to be the best reinsman and the most expert driver on the whole line of the road.
10030-8{_}1391{_}He was a very gentlemanly fellow in his general appearance and conduct, but at times he would become a reckless dare-devil, and would take more desperate chances than any other driver.
10030-8{_}1392{_}He delighted in driving wild teams on the darkest nights, over a mountain road, and had thus become the hero of many a thrilling adventure.
10030-8{_}1393{_}It happened on this day he was to drive a team of six pony express horses, which had been only partially broken in as a stage team.
10030-8{_}1394{_}As the stock-tenders were hitching them up, Bob, who was standing by, said, "I'll show them Englishmen that we 'blarsted heathens' do know something about staging in this country."
10030-8{_}1395{_}We all knew from Bob's looks that something was up.
10030-8{_}1396{_}It required several men to hitch up this frisky team, as a man had to hold on to each one of the horses by the bits, while they were stringing them out.
10030-8{_}1397{_}The Englishmen came out from dinner, and were delighted to see the horses prancing and pawing as if anxious to start.
10030-8{_}1398{_}"Ha!
10030-8{_}1399{_}my deah fellah, now we will 'ave a fine ride this hafternoon," said one of them.
10030-8{_}1400{_}"By Jove!
10030-8{_}1401{_}those are the kind of 'orses they hought to 'ave on hall the teams," remarked another.
10030-8{_}1402{_}"Are you the lad who is going to drive to-day?"
10030-8{_}1403{_}asked another of Bob.
10030-8{_}1404{_}"Yes, gentlemen," answered Bob, "I'll show you how we stage it in this country."
10030-8{_}1405{_}Bob mounted the box, gathered the lines, and pulling the horses strongly by the bits, he sang out to the Englishmen, "All aboard!"
10030-8{_}1406{_}Bob's companion on the box was Capt.
10030-8{_}1407{_}Cricket; a little fellow who was the messenger of the coach.
10030-8{_}1408{_}After everybody was seated, Bob told the stock-tenders to "turn 'em loose."
10030-8{_}1409{_}We, who were standing around to see the stage start out, expected it would go off at a lively rate.
10030-8{_}1410{_}We were considerably surprised, therefore, when, after the horses had made a few lively jumps, Bob put on the big California brakes and brought them down to a walk.
10030-8{_}1411{_}The road, for a distance of four miles, gradually rose to the top of a hill, and all the way up this ascent, Bob held the impatient team in check.
10030-8{_}1412{_}"Blarst your heyes, driver, why don't you let them go?"
10030-8{_}1413{_}exclaimed one of the passengers, who had all along been expecting a very brisk ride.
10030-8{_}1414{_}Every once in a while they would ask him some such question, but he paid no attention to them.
10030-8{_}1415{_}At last he reached the top of the hill, and then he suddenly flung three of the lines on the left side of the team, and the other three on the right side.
10030-8{_}1416{_}He then began "playing the silk to them,"--that is to say, he began to lash them unmercifully.
10030-8{_}1417{_}The team started off like a streak of lightning, so to speak, without a single rein being held by the driver.
10030-8{_}1418{_}Bob cried out to the Englishmen, saying, "Hold on, gentlemen, and I'll give you a lively ride, and show you how to stage it in the Rocky Mountains."
10030-8{_}1419{_}[Illustration: BOB SCOTT'S FAMOUS COACH RIDE.]
10030-8{_}1420{_}His next movement was to pull the lamps out of the sockets and throw them at the leaders.
10030-8{_}1421{_}The glass broke upon their backs and nearly set them wild, but being so accustomed to running the road, they never once left the track, and went flying on down the grade towards the next station, eight miles distant, the coach bouncing over the loose stones and small obstacles, and surging from side to side, as an eggshell would in the rapids of Niagara.
10030-8{_}1422{_}Not satisfied with the break-neck rate at which they were traveling, Bob pulled out his revolver and fired in rapid succession, at the same time yelling in a demoniacal manner.
10030-8{_}1423{_}By this time the Englishmen had become thoroughly frightened, as they saw the lines flying wildly in every direction and the team running away.
10030-8{_}1424{_}They did not know whether to jump out or remain in the coach.
10030-8{_}1425{_}Bob would occasionally look down from his seat, and, seeing their frightened faces, would ask, "Well, how do you like staging in this country now?"
10030-8{_}1426{_}The Englishmen stuck to the coach, probably thinking it would be better to do so than to take the chances of breaking their necks by jumping.
10030-8{_}1427{_}As the flying team was nearing the station, the stock tender saw that they were running away and that the driver had no control over them whatever.
10030-8{_}1428{_}Being aware that the pony express horses were accustomed to running right into the stable on arriving at the station, he threw open the large folding doors, which would just allow the passage of the team and coach into the stable.
10030-8{_}1429{_}The horses, sure enough, made for the open doorway.
10030-8{_}1430{_}Capt.
10030-8{_}1431{_}Cricket, the messenger, and Scott got down in the boot of the coach to save themselves from colliding with the top of the stable door.
10030-8{_}1432{_}The coach would probably have passed through into the stable without any serious damage had it not been for the bar or threshold that was stretched across the ground to fasten the doors to.
10030-8{_}1433{_}This bar was a small log, and the front wheels struck it with such force that the coach was thrown up high enough to strike the upper portion of the door frame.
10030-8{_}1434{_}The top of the coach was completely torn off, and one of the passenger's arms was broken.
10030-8{_}1435{_}This was the only serious injury that was done; though it was a matter of surprise to all, that any of the travelers escaped.
10030-8{_}1436{_}The coach was backed out, when the running gear was found to be as good as ever.
10030-8{_}1437{_}The top was soon patched up, a change of team was made, and Bob Scott, mounting the box as if nothing had happened, took the reins in hand, and shouted, "All aboard!"
10030-8{_}1438{_}The Englishmen, however, had had enough of Bob Scott, and not one of the party was willing to risk his life with him again.
10030-8{_}1439{_}They said that he was drunk, or crazy or both, and that they would report him and have him discharged for what he had already done.
10030-8{_}1440{_}Bob waited a few minutes to give them an opportunity to take their seats in the coach, but they told him most emphatically that he could drive on without them, as they intended to wait there for the next stage.
10030-8{_}1441{_}Their traps were taken off, and Bob drove away without a single passenger.
10030-8{_}1442{_}He made his usual time into Fort Laramie, which was the end of his run.
10030-8{_}1443{_}The Englishmen came through on the next day's coach, and proceeded on to Atchison, where they reported Bob to the superintendent of the line, who, however, paid little or no attention to the matter, as Bob remained on the road.
10030-8{_}1444{_}Such is the story of the liveliest and most reckless piece of stage-driving that ever occurred on the Overland stage road.
10030-8{_}1445{_}CHAPTER X.
10030-8{_}1446{_}QUESTIONABLE PROCEEDINGS.
10030-8{_}1447{_}Having been away from home nearly a year, and having occasionally heard of my mother's poor health, I determined to make her a visit; so procuring a pass over the road, I went to Leavenworth, arriving there about June 1st, 1861, going from there home.
10030-8{_}1448{_}The civil war had broken out, and excitement ran high in that part of the country.
10030-8{_}1449{_}My mother, of course, was a strong Union woman, and had such great confidence in the government that she believed the war would not last over six months.
10030-8{_}1450{_}Leavenworth at that time was quite an important outfitting post for the West and Southwest, and the fort there was garrisoned by a large number of troops.
10030-8{_}1451{_}While in the city one day I met several of the old, as well as the young men, who had been members of the Free State party all through the Kansas troubles, and who had, like our family, lost everything at the hands of the Missourians.
10030-8{_}1452{_}They now thought a good opportunity offered to retaliate and get even with their persecutors, as they were all considered to be secessionists.
10030-8{_}1453{_}That they were all secessionists, however, was not true, as all of them did not sympathize with the South.
10030-8{_}1454{_}But the Free State men, myself among them, took it for granted that as Missouri was a slave state the inhabitants must all be secessionists, and therefore our enemies.
10030-8{_}1455{_}A man by the name of Chandler proposed that we organize an independent company for the purpose of invading Missouri and making war on its people on our own responsibility.
10030-8{_}1456{_}He at once went about it in a very quiet way, and succeeded in inducing twenty-five men to join him in the hazardous enterprise.
10030-8{_}1457{_}Having a longing and revengeful desire to retaliate upon the Missourians for the brutal manner in which they had treated and robbed my family, I became a member of Chandler's company.
10030-8{_}1458{_}His plan was that we should leave our homes in parties of not more than two or three together, and meet at a certain point near Westport, Missouri, on a fixed day.
10030-8{_}1459{_}His instructions were carried out to the letter, and we met at the rendezvous at the appointed time.
10030-8{_}1460{_}Chandler had been there some days before us, and, thoroughly disguised, had been looking around the country for the whereabouts of all the best horses.
10030-8{_}1461{_}He directed us to secretly visit certain farms and collect all the horses possible, and bring them together the next night.
10030-8{_}1462{_}This we did, and upon reassembling it was found that nearly every man had two horses.
10030-8{_}1463{_}We immediately struck out for the Kansas line, which we crossed at an Indian ferry on the Kansas river, above Wyandotte, and as soon as we had set foot upon Kansas soil we separated with the understanding; that we were to meet one week from that day at Leavenworth.
10030-8{_}1464{_}[Illustration: NEARLY EVERY MAN HAD TWO HORSES.]
10030-8{_}1465{_}Some of the parties boldly took their confiscated horses into Leavenworth, while others rode them to their homes.
10030-8{_}1466{_}This action may look to the reader like horse-stealing, and some people might not hesitate to call it by that name; but Chandler plausibly maintained that we were only getting back our own, or the equivalent, from the Missourians, and as the government was waging war against the South, it was perfectly square and honest, and we had a good right to do it.
10030-8{_}1467{_}So we didn't let our consciences trouble us very much.
10030-8{_}1468{_}We continued to make similar raids upon the Missourians off and on during the summer, and occasionally we had running fights with them; none of the skirmishes, however, amounting to much.
10030-8{_}1469{_}The government officials hearing of our operations, put detectives upon our track, and several of the party were arrested.
10030-8{_}1470{_}My mother, upon learning that I was engaged in this business, told me it was neither honorable nor right, and she would not for a moment countenance any such proceedings.
10030-8{_}1471{_}Consequently I abandoned the jay-hawking enterprise, for such it really was.
10030-8{_}1472{_}About this time the government bought from Jones and Cartwright several ox-trains, which were sent to Rolla, Missouri, all being put in charge of my old and gallant friend, Wild Bill, who had just become the hero of the day, on account of a terrible fight which he had had with a gang of desperadoes and outlaws, who infested the border under the leadership of the then notorious Jake McCandless.
10030-8{_}1473{_}In this fight he had killed McCandless and three of his men.
10030-8{_}1474{_}The affair occurred while Wild Bill was riding the pony express in western Kansas.
10030-8{_}1475{_}The custom with the express riders, when within half a mile of a station, was either to begin shouting or blowing a horn in order to notify the stock tender of his approach, and to have a fresh horse already saddled for him on his arrival, so that he could go right on without a moment's delay.
10030-8{_}1476{_}One day, as Wild Bill neared Rock Creek station, where he was to change horses, he began shouting as usual at the proper distance; but the stock-tender, who had been married only a short time and had his wife living with him at the station, did not make his accustomed appearance.
10030-8{_}1477{_}Wild Bill galloped up and instead of finding the stock-tender ready for him with a fresh horse, he discovered him lying across the stable door with the blood oozing from a bullet-hole in his head.
10030-8{_}1478{_}The man was dead, and it was evident that he had been killed only a few moments before.
10030-8{_}1479{_}In a second Wild Bill jumped from his horse, and looking in the direction of the house he saw a man coming towards him.
10030-8{_}1480{_}The approaching man fired on him at once, but missed his aim.
10030-8{_}1481{_}Quick as lightning Wild Bill pulled his revolver and returned the fire.
10030-8{_}1482{_}The stranger fell dead, shot through the brain.
10030-8{_}1483{_}"Bill, Bill!
10030-8{_}1484{_}Help!
10030-8{_}1485{_}Help!
10030-8{_}1486{_}save me!"
10030-8{_}1487{_}Such was the cry that Bill now heard.
10030-8{_}1488{_}It was the shrill and pitiful voice of the dead stock-tender's wife, and it came from a window of the house.
10030-8{_}1489{_}She had heard the exchange of shots, and knew that Wild Bill had arrived.
10030-8{_}1490{_}He dashed over the dead body of the villain whom he had killed, and just as he sprang into the door of the house, he saw two powerful men assaulting the woman.
10030-8{_}1491{_}One of the desperadoes was in the act of striking her with the butt end of a revolver, and while his arm was still raised, Bill sent a ball crashing through his skull, killing him instantly.
10030-8{_}1492{_}Two other men now came rushing from an adjoining room, and Bill, seeing that the odds were three to one against him, jumped into a corner, and then firing, he killed another of the villains.
10030-8{_}1493{_}Before he could shoot again the remaining two men closed in upon him, one of whom had drawn a large bowie knife.
10030-8{_}1494{_}Bill wrenched the knife from his grasp and drove it through the heart of the outlaw.
10030-8{_}1495{_}[Illustration: WILD BILL AND THE OUTLAWS.]
10030-8{_}1496{_}The fifth and last man now grabbed Bill by the throat, and held him at arm's length, but it was only for a moment, as Bill raised his own powerful right arm and struck his antagonist's left arm such a terrible blow that he broke it.
10030-8{_}1497{_}The disabled desperado, seeing that he was no longer a match for Bill, jumped through the door, and mounting a horse he succeeded in making his escape--being the sole survivor of the Jake McCandless gang.
10030-8{_}1498{_}Wild Bill remained at the station with the terrified woman until the stage came along, and he then consigned her to the care of the driver.
10030-8{_}1499{_}Mounting his horse he at once galloped off, and soon disappeared in the distance, making up for lost time.
10030-8{_}1500{_}This was the exploit that was on everybody's tongue and in every newspaper.
10030-8{_}1501{_}It was one of the most remarkable and desperate hand to hand encounters that has ever taken place on the border.
10030-8{_}1502{_}I happened to meet Wild Bill at Leavenworth as he was about to depart for Rolla; he wished me to take charge of the government trains as a sort of assistant under him, and I gladly accepted the offer.
10030-8{_}1503{_}Arriving at Rolla, we loaded the trains with freight and took them to Springfield, Missouri.
10030-8{_}1504{_}On our return to Rolla we heard a great deal of talk about the approaching fall races at St. Louis, and Wild Bill having brought a fast running horse from the mountains, determined to take him to that city and match him against some of the high-flyers there; and down to St. Louis we went with this running horse, placing our hopes very high on him.
10030-8{_}1505{_}Wild Bill had no difficulty in making up a race for him.
10030-8{_}1506{_}All the money that he and I had we put up on the mountain runner, and as we thought we had a sure thing, we also bet the horse against $250.
10030-8{_}1507{_}I rode the horse myself, but nevertheless, our sure thing, like many another sure thing, proved a total failure, and we came out of that race minus the horse and every dollar we had in the world.
10030-8{_}1508{_}Before the race it had been "make or break" with us, and we got "broke."
10030-8{_}1509{_}We were "busted" in the largest city we had ever been in, and it is no exaggeration to say that we felt mighty blue.
10030-8{_}1510{_}On the morning after the race we went to the military headquarters, where Bill succeeded in securing an engagement for himself as a government scout, but I being so young failed in obtaining similar employment.
10030-8{_}1511{_}Wild Bill, however, raised some money, by borrowing it from a friend, and then buying me a steamboat ticket he sent me back to Leavenworth, while he went to Springfield, which place he made his headquarters while scouting in southeastern Missouri.
10030-8{_}1512{_}One night, after he had returned from a scouting expedition, he took a hand in a game of poker, and in the course of the game he became involved in a quarrel with Dave Tutt, a professional gambler, about a watch which he had won from Tutt, who would not give it up.
10030-8{_}1513{_}Bill told him he had won it fairly, and that he proposed to have it; furthermore, he declared his intention of carrying the watch across the street next morning to military headquarters, at which place he had to report at nine o'clock.
10030-8{_}1514{_}Tutt replied that he would himself carry the watch across the street at nine o'clock, and no other man would do it.
10030-8{_}1515{_}Bill then said to Tutt that if he attempted anything of the kind, he would kill him.
10030-8{_}1516{_}A challenge to a duel had virtually been given and accepted, and everybody knew that the two men meant business.
10030-8{_}1517{_}At nine o'clock the next morning, Tutt started to cross the street.
10030-8{_}1518{_}Wild Bill, who was standing on the opposite side, told him to stop.
10030-8{_}1519{_}At that moment Tutt, who was carrying his revolver in his hand, fired at Bill but missed him.
10030-8{_}1520{_}Bill quickly pulled out his revolver and returned the fire, hitting Tutt squarely in the forehead and killing him instantly.
10030-8{_}1521{_}Quite a number of Tutt's friends were standing in the vicinity, having assembled to witness the duel, and Bill, as soon as Tutt fell to the ground, turned to them and asked if any one of them wanted to take it up for Tutt; if so, he would accommodate any of them then and there.
10030-8{_}1522{_}But none of them cared to stand in front of Wild Bill to be shot at by him.
10030-8{_}1523{_}Nothing of course was ever done to Bill for the killing of Tutt.
10030-8{_}1524{_}CHAPTER XI.
10030-8{_}1525{_}A SOLDIER.
10030-8{_}1526{_}In the fall of 1861 I made a trip to Fort Larned, Kansas, carrying military dispatches, and in the winter I accompanied George Long through the country, and assisted him in buying horses for the government.
10030-8{_}1527{_}The next spring, 1862, an expedition against the Indians was organized, consisting of a volunteer regiment, the Ninth Kansas, under Colonel Clark.
10030-8{_}1528{_}This expedition, which I had joined in the capacity of guide and scout, proceeded to the Kiowa and Comanche country, on the Arkansas river, along which stream we scouted all summer between Fort Lyon and Fort Larned, on the old Santa Fe trail.
10030-8{_}1529{_}We had several engagements with the Indians, but they were of no great importance.
10030-8{_}1530{_}In the winter of 1862, I became one of the "Red Legged Scouts,"--a company of scouts commanded by Captain Tuff.
10030-8{_}1531{_}Among its members were some of the most noted Kansas Rangers, such as Red Clark, the St. Clair brothers, Jack Harvey, an old pony express-rider named Johnny Fry, and many other well known frontiersmen.
10030-8{_}1532{_}Our field of operations was confined mostly to the Arkansas country and southwestern Missouri.
10030-8{_}1533{_}We had many a lively skirmish with the bushwhackers and Younger brothers, and when we were not hunting them, we were generally employed in carrying dispatches between Forts Dodge, Gibson, Leavenworth, and other posts.
10030-8{_}1534{_}Whenever we were in Leavenworth we had a very festive time.
10030-8{_}1535{_}We usually attended all the balls in full force, and "ran things" to suit ourselves.
10030-8{_}1536{_}Thus I passed the winter of 1862 and the spring of 1863.
10030-8{_}1537{_}Subsequently I engaged to conduct a small train to Denver for some merchants, and on reaching that place in September, I received a letter stating that my mother was not expected to live.
10030-8{_}1538{_}I hastened home, and found her dangerously ill. She grew gradually worse, and at last, on the 22d of November, 1863, she died.
10030-8{_}1539{_}Thus passed away a loving and affectionate mother and a noble, brave, good and loyal woman.
10030-8{_}1540{_}That I loved her above all other persons, no one who has read these reminiscences can for a moment doubt.
10030-8{_}1541{_}Previous to this said event my sister Julia had been married to a gentleman named J.A.
10030-8{_}1542{_}Goodman, and they now came to reside at our house and take charge of the children, as my mother had desired that they should not be separated.
10030-8{_}1543{_}Mr. Goodman became the guardian of the minor children.
10030-8{_}1544{_}I soon left the home now rendered gloomy by the absence of her whom I had so tenderly loved, and going to Leavenworth I entered upon a dissolute and reckless life--to my shame be it said--and associated with gamblers, drunkards, and bad characters generally.
10030-8{_}1545{_}I continued my dissipation about two months, and was becoming a very "hard case."
10030-8{_}1546{_}About this time the Seventh Kansas regiment, known as "Jennison's Jay-hawkers," returned from the war, and re-enlisted and re-organized as veterans.
10030-8{_}1547{_}Among them I met quite a number of my old comrades and neighbors, who tried to induce me to enlist and go south with them.
10030-8{_}1548{_}I had no idea of doing anything of the kind; but one day, after having been under the influence of bad whisky, I awoke to find myself a soldier in the Seventh Kansas.
10030-8{_}1549{_}I did not remember how or when I had enlisted, but I saw I was in for it, and that it would not do for me to endeavor to back out.
10030-8{_}1550{_}In the spring of 1864 the regiment was ordered to Tennessee, and we got into Memphis just about the time that General Sturgis was so badly whipped by General Forrest.
10030-8{_}1551{_}General A. J. Smith re-organized the army to operate against Forrest, and after marching to Tupalo, Mississippi, we had an engagement with him and defeated him.
10030-8{_}1552{_}This kind of fighting was all new to me, being entirely different from any in which I had ever before engaged.
10030-8{_}1553{_}I soon became a non-commissioned officer, and was put on detached service as a scout.
10030-8{_}1554{_}After skirmishing around the country with the rest of the army for some little time, our regiment returned to Memphis, but was immediately ordered to Cape Girardeau, in Missouri, as a confederate force under General Price was then raiding that state.
10030-8{_}1555{_}The command of which my regiment was a part hurried to the front to intercept Price, and our first fight with him occurred at Pilot Knob.
10030-8{_}1556{_}From that time for nearly six weeks we fought or skirmished every day.
10030-8{_}1557{_}I was still acting as a scout, when one day I rode ahead of the command, some considerable distance, to pick up all possible information concerning Price's movements.
10030-8{_}1558{_}I was dressed in gray clothes, or Missouri jeans, and on riding up to a farm-house and entering, I saw a man, also dressed in gray costume, sitting at a table eating bread and milk.
10030-8{_}1559{_}He looked up as I entered, and startled me by saying:  "You little rascal, what are you doing in those 'secesh' clothes?"
10030-8{_}1560{_}Judge of my surprise when I recognized in the stranger my old friend and partner, Wild Bill, disguised as a Confederate officer.
10030-8{_}1561{_}"I ask you the same question, sir," said I without the least hesitation.
10030-8{_}1562{_}"Hush!
10030-8{_}1563{_}sit down and have some bread and milk, and we'll talk it all over afterwards," said he.
10030-8{_}1564{_}I accepted the invitation and partook of the refreshments.
10030-8{_}1565{_}Wild Bill paid the woman of the house, and we went out to the gate where my horse was standing.
10030-8{_}1566{_}"Billy, my boy," said he, "I am mighty glad to see you.
10030-8{_}1567{_}I haven't seen or heard of you since we got busted on that St. Louis' horse-race."
10030-8{_}1568{_}"What are you doing out here?"
10030-8{_}1569{_}I asked.
10030-8{_}1570{_}"I am a scout under General McNiel.
10030-8{_}1571{_}For the last few days I have been with General Marmaduke's division of Price's army, in disguise as a southern officer from Texas, as you see me now," said he.
10030-8{_}1572{_}"That's exactly the kind of business that I am out on to-day," said I; "and I want to get some information concerning Price's movements."
10030-8{_}1573{_}"I'll give you all that I have;" and he then went on and told me all that he knew regarding Price's intentions, and the number and condition of his men.
10030-8{_}1574{_}He then asked about my mother, and when he learned that she was dead he was greatly surprised and grieved; he thought a great deal of her, for she had treated him almost as one of her own children.
10030-8{_}1575{_}He finally took out a package, which he had concealed about his person, and handing it to me he said:  "Here are some letters which I want you to give to General McNiel."
10030-8{_}1576{_}"All right," said I as I took them, "but where will I meet you again?"
10030-8{_}1577{_}"Never mind that," he replied; "I am getting so much valuable information that I propose to stay a little while longer in this disguise."
10030-8{_}1578{_}Thereupon we shook hands and parted.
10030-8{_}1579{_}It is not necessary to say much concerning Price's raid in general, as that event is a matter of recorded history.
10030-8{_}1580{_}I am only relating the incidents in which I was personally interested either as one of the actors or as an observer.
10030-8{_}1581{_}Another interesting and I may say exciting episode happened to me a day or two after my unexpected meeting with Wild Bill.
10030-8{_}1582{_}I was riding with the advance guard of our army, and wishing a drink of water, I stopped at a farmhouse.
10030-8{_}1583{_}There were no men about the premises, and no one excepting a very fine and intellectual looking lady and her two daughters.
10030-8{_}1584{_}They seemed to be almost frightened to death at seeing me--a "yank"--appear before them.
10030-8{_}1585{_}I quieted their fears somewhat, and the mother then asked me how far back the army was.
10030-8{_}1586{_}When I told her it would be along shortly, she expressed her fears that they would take everything on the premises.
10030-8{_}1587{_}They set me out a lunch and treated me rather kindly, so that I really began to sympathize with them; for I knew that the soldiers would ransack their house and confiscate everything they could lay their hands on.
10030-8{_}1588{_}At last I resolved to do what I could to protect them.
10030-8{_}1589{_}After the generals and the staff officers had passed by, I took it upon myself to be a sentry over the house.
10030-8{_}1590{_}When the command came along some of the men rushed up with the intention of entering the place and carrying off all the desirable plunder possible, and then tearing and breaking everything to pieces, as they usually did along the line of march.
10030-8{_}1591{_}"Halt!"
10030-8{_}1592{_}I shouted; "I have been placed here by the commanding officer as a guard over this house, and no man must enter it."
10030-8{_}1593{_}This stopped the first squad; and seeing that my plan was a success, I remained at my post during the passage of the entire command and kept out all intruders.
10030-8{_}1594{_}It seemed as if the ladies could not thank me sufficiently for the protection I had afforded them.
10030-8{_}1595{_}They were perfectly aware of the fact that I had acted without orders and entirely on my own responsibility, and therefore they felt the more grateful.
10030-8{_}1596{_}They urgently invited me to remain a little while longer and partake of an excellent dinner which they said they were preparing for me.
10030-8{_}1597{_}I was pretty hungry about that time, as our rations had been rather slim of late, and a good dinner was a temptation I could not withstand, especially as it was to be served up by such elegant ladies.
10030-8{_}1598{_}While I was eating the meal, I was most agreeably entertained by the young ladies, and before I had finished it the last of the rear-guard must have been at least two miles from the house.
10030-8{_}1599{_}Suddenly three men entered the room, and I looked up and saw three double-barreled shot-guns leveled straight at me.
10030-8{_}1600{_}Before I could speak, however, the mother and her daughters sprang between the men and me.
10030-8{_}1601{_}"Father!
10030-8{_}1602{_}Boys!
10030-8{_}1603{_}Lower your guns!
10030-8{_}1604{_}You must not shoot this man," and similar exclamations, were the cry of all three.
10030-8{_}1605{_}The guns were lowered, and then the men, who were the father and brothers of the young ladies, were informed of what I had done for them.
10030-8{_}1606{_}It appeared that they had been concealed in the woods near by while the army was passing, and on coming into the house and finding a Yankee there, they determined to shoot him.
10030-8{_}1607{_}Upon learning the facts, the old man extended his hand to me, saying:  "I would not harm a hair of your head for the world; but it is best that you stay here no longer, as your command is some distance from here now, and you might be cut off by bushwhackers before reaching it."
10030-8{_}1608{_}Bidding them all good-bye, and with many thanks from the mother and daughters, I mounted my horse and soon overtook the column, happy in the thought that I had done a good deed, and with no regrets that I had saved from pillage and destruction the home and property of a confederate and his family.
10030-8{_}1609{_}Our command kept crowding against Price and his army until they were pushed into the vicinity of Kansas City, where their further advance was checked by United States troops from Kansas; and then was begun their memorable and extraordinary retreat back into Kansas.
10030-8{_}1610{_}While both armies were drawn up in skirmish line near Fort Scott, Kansas, two men on horseback were seen rapidly leaving the Confederate lines, and suddenly they made a dash towards us.
10030-8{_}1611{_}Instantly quick volleys were discharged from the Confederates, who also began a pursuit, and some five hundred shots were fired at the flying men.
10030-8{_}1612{_}It was evident that they were trying to reach our lines, but when within about a quarter of a mile of us, one of them fell from his horse to rise no more.
10030-8{_}1613{_}He had been fatally shot.
10030-8{_}1614{_}His companion galloped on unhurt, and seven companies of our regiment charged out and met him, and checked his pursuers.
10030-8{_}1615{_}The fugitive was dressed in Confederate uniform, and as he rode into our lines I recognized him as Wild Bill, the Union scout.
10030-8{_}1616{_}He immediately sought Generals Pleasanton and McNiel, with whom he held a consultation.
10030-8{_}1617{_}He told them that although Price made a bold showing on the front, by bringing all his men into view, yet he was really a great deal weaker than the appearance of his lines would indicate; and that he was then trying to cross a difficult stream four miles from Fort Scott.
10030-8{_}1618{_}It was late in the afternoon, but General Pleasanton immediately ordered an advance, and we charged in full force upon the rear of Price's army, and drove it before us for two hours.
10030-8{_}1619{_}If Wild Bill could have made his successful dash into our lines earlier in the day, the attack would have been made sooner, and greater results might have been expected.
10030-8{_}1620{_}The Confederates had suspected him of being a spy for two or three days, and had watched him too closely to allow an opportunity to get away from them sooner.
10030-8{_}1621{_}His unfortunate companion who had been shot, was a scout from Springfield, Missouri, whose name I cannot now remember.
10030-8{_}1622{_}From this time on, Wild Bill and myself continued to scout together until Price's army was driven south of the Arkansas River and the pursuit abandoned.
10030-8{_}1623{_}We then returned to Springfield, Missouri, for a rest and for supplies, and Wild Bill and myself spent two weeks there in "having a jolly good time," as some people would express it.
10030-8{_}1624{_}CHAPTER XII.
10030-8{_}1625{_}A WEDDING.
10030-8{_}1626{_}It was during the winter of 1864-65, while I was on detached service at military headquarters, at St. Louis, that I became acquainted with a young lady named Louisa Frederici, whom I greatly admired and in whose charming society I spent many a pleasant hour.
10030-8{_}1627{_}The war closing in 1865, I was discharged, and after a brief visit at Leavenworth I returned to St. Louis, having made up my mind to capture the heart of Miss Frederici, whom I now adored above any other young lady I had ever seen.
10030-8{_}1628{_}Her lovely face, her gentle disposition and her graceful manners won my admiration and love; and I was not slow in declaring my sentiments to her.
10030-8{_}1629{_}The result was that I obtained her consent to marry me in the near future, and when I bade her good-bye I considered myself one of the happiest of men.
10030-8{_}1630{_}Meantime I drove a string of horses from Leavenworth to Fort Kearney, where I met my old friend Bill Trotter, who was then division stage agent.
10030-8{_}1631{_}He employed me at once to drive stage between Kearney and Plum Creek, the road running near the spot where I had my first Indian fight with the McCarthy brothers, and where I killed my first Indian, nearly nine years before.
10030-8{_}1632{_}I drove stage over this route until February, 1866, and while bounding over the cold, dreary road day after day, my thoughts turned continually towards my promised bride, until I at last determined to abandon staging forever, and marry and settle down.
10030-8{_}1633{_}Immediately after coming to this conclusion, I went to St. Louis, where I was most cordially received by my sweetheart; it was arranged between us that our wedding should take place on the 6th day of March, following.
10030-8{_}1634{_}At last the day arrived, and the wedding ceremony was performed at the residence of the bride's parents, in the presence of a large number of invited friends, whose hearty congratulations we received.
10030-8{_}1635{_}I was certainly to be congratulated, for I had become possessed of a lovely and noble woman, and as I gazed upon her as she stood beside me arrayed in her wedding costume, I indeed felt proud of her; and from that time to this I have always thought that I made a most fortunate choice for a life partner.
10030-8{_}1636{_}An hour after the ceremony we--my bride and myself--were on board of a Missouri river steamboat, bound for our new home in Kansas.
10030-8{_}1637{_}My wife's parents had accompanied us to the boat, and had bidden us a fond farewell and a God-speed on our journey.
10030-8{_}1638{_}During the trip up the river several very amusing, yet awkward incidents occurred, some of which I cannot resist relating.
10030-8{_}1639{_}There happened to be on board the boat an excursion party from Lexington, Missouri, and those comprising it seemed to shun me, for some reason which I could not then account for.
10030-8{_}1640{_}They would point at me, and quietly talk among themselves, and eye me very closely.
10030-8{_}1641{_}Their actions seemed very strange to me.
10030-8{_}1642{_}After the boat had proceeded some little distance, I made the acquaintance of several families from Indiana, who were _en route_ to Kansas.
10030-8{_}1643{_}A gentleman, who seemed to be the leader of these colonists, said to me, "The people of this excursion party don't seem to have any great love for you."
10030-8{_}1644{_}"What does it mean?"
10030-8{_}1645{_}I asked; "What are they saying?
10030-8{_}1646{_}It's all a mystery to me."
10030-8{_}1647{_}"They say that you are one of the Kansas jay-hawkers, and one of Jennison's house burners," replied the gentleman.
10030-8{_}1648{_}"I am from Kansas--that's true; and was a soldier and a scout in the Union army," said I; "and I was in Kansas during the border ruffian war of 1856.
10030-8{_}1649{_}Perhaps these people know who I am, and that explains their hard looks."
10030-8{_}1650{_}I had a lengthy conversation with this gentleman--for such he seemed to be--and entertained him with several chapters of the history of the early Kansas troubles, and told him the experiences of my own family.
10030-8{_}1651{_}In the evening the Lexington folks got up a dance, but neither the Indiana people, my wife or myself were invited to join them.
10030-8{_}1652{_}My new-found friend thereupon came to me and said: "Mr. Cody, let us have a dance of our own."
10030-8{_}1653{_}"Very well," was my reply.
10030-8{_}1654{_}"We have some musicians along with us, so we can have plenty of music," remarked the gentleman.
10030-8{_}1655{_}"Good enough!"
10030-8{_}1656{_}said I, "and I will hire the negro barber to play the violin for us.
10030-8{_}1657{_}He is a good fiddler, as I heard him playing only a little while ago."
10030-8{_}1658{_}The result was that we soon organized a good string band and had a splendid dance, keeping it up as long as the Lexington party did theirs.
10030-8{_}1659{_}The second day out from St. Louis, the boat stopped to wood up, at a wild-looking landing.
10030-8{_}1660{_}Suddenly twenty horsemen were seen galloping up through the timber, and as they came nearer the boat they fired on the negro deckhands, against whom they seemed to have a special grudge, and who were engaged in throwing wood on board.
10030-8{_}1661{_}The negroes all quickly jumped on the boat and pulled in the gang plank, and the captain had only just time to get the steamer out into the stream before the bushwhackers--for such they proved to be--appeared on the bank.
10030-8{_}1662{_}"Where is the black abolition jay-hawker?"
10030-8{_}1663{_}shouted the leader.
10030-8{_}1664{_}"Show him to us, and we'll shoot him," yelled another.
10030-8{_}1665{_}But as the boat had got well out in the river by this time, they could not board us, and the captain ordering a full head of steam, pulled out and left them.
10030-8{_}1666{_}I afterwards ascertained that some of the Missourians, who were with the excursion party, were bushwhackers themselves, and had telegraphed to their friends from some previous landing that I was on board, telling them to come to the landing which we had just left, and take me off.
10030-8{_}1667{_}Had the villains captured me they would have undoubtedly put an end to my career, and the public would never have had the pleasure of being bored by this autobiography.
10030-8{_}1668{_}I noticed that my wife felt grieved over the manner in which these people had treated me.
10030-8{_}1669{_}Just married, she was going into a new country, and seeing how her husband was regarded, how he had been shunned, and how his life had been threatened, I was afraid she might come to the conclusion too soon that she had wedded a "hard customer."
10030-8{_}1670{_}So when the boat landed at Kansas City I telegraphed to some of my friends in Leavenworth that I would arrive there in the evening.
10030-8{_}1671{_}My object was to have my acquaintances give me a reception, so that my wife could see that I really did have some friends, and was not so bad a man as the bushwhackers tried to make out.
10030-8{_}1672{_}Just as I expected, when the boat reached Leavenworth, I found a general round-up of friends at the landing to receive us.
10030-8{_}1673{_}There were about sixty gentlemen and ladies.
10030-8{_}1674{_}They had a band of music with them, and we were given a fine serenade.
10030-8{_}1675{_}Taking carriages, we all drove to South Leavenworth to the home of my sister Eliza, who had married George Myers, and there we were given a very handsome reception.
10030-8{_}1676{_}All this cheered up my wife, who concluded that I was not a desperado after all.
10030-8{_}1677{_}Having promised my wife that I would abandon the plains, I rented a hotel in Salt Creek Valley--the same house by the way, which my mother had formerly kept, but which was then owned by Dr. J.J. Crook, late surgeon of the 7th Kansas.
10030-8{_}1678{_}This hotel I called the Golden Rule House, and I kept it until the next September.
10030-8{_}1679{_}People generally said I made a good landlord, and knew how to run a hotel--a business qualification which, it is said, is possessed by comparatively few men.
10030-8{_}1680{_}But it proved too tame employment for me, and again I sighed for the freedom of the plains.
10030-8{_}1681{_}Believing that I could make more money out West on the frontier than I could at Salt Creek Valley, I sold out the Golden Rule House, and started alone for Saline, Kansas, which was then the end of the track of the Kansas Pacific railway, which was at that time being built across the plains.
10030-8{_}1682{_}On my way I stopped at Junction City, where I again met my old friend Wild Bill, who was scouting for the government; his headquarters being at Fort Ellsworth, afterwards called Fort Harker.
10030-8{_}1683{_}He told me that they needed more scouts at this post, and I accordingly accompanied him to that fort, where I had no difficulty in obtaining employment.
10030-8{_}1684{_}During the winter of 1866-67, I scouted between Fort Ellsworth and Fort Fletcher.
10030-8{_}1685{_}In the spring of 1867 I was at Fort Fletcher, when General Custer came out to go on an Indian expedition with General Hancock.
10030-8{_}1686{_}I remained at this post until it was drowned out by the heavy floods of Big Creek, on which it was located; the water rose about the fortifications and rendered the place unfit for occupancy; so the government abandoned the fort, and moved the troops and supplies to a new post--which had been named Fort Hays--located further west, on the south fork of Big Creek.
10030-8{_}1687{_}It was while scouting in the vicinity of Fort Hays that I had my first ride with the dashing and gallant Custer, who had come up to the post from Fort Ellsworth with an escort of only ten men.
10030-8{_}1688{_}He wanted a guide to pilot him to Fort Larned, a distance of sixty-five miles across the country.
10030-8{_}1689{_}I was ordered by the commanding officer to guide General Custer to his desired destination, and I soon received word from the General that he would start out in the morning with the intention of making the trip in one day.
10030-8{_}1690{_}Early in the morning, after a good night's rest, I was on hand, mounted on my large mouse-colored mule--an animal of great endurance--and ready for the journey; when the General saw me, he said:  "Cody, I want to travel fast and go through as quickly as possible, and I don't think that mule of yours is fast enough to suit me."
10030-8{_}1691{_}"General, never mind the mule," said I, "he'll get there as soon as your horses.
10030-8{_}1692{_}That mule is a good one," as I knew that the animal was better than most horses.
10030-8{_}1693{_}"Very well; go ahead, then," said he, though he looked as if he thought I would delay the party on the road.
10030-8{_}1694{_}For the first fifteen miles, until we came to the Smoky Hill River, which we were to cross, I could hardly keep the mule in advance of the General, who rode a frisky, impatient and ambitious thoroughbred steed; in fact, the whole party was finely mounted.
10030-8{_}1695{_}The General repeatedly told me that the mule was "no good," and that I ought to have had a good horse.
10030-8{_}1696{_}But after crossing the river and striking the sand-hills, I began letting my mule out a little, and putting the "persuaders" to him.
10030-8{_}1697{_}He was soon out-traveling the horses, and by the time we had made about half the distance to Fort Larned, I occasionally had to wait for the General or some of his party, as their horses were beginning to show signs of fatigue.
10030-8{_}1698{_}"General, how about this mule, anyhow?"
10030-8{_}1699{_}I asked, at last.
10030-8{_}1700{_}"Cody, you have a better vehicle than I thought you had," was his reply.
10030-8{_}1701{_}From that time on to Fort Larned I had no trouble in keeping ahead of the party.
10030-8{_}1702{_}We rode into the fort at four o'clock in the afternoon with about half the escort only, the rest having lagged far behind.
10030-8{_}1703{_}General Custer thanked me for having brought him straight across the country without any trail, and said that if I were not engaged as post scout at Fort Hays he would like to have me accompany him as one of his scouts during the summer; and he added that whenever I was out of employment, if I would come to him he would find something for me to do.
10030-8{_}1704{_}This was the beginning of my acquaintance with General Custer, whom I always admired as a man and as an officer.
10030-8{_}1705{_}[Illustration: GENERAL CUSTER]  A few days after my return to Fort Hays, the Indians made a raid on the Kansas Pacific Railroad, killing five or six men and running off about one hundred horses and mules.
10030-8{_}1706{_}The news was brought to the commanding officer, who immediately ordered Major Arms, of the Tenth Cavalry--which, by the way, was a negro regiment,--with his company and one mountain howitzer, to go in pursuit of the red-skins, and I was sent along with the expedition as scout and guide.
10030-8{_}1707{_}On the second day out we suddenly discovered, on the opposite side of the Saline River, about a mile distant, a large body of Indians, who were charging down upon us.
10030-8{_}1708{_}Major Arms, placing the cannon on a little knoll, limbered it up and left twenty men to guard it; and then, with the rest of the command, he crossed the river to meet the Indians.
10030-8{_}1709{_}Just as he had got the men over the stream, we heard a terrific yelling and shouting in our rear, and looking back to the knoll where the cannon had been stationed, we saw the negroes, who had been left there to guard the gun, flying towards us, being pursued by about one hundred Indians; while another large party of the latter were dancing around the captured cannon, as if they had got hold of an elephant and did not know what to do with it.
10030-8{_}1710{_}Major Arms turned his command back and drove the Indians from the gun.
10030-8{_}1711{_}The troops then dismounted and took position there.
10030-8{_}1712{_}Quite a severe fight ensued, lasting about two hours.
10030-8{_}1713{_}Five or six of the soldiers, as well as Major Arms, were wounded, and several of the horses were shot.
10030-8{_}1714{_}The Indians seemed to grow thicker and thicker, as if receiving reinforcements from some large party.
10030-8{_}1715{_}The colored troops, who had been bragging all the way that if they could only see some Indians "dey would blow 'em off de farm,"--which was a favorite expression of theirs,--were now singing a different tune.
10030-8{_}1716{_}Every time the Indians would make a charge at us, the darkeys would cry out:  "Heah dey cum;" "Dey must be ten thousand ob 'em;" "De whole country is alive wid 'em;" "Massa Bill, does you tink we is eber agoin' to get out o' heah?"
10030-8{_}1717{_}and many other similar expressions.
10030-8{_}1718{_}Major Arms, who was wounded and lying under the cannon--which, by the way, had become useless,--called me up and asked if I thought there was any show of getting back to the fort.
10030-8{_}1719{_}I replied that there was.
10030-8{_}1720{_}Orders were accordingly given by Major Arms for a retreat, the cannon being left behind.
10030-8{_}1721{_}During the movement several of our men were killed, but as night came and dense darkness prevailed, we succeeded in making good headway, and got into Fort Hays just at daylight next morning, in a very played-out condition.
10030-8{_}1722{_}During our absence the cholera had broken out at the post, and five or six men were dying daily.
10030-8{_}1723{_}It was difficult to tell which was the greater danger--fighting Indians on the prairie, or facing the cholera in camp; but the former was decidedly the more inviting.
10030-8{_}1724{_}CHAPTER XIII.
10030-8{_}1725{_}A MILLIONAIRE.
10030-8{_}1726{_}Soon after returning to Fort Hays, I was sent with dispatches to Fort Harker.
10030-8{_}1727{_}After delivering the messages, I visited the town of Ellsworth, about three miles west of Fort Harker, and there I met a man named William Rose, a contractor on the Kansas Pacific Railroad, who had a contract for grading near Fort Hays.
10030-8{_}1728{_}He had had his stock stolen by the Indians, and had come to Ellsworth to buy more.
10030-8{_}1729{_}During the course of our conversation, Mr. Rose incidentally remarked that he had some idea of laying out a town on the west side of Big Creek, about one mile from the fort, where the railroad was to cross.
10030-8{_}1730{_}He asked my opinion of the contemplated enterprise, and I told him that I thought it was "a big thing."
10030-8{_}1731{_}He then proposed taking me as a partner in the scheme, and suggested that after we got the town laid out and thrown open to the public, we should establish a store and saloon there.
10030-8{_}1732{_}Thinking it would be a grand thing to be half-owner of a town, I at once accepted his proposition.
10030-8{_}1733{_}We bought a stock of such articles as are usually found in a frontier store, and transported them to the place on Big Creek, where we were to found our town.
10030-8{_}1734{_}We hired a railroad engineer to survey the site and stake it off into lots; and we gave the new town the ancient and historical name of Rome.
10030-8{_}1735{_}To a "starter," we donated lots to any one who would build on them, but reserved the corner lots and others which were best located for ourselves.
10030-8{_}1736{_}These reserved lots we valued at fifty dollars each.
10030-8{_}1737{_}Our modern Rome, like all mushroom towns along the line of a new railroad, sprang up as if by magic, and in less than one month we had two hundred frame and log houses, three or four stores, several saloons, and one good hotel.
10030-8{_}1738{_}Rome was looming up, and Rose and I already considered ourselves millionaires, and thought we "had the world by the tail."
10030-8{_}1739{_}But one day a fine-looking gentleman, calling himself Dr. W.E.
10030-8{_}1740{_}Webb, appeared in town, and dropping into our store introduced himself in a very pleasant way.
10030-8{_}1741{_}"Gentlemen, you've got a very flourishing little town here.
10030-8{_}1742{_}Wouldn't you like to have a partner in your enterprise?"
10030-8{_}1743{_}"No, thank you," said I, "we have too good a thing here to whack up with anybody."
10030-8{_}1744{_}My partner agreed with me, but the conversation was continued, and at last the stranger said:  "Gentlemen, I am the agent or prospector of the Kansas Pacific Railroad, and my business is to locate towns for the company along the line."
10030-8{_}1745{_}"We think we have the only suitable town-site in this immediate locality," said Mr. Rose, "and as a town is already started, we have saved the company considerable expense."
10030-8{_}1746{_}"You know as well as I do," said Dr. Webb, "that the company expects to make money by selling lands and town lots; and as you are not disposed to give the company a show, or share with me, I shall probably have to start another town near you.
10030-8{_}1747{_}Competition is the life of trade, you know."
10030-8{_}1748{_}"Start your town, if you want to.
10030-8{_}1749{_}We've got the 'bulge' on you, and can hold it," said I, somewhat provoked at his threat.
10030-8{_}1750{_}But we acted too independently and too indiscreetly for our own good Dr. Webb, the very next day after his interview with us, began hauling material to a spot about one mile east of us, where he staked out a new town, which he called Hays City.
10030-8{_}1751{_}He took great pains to circulate in our town the story that the railroad company would locate their round-houses and machine shops at Hays City, and that it was to be _the_ town and a splendid business center.
10030-8{_}1752{_}A ruinous stampede from our place was the result.
10030-8{_}1753{_}People who had built in Rome came to the conclusion that they had built in the wrong place; they began pulling down their buildings and moving them over to Hays City, and in less than three days our once flourishing city had dwindled down to the little store which Rose and I had built.
10030-8{_}1754{_}It was on a bright summer morning that we sat on a pine box in front of our crib, moodily viewing the demolition of the last building.
10030-8{_}1755{_}Three days before, we had considered ourselves millionaires; on that morning we looked around and saw that we were reduced to the ragged edge of poverty.
10030-8{_}1756{_}Our sanguine expectations of realizing immense fortunes were dashed to the ground, and we felt pretty blue.
10030-8{_}1757{_}The new town of Hays had swallowed Rome entirely.
10030-8{_}1758{_}Mr. Rose facetiously remarked that he felt like "the last rose of summer," with all his lovely companions faded and gone, and _he_ left blooming alone.
10030-8{_}1759{_}I told him I was still there, staunch and true, but he replied that that didn't help the matter much.
10030-8{_}1760{_}Thus ends the brief history of the "Rise, Decline and Fall" of Modern Rome.
10030-8{_}1761{_}It having become evident to me that there was very little hope of Rome ever regaining its former splendor and prosperity, I sent my wife and daughter Arta--who had been born at Leavenworth in the latter part of December, 1866--to St. Louis on a visit.
10030-8{_}1762{_}They had been living with me for some little time in the rear part of our "store."
10030-8{_}1763{_}At this time Mr. Rose and myself had a contract under Schumacher, Miller & Co., constructors of the Kansas Pacific, for grading five miles of track westward from Big Creek, and running through the site of Rome.
10030-8{_}1764{_}Notwithstanding we had been deserted, we had some small hope that they would not be able to get water at the new town, and that the people would all soon move back to Rome, as we really had the best location.
10030-8{_}1765{_}We determined, therefore, to go on with our grading contract, and wait for something better to turn up.
10030-8{_}1766{_}It was indeed hard for us, who had been millionaires, to come down to the level of common railroad contractors-- but we had to do it, all the same.
10030-8{_}1767{_}We visited the new town of Hays almost daily, to see how it was progressing, and in a short time we became much better acquainted with Dr. Webb, who had reduced us from our late independent to our present dependent position.
10030-8{_}1768{_}We found him a perfect gentleman--a whole-souled, genial-hearted fellow, whom everybody liked and respected.
10030-8{_}1769{_}Nearly every day, "Doc."
10030-8{_}1770{_}and I would take a ride over the prairie together and hunt buffalo.
10030-8{_}1771{_}On one occasion, having ventured about ten miles from the town, we spied a band of Indians not over two miles distant, who were endeavoring to get between us and the town, and thus cut us off.
10030-8{_}1772{_}I was mounted on my celebrated horse Brigham, the fleetest steed I ever owned.
10030-8{_}1773{_}On several subsequent occasions he saved my life, and he was the horse that I rode when I killed sixty-nine buffaloes in one day.
10030-8{_}1774{_}Dr. Webb was riding a beautiful thoroughbred bay, which he had brought with him from the East.
10030-8{_}1775{_}Having such splendid horses, we laughed at the idea of a band of Indians overtaking us on a square run, no matter how well they might be mounted; but not caring to be cut off by them, we ran our steeds about three miles towards home, thus getting between the braves and the town.
10030-8{_}1776{_}The Indians were then about three-quarters of a mile distant, and we stopped and waved our hats at them, and fired some shots at long range.
10030-8{_}1777{_}There were thirteen in the party, and as they were getting pretty close to us, we struck out for Hays.
10030-8{_}1778{_}They came on in pursuit and sent several scattering shots after us, but we easily left them behind.
10030-8{_}1779{_}They finally turned and rode off towards the Saline River.
10030-8{_}1780{_}The Doctor thought this glorious sport, and wanted to organize a party to go in pursuit of them, but I induced him to give up this idea, although he did so rather reluctantly.
10030-8{_}1781{_}The Doctor soon became quite an expert hunter, and before he had remained on the prairie a year there were but few men in the country who could kill more buffaloes on a hunt than he.
10030-8{_}1782{_}Being aware that Rose and myself felt rather downhearted over our deserted village, the Doctor one day said that, as he had made the proprietors of Rome "howl," he would give us two lots each in Hays, and did so.
10030-8{_}1783{_}We finally came to the conclusion that our old town was dead beyond redemption or revival, and we thereupon devoted our undivided attention to our railroad contract.
10030-8{_}1784{_}One day we were pushed for horses to work on our scrapers--so I hitched up Brigham, to see how he would work.
10030-8{_}1785{_}He was not much used to that kind of labor, and I was about giving up the idea of making a work-horse of him, when one of the men called to me that there were some buffaloes coming over the hill.
10030-8{_}1786{_}As there had been no buffaloes seen anywhere in the vicinity of the camp for several days, we had become rather short of meat.
10030-8{_}1787{_}I immediately told one of our men to hitch his horses to a wagon and follow me, as I was going out after the herd, and we would bring back some fresh meat for supper.
10030-8{_}1788{_}I had no saddle, as mine had been left at the camp a mile distant, so taking the harness from Brigham, I mounted him bareback and started out after the game, being armed with my celebrated buffalo-killer, "Lucretia Borgia,"--a newly-improved breech-loading needle gun, which I had obtained from the government.
10030-8{_}1789{_}While I was riding toward the buffaloes I observed five horsemen coming out from the fort, who had evidently seen the buffaloes from the post, and were going out for a chase.
10030-8{_}1790{_}They proved to be some newly-arrived officers in that part of the country, and when they came up closer, I could see by the shoulder straps that the senior officer was a captain, while the others were lieutenants.
10030-8{_}1791{_}"Hello!
10030-8{_}1792{_}may friend," sang out the captain, "I see you are after the same game we are."
10030-8{_}1793{_}"Yes, sir; I saw those buffaloes coming over the hill, and as we were about out of fresh meat I thought I would go and get some," said I.
10030-8{_}1794{_}They scanned my cheap-looking outfit pretty closely, and as my horse was not very prepossessing in appearance, having on only a blind bridle, and otherwise looking like a work-horse they evidently considered me a green hand at hunting.
10030-8{_}1795{_}"Do you expect to catch those buffaloes on that Gothic steed?"
10030-8{_}1796{_}laughingly asked the captain.
10030-8{_}1797{_}"I hope so, by pushing on the reins hard enough," was my reply.
10030-8{_}1798{_}"You'll never catch them in the world, my fine fellow," said the captain.
10030-8{_}1799{_}"It requires a fast horse to overtake the animals on these prairies."
10030-8{_}1800{_}"Does it?"
10030-8{_}1801{_}asked I as if I didn't know it.
10030-8{_}1802{_}"Yes; but come along with us as we are going to kill them more for pleasure than anything else.
10030-8{_}1803{_}All we want are the tongues and a piece of tender loin, and you may have all that is left," said the generous man.
