10030-8{_}1804{_}"I am much obliged to you, Captain, and will follow you," I replied.
10030-8{_}1805{_}There were eleven buffaloes in the herd and they were not more than a mile from us.
10030-8{_}1806{_}The officers dashed ahead as if they had a sure thing on killing them all before I could come up with them; but I had noticed that the herd was making towards the creek for water, and as I knew buffalo nature, I was perfectly aware that it would be difficult to turn them from their direct course.
10030-8{_}1807{_}Thereupon, I started towards the creek to head them off, while the officers came up in the rear and gave chase.
10030-8{_}1808{_}The buffaloes came rushing past me not a hundred yards distant, with the officers about three hundred yards in the rear.
10030-8{_}1809{_}Now, thought I, is the time to "get my work in," as they say; and I pulled the blind-bridle from my horse, who knew as well as I did that we were out for buffaloes--as he was a trained hunter.
10030-8{_}1810{_}The moment the bridle was off, he started at the top of his speed, running in ahead of the officers, and with a few jumps he brought me alongside of the rear buffalo.
10030-8{_}1811{_}Raising old "Lucretia Borgia" to my shoulder, I fired, and killed the animal at the first shot.
10030-8{_}1812{_}My horse then carried me alongside the next one, not ten feet away, and I dropped him at the next fire.
10030-8{_}1813{_}As soon as one buffalo would fall, Brigham would take me so close to the next, that I could almost touch it with my gun.
10030-8{_}1814{_}In this manner I killed the eleven buffaloes with twelve shots; and, as the last animal dropped, my horse stopped.
10030-8{_}1815{_}I jumped to the ground, knowing that he would not leave me--it must be remembered that I had been riding him without bridle, reins or saddle--and turning round as the party of astonished officers rode up, I said to them:  "Now, gentlemen, allow me to present to you all the tongues and tender-loins you wish from these buffaloes."
10030-8{_}1816{_}[Illustration: TONGUES AND TENDER LOINS.]
10030-8{_}1817{_}Captain Graham, for such I soon learned was his name, replied: "Well, I never saw the like before.
10030-8{_}1818{_}Who under the sun are you, anyhow?"
10030-8{_}1819{_}"My name is Cody," said I.
10030-8{_}1820{_}One of the lieutenants, Thompson by name, who had met me at Fort Harker, then recognized me, and said: "Why, that is Bill Cody, our old scout."
10030-8{_}1821{_}He then introduced me to the other officers, who were Captain Graham, of the Tenth Cavalry, and Lieutenants Reed, Emmick and Ezekiel.
10030-8{_}1822{_}Captain Graham, who was considerable of a horseman, greatly admired Brigham, and said: "That horse of yours has running points."
10030-8{_}1823{_}"Yes, sir; he has not only got the points, he is a runner and knows how to use the points," said I.
10030-8{_}1824{_}"So I noticed," said the captain.
10030-8{_}1825{_}They all finally dismounted, and we continued chatting for some little time upon the different subjects of horses, buffaloes, Indians and hunting.
10030-8{_}1826{_}They felt a little sore at not getting a single shot at the buffaloes, but the way I had killed them had, they said, amply repaid them for their disappointment.
10030-8{_}1827{_}They had read of such feats in books, but this was the first time they had ever seen anything of the kind with their own eyes.
10030-8{_}1828{_}It was the first time, also, that they had ever witnessed or heard of a white man running buffaloes on horseback without a saddle or a bridle.
10030-8{_}1829{_}I told them that Brigham knew nearly as much about the business as I did, and if I had had twenty bridles they would have been of no use to me, as he understood everything, and all that he expected of me was to do the shooting.
10030-8{_}1830{_}It is a fact, that Brigham would stop if a buffalo did not fall at the first fire, so as to give me a second chance, but if I did not kill the buffalo then, he would go on, as if to say, "You are no good, and I will not fool away time by giving you more than two shots."
10030-8{_}1831{_}Brigham was the best horse I ever owned or saw for buffalo chasing.
10030-8{_}1832{_}Our conversation was interrupted in a little while by the arrival of the wagon which I had ordered out; I loaded the hind-quarters of the youngest buffaloes on it, and then cut out the tongues and tender loins, and presented them to the officers, after which I rode towards the fort with them, while the wagon returned to camp.
10030-8{_}1833{_}Captain Graham told me that he expected to be stationed at Fort Hays during the summer, and would probably be sent out on a scouting expedition, and in case he was he would like to have me accompany him as scout and guide.
10030-8{_}1834{_}I replied that notwithstanding I was very busy with my railroad contract I would go with him if he was ordered out.
10030-8{_}1835{_}I then left the officers and returned to our camp.
10030-8{_}1836{_}That very night the Indians unexpectedly made a raid on the horses, and ran off five or six of our very best work-teams, leaving us in a very crippled condition.
10030-8{_}1837{_}At daylight I jumped on old Brigham and rode to Fort Hays, when I reported the affair to the commanding officer; Captain Graham and Lieutenant Emmick were at once ordered out with their company of one hundred colored troops, to pursue the Indians and recover our stock if possible.
10030-8{_}1838{_}In an hour we were under way.
10030-8{_}1839{_}The darkies had never been in an Indian fight and were anxious to catch the band we were after and "Sweep de red debels from off de face ob de earth."
10030-8{_}1840{_}Captain Graham was a brave, dashing officer, eager to make a record for himself, and it was with difficulty that I could trail fast enough to keep out of the way of the impatient soldiers.
10030-8{_}1841{_}Every few moments Captain Graham would ride up to see if the trail was freshening and how soon we should be likely to overtake the thieves.
10030-8{_}1842{_}At last we reached the Saline river, where we found the Indians had only stopped to feed and water the animals, and had then pushed on towards the Solomon.
10030-8{_}1843{_}After crossing the Saline they made no effort to conceal their trail, thinking they would not be pursued beyond that point--consequently we were able to make excellent time.
10030-8{_}1844{_}We reached the Soloman before sunset, and came to a halt; we surmised that if the Indians were camped on this river, that they had no suspicion of our being in the neighborhood.
10030-8{_}1845{_}I advised Captain Graham to remain with the company where it was, while I went ahead on a scout to find the Indians, if they were in the vicinity.
10030-8{_}1846{_}After riding some distance down the ravine that led to the river, I left my horse at the foot of a hill; then, creeping to the top, I looked cautiously over the summit upon the Solomon, below.
10030-8{_}1847{_}I at once discovered in plain view, not a mile away, a herd of horses grazing, our lost ones among them; very shortly I made out the Indian camp, noted its lay, and how we could best approach it.
10030-8{_}1848{_}Reporting to Captain Graham, whose eyes fairly danced with delight at the prospect of surprising and whipping the redskins, we concluded to wait until the moon rose, then get into the timber so as to approach the Indians as closely as possible without being discovered, and finally to make a sudden dash into their camp, and clean them out.
10030-8{_}1849{_}We had everything "cut and dried," as we thought, but, alas!
10030-8{_}1850{_}just as we were nearing the point where we were to take the open ground and make our charge, one of the colored gentlemen became so excited that he fired off his gun.
10030-8{_}1851{_}We immediately commenced the charge, but the firing of the gun and the noise of our rush through the crackling timber alarmed the Indians, who at once sprang to their horses and were away from us before we reached their late camp.
10030-8{_}1852{_}Captain Graham called out "Follow me boys!"
10030-8{_}1853{_}which we did for awhile, but in the darkness the Indians made good their escape.
10030-8{_}1854{_}The bugle then gave the re-call, but some of the darkies did not get back until morning, having, in their fright, allowed their horses to run away with them whithersoever it suited the animal's pleasure to go.
10030-8{_}1855{_}[Illustration: THE INDIAN HORSE THIEVES.]
10030-8{_}1856{_}We followed the trail the next day for awhile, but as it become evident that it would be a long chase to overtake the enemy, and as we had rations only for the day, we commenced the return.
10030-8{_}1857{_}Captain Graham was bitterly disappointed in not being able to get the fight when it seemed so near at one time.
10030-8{_}1858{_}He roundly cursed the "nigger" who fired the gun, and as a punishment for his carelessness, he was compelled to walk all the way back to Fort Hays.
10030-8{_}1859{_}CHAPTER XIV.
10030-8{_}1860{_}EARNING A TITLE.
10030-8{_}1861{_}It was about this time that the end of the Kansas Pacific track was in the heart of the buffalo country, and the company was employing about twelve hundred men in the construction of the road.
10030-8{_}1862{_}As the Indians were very troublesome, it was difficult to obtain fresh meat for the workmen, and the company therefore concluded to engage the services of hunters to kill buffaloes.
10030-8{_}1863{_}Having heard of my experience and success as a buffalo hunter, Messrs. Goddard Brothers, who had the contract for boarding the employees of the road, met me in Hays City one day and made me a good offer to become their hunter, and I at once entered into a contract with them.
10030-8{_}1864{_}They said that they would require about twelve buffaloes per day; that would be twenty-four hams, as we took only the hind-quarters and hump of each buffalo.
10030-8{_}1865{_}As this was to be dangerous work, on account of the Indians, who were riding all over that section of the country, and as I would be obliged to go from five to ten miles from the road each day to hunt the buffaloes, accompanied by only one man with a light wagon for the transportation of the meat, I of course demanded a large salary.
10030-8{_}1866{_}They could afford to remunerate me well, because the meat would not cost them anything.
10030-8{_}1867{_}They agreed to give me five hundred dollars per month, provided I furnished them all the fresh meat required.
10030-8{_}1868{_}Leaving my partner, Rose, to complete our grading contract, I immediately began my career as a buffalo hunter for the Kansas Pacific Railroad, and it was not long before I acquired considerable notoriety.
10030-8{_}1869{_}It was at this time that the very appropriate name of "Buffalo Bill," was conferred upon me by the road-hands.
10030-8{_}1870{_}It has stuck to me ever since, and I have never been ashamed of it.
10030-8{_}1871{_}During my engagement as hunter for the company--a period of less than eighteen mouths--I killed 4,280 buffaloes; and I had many exciting adventures with the Indians, as well as hair-breadth escapes, some of which are well worth relating.
10030-8{_}1872{_}One day in the spring of 1868, I mounted Brigham and started for Smoky Hill River.
10030-8{_}1873{_}After galloping about twenty miles I reached the top of a small hill overlooking the valley of that beautiful stream.
10030-8{_}1874{_}As I was gazing on the landscape, I suddenly saw a band of about thirty Indians nearly half a mile distant; I knew by the way they jumped on their horses that they had seen me as soon as I came into sight.
10030-8{_}1875{_}The only chance I had for my life was to make a run for it, and I immediately wheeled and started back towards the railroad.
10030-8{_}1876{_}Brigham seemed to understand what was up, and he struck out as if he comprehended that it was to be a run for life.
10030-8{_}1877{_}He crossed a ravine in a few jumps, and on reaching a ridge beyond, I drew rein, looked back and saw the Indians coming for me at full speed and evidently well-mounted.
10030-8{_}1878{_}I would have had little or no fear of being overtaken if Brigham had been fresh; but as he was not, I felt uncertain as to how he would stand a long chase.
10030-8{_}1879{_}[Illustration: BUFFALO BILL.]
10030-8{_}1880{_}My pursuers seemed to be gaining on me a little, and I let Brigham shoot ahead again; when we had run about three miles farther, some eight or nine of the Indians were not over two hundred yards behind, and five or six of these seemed to be shortening the gap at every jump.
10030-8{_}1881{_}Brigham now exerted himself more than ever, and for the next three or four miles he got "right down to business," and did some of the prettiest running I ever saw.
10030-8{_}1882{_}But the Indians were about as well-mounted as I was, and one of their horses in particular--a spotted animal--was gaining on me all the time.
10030-8{_}1883{_}Nearly all the other horses were strung out behind for a distance of two miles, but still chasing after me.
10030-8{_}1884{_}[Illustration: DOWN WENT HIS HORSE.]
10030-8{_}1885{_}The Indian who was riding the spotted horse was armed with a rifle, and would occasionally send a bullet whistling along, sometimes striking the ground ahead of me.
10030-8{_}1886{_}I saw that this fellow must be checked, or a stray bullet from his gun might hit me or my horse; so, suddenly stopping Brigham, and quickly wheeling him around, I raised old "Lucretia" to my shoulder, took deliberate aim at the Indian and his horse, hoping to hit one or the other, and fired.
10030-8{_}1887{_}He was not over eighty yards from me at this time, and at the crack of my rifle down went his horse.
10030-8{_}1888{_}Not waiting to see if he recovered, I turned Brigham, and in a moment we were again fairly flying towards our destination; we had urgent business about that time, and were in a hurry to get there.
10030-8{_}1889{_}The other Indians had gained on us while I was engaged in shooting at their leader, and they sent several shots whizzing past me, but fortunately none of them hit the intended mark.
10030-8{_}1890{_}To return their compliment I occasionally wheeled myself in the saddle and fired back at them, and one of my shots broke the leg of one of their horses, which left its rider _hors(e) de combat_, as the French would say.
10030-8{_}1891{_}Only seven or eight Indians now remained in dangerous proximity to me, and as their horses were beginning to lag somewhat, I checked my faithful old steed a little, to allow him an opportunity to draw an extra breath or two.
10030-8{_}1892{_}I had determined, if it should come to the worst, to drop into a buffalo wallow, where I could stand the Indians off for a while; but I was not compelled to do this, as Brigham carried me through most nobly.
10030-8{_}1893{_}The chase was kept up until we came within three miles of the end of the railroad track, where two companies of soldiers were stationed for the purpose of protecting the workmen from the Indians.
10030-8{_}1894{_}One of the outposts saw the Indians chasing me across the prairie, and gave the alarm.
10030-8{_}1895{_}In a few minutes I saw, greatly to my delight, men coming on foot, and cavalrymen, too, came galloping to our rescue as soon as they could mount their horses.
10030-8{_}1896{_}When the Indians observed this, they turned and ran in the direction from which they had come.
10030-8{_}1897{_}In a very few minutes I was met by some of the infantrymen and trackmen, and jumping to the ground and pulling the blanket and saddle off of Brigham, I told them what he had done for me; they at once took him in charge, led him around, and rubbed him down so vigorously that I thought they would rub him to death.
10030-8{_}1898{_}Captain Nolan, of the Tenth Cavalry, now came up with forty of his men, and upon learning what had happened he determined to pursue the Indians.
10030-8{_}1899{_}He kindly offered me one of the cavalry horses, and after putting my own saddle and bridle on the animal, we started out after the flying Indians, who only a few minutes before had been making it so uncomfortably lively for me.
10030-8{_}1900{_}Our horses were all fresh and of excellent stock, and we soon began shortening the distance between ourselves and the redskins.
10030-8{_}1901{_}Before they had gone five miles we overtook and killed eight of their number.
10030-8{_}1902{_}The others succeeded in making their escape.
10030-8{_}1903{_}On coming up to the place where I had killed the first horse--the spotted one--on my "home run," I found that my bullet had struck him in the forehead and killed him instantly.
10030-8{_}1904{_}He was a noble animal, and ought to have been engaged in better business.
10030-8{_}1905{_}When we got back to camp I found old Brigham grazing quietly and contentedly on the grass.
10030-8{_}1906{_}He looked up at me as if to ask if we had got away with any of those fellows who had chased us.
10030-8{_}1907{_}I believe he read the answer in my eyes.
10030-8{_}1908{_}Another very exciting hunting adventure of mine which deserves a place in these reminiscences occurred near Saline river.
10030-8{_}1909{_}My companion at the time was a man called Scotty, a butcher, who generally accompanied me on these hunting expeditions to cut up the buffaloes and load the meat into a light wagon which he brought to carry it in.
10030-8{_}1910{_}He was a brave little fellow and a most excellent shot.
10030-8{_}1911{_}I had killed some fifteen buffaloes, and we had started for home with a wagon-load of meat.
10030-8{_}1912{_}When within about eight miles of our destination, we suddenly ran on to a party of at least thirty Indians who came riding out of the head of a ravine.
10030-8{_}1913{_}On this occasion I was mounted on a most excellent horse belonging to the railroad company, and could easily have made my escape; but of course I could not leave Scotty who was driving a pair of mules hitched to the wagon.
10030-8{_}1914{_}To think was to act, in those days; and as Scotty and I had often talked over a plan of defense in case we were ever surprised by Indians, we instantly proceeded to carry it out.
10030-8{_}1915{_}We jumped to the ground, unhitched the mules quicker than it had ever been done before, and tied them and my horse to the wagon.
10030-8{_}1916{_}We threw the buffalo hams upon the ground, and piled them around the wheels in such a shape as to form a breastwork.
10030-8{_}1917{_}All this was done in a shorter time than it takes to tell it; and then, with our extra box of ammunition and three or four extra revolvers, which we always carried along with us, we crept under the wagon and were fully prepared to give our visitors the warmest kind of a reception.
10030-8{_}1918{_}The Indians came on pell-mell, but when they were within one hundred yards of us we opened such a sudden and galling fire upon them, that they held up and began to circle around the wagon instead of riding up to take tea with us.
10030-8{_}1919{_}They however charged back and forth upon us several times, and their shots killed the two mules and my horse; but we gave it to them right and left, and had the satisfaction of seeing three of them fall to the ground not more than fifty yards away.
10030-8{_}1920{_}On seeing how well we were fortified and protected by our breastwork of hams, they probably came to the conclusion that it would be a difficult undertaking to dislodge us, for they drew off and gave us a rest, but only a short one.
10030-8{_}1921{_}This was the kind of fighting we had been expecting for a long time, as we knew that sooner or later we would be "jumped" by Indians while we were out buffalo hunting.
10030-8{_}1922{_}I had an understanding with the officers who commanded the troops at the end of the track, that in case their pickets should at any time notice a smoke in the direction of our hunting ground, they were to give the alarm, so that assistance might be sent to us for the smoke was to indicate that we were in danger.
10030-8{_}1923{_}I now resolved to signal to the troops in the manner agreed on, and at the first opportunity set fire to the grass on the windward side of the wagon.
10030-8{_}1924{_}The fire spread over the prairie at a rapid rate, causing a dense smoke which I knew would be seen at the camp.
10030-8{_}1925{_}The Indians did not seem to understand this strategic movement.
10030-8{_}1926{_}They got off from their horses, and from behind a bank or knoll, again peppered away at us; but we were well fortified, and whenever they showed their heads we let them know that we could shoot as well as they.
10030-8{_}1927{_}[Illustration: THE FIRE SIGNAL.]
10030-8{_}1928{_}After we had been cooped up in our little fort, for about an hour, we discovered cavalry coming toward us at full gallop over the prairie.
10030-8{_}1929{_}Our signal of distress had proved a success.
10030-8{_}1930{_}The Indians saw the soldiers at about the same time that we did, and thinking that it would not be healthy for them to remain much longer in that vicinity, they mounted their horses and disappeared down the cañons of the creek.
10030-8{_}1931{_}When the soldiers came up we had the satisfaction of showing them five "_good_" Indians, that is dead ones.
10030-8{_}1932{_}Two hours later we pulled into camp with our load of meat, which was found to be all right, except that it had a few bullets and arrows sticking in it.
10030-8{_}1933{_}While I was hunting for the Kansas Pacific railway, I had the pleasure, in the fall of 1867, of meeting the celebrated Kit Carson, one of, if not the oldest and most noted scout, guide, and hunter that our western country has ever produced.
10030-8{_}1934{_}He was on his way to Washington.
10030-8{_}1935{_}I also met him on his return from the East, and invited him to be my guest for a few days at Hays City, which invitation he accepted.
10030-8{_}1936{_}He then proceeded to Fort Lyon, Colorado, near which place his son-in-law, Mr. Boggs, and family, resided.
10030-8{_}1937{_}At this time his health was failing, and shortly afterwards he died at Mr. Boggs' residence on the Picket Wire Creek.
10030-8{_}1938{_}[Illustration: KIT CARSON]     CHAPTER XV.
10030-8{_}1939{_}CHAMPION BUFFALO KILLER.
10030-8{_}1940{_}Shortly after the adventures mentioned in the preceding chapter, I had my celebrated buffalo hunt with Billy Comstock, a noted scout, guide and interpreter, who was then chief of scouts at Fort Wallace, Kansas.
10030-8{_}1941{_}Comstock had the reputation, for a long time, of being a most successful buffalo hunter, and the officers in particular, who had seen him kill buffaloes, were very desirous of backing him in a match against me.
10030-8{_}1942{_}It was accordingly arranged that I should shoot him a buffalo-killing match, and the preliminaries were easily and satisfactorily agreed upon.
10030-8{_}1943{_}We were to hunt one day of eight hours, beginning at eight o'clock in the morning, and closing at four o'clock in the afternoon.
10030-8{_}1944{_}The wager was five hundred dollars a side, and the man who should kill the greater number of buffaloes from on horseback was to be declared the winner.
10030-8{_}1945{_}The hunt took place about twenty miles east of Sheridan, and as it had been pretty well advertised and noised abroad, a large crowd witnessed the interesting and exciting scene.
10030-8{_}1946{_}An excursion party, mostly from St. Louis, consisting of about a hundred gentlemen and ladies, came out on a special train to view the sport, and among the number was my wife, with little baby Arta, who had come to remain with me for a while.
10030-8{_}1947{_}The buffaloes were quite plenty, and it was agreed that we should go into the same herd at the same time and "make a run," as we called it, each one killing as many as possible.
10030-8{_}1948{_}A referee was to follow each of us on horseback when we entered the herd, and count the buffaloes killed by each man.
10030-8{_}1949{_}The St. Louis excursionists, as well as the other spectators, rode out to the vicinity of the hunting grounds in wagons and on horseback, keeping well out of sight of the buffaloes, so as not to frighten them, until the time came for us to dash into the herd; when they were to come up as near as they pleased and witness the chase.
10030-8{_}1950{_}We were fortunate in the first run in getting good ground.
10030-8{_}1951{_}Comstock was mounted on one of his favorite horses, while I rode old Brigham.
10030-8{_}1952{_}I felt confident that I had the advantage of Comstock in two things--first, I had the best buffalo horse that ever made a track; and second, I was using what was known at that time as the needle-gun, a breech-loading Springfield rifle--calibre 50,--it was my favorite old "Lucretia," which has already been introduced to the notice of the reader; while Comstock was armed with a Henry rifle, and although he could fire a few shots quicker than I could, yet I was pretty certain that it did not carry powder and lead enough to do execution equal to my calibre 50.
10030-8{_}1953{_}At last the time came to begin the match.
10030-8{_}1954{_}Comstock and I dashed into a herd, followed by the referees.
10030-8{_}1955{_}The buffaloes separated; Comstock took the left bunch and I the right.
10030-8{_}1956{_}My great _forte_ in killing buffaloes from horseback was to get them circling by riding my horse at the head of the herd, shooting the leaders, thus crowding their followers to the left, till they would finally circle round and round.
10030-8{_}1957{_}On this morning the buffaloes were very accommodating, and I soon had them running in a beautiful circle, when I dropped them thick and fast, until I had killed thirty-eight; which finished my run.
10030-8{_}1958{_}Comstock began shooting at the rear of the herd, which he was chasing, and they kept straight on.
10030-8{_}1959{_}He succeeded, however, in killing twenty-three, but they were scattered over a distance of three miles, while mine lay close together.
10030-8{_}1960{_}I had "nursed" my buffaloes, as a billiard-player does the balls when he makes a big run.
10030-8{_}1961{_}After the result of the first run had been duly announced, our St. Louis excursion friends--who had approached to the place where we had stopped--set out a lot of champagne, which they had brought with them, and which proved a good drink on a Kansas prairie, and a buffalo hunter was a good man to get away with it.
10030-8{_}1962{_}While taking a short rest, we suddenly spied another herd of buffaloes coming toward us.
10030-8{_}1963{_}It was only a small drove, and we at once prepared to give the animals a lively reception.
10030-8{_}1964{_}They proved to be a herd of cows and calves--which, by the way, are quicker in their movements than the bulls.
10030-8{_}1965{_}We charged in among them, and I concluded my run with a score of eighteen, while Comstock killed fourteen.
10030-8{_}1966{_}The score now stood fifty-six to thirty-seven, in my favor.
10030-8{_}1967{_}Again the excursion party approached, and once more the champagne was tapped.
10030-8{_}1968{_}After we had eaten a lunch which was spread for us, we resumed the hunt.
10030-8{_}1969{_}Striking out for a distance of three miles, we came up close to another herd.
10030-8{_}1970{_}As I was so far ahead of my competitor in the number killed, I thought I could afford to give an extra exhibition of my skill.
10030-8{_}1971{_}I had told the ladies that I would, on the next run, ride my horse without saddle or bridle.
10030-8{_}1972{_}This had raised the excitement to fever heat among the excursionists, and I remember one fair lady who endeavored to prevail upon me not to attempt it.
10030-8{_}1973{_}"That's nothing at all," said I; "I have done it many a time, and old Brigham knows as well as I what I am doing, and sometimes a great deal better."
10030-8{_}1974{_}So, leaving my saddle and bridle with the wagons, we rode to the windward of the buffaloes, as usual, and when within a few hundred yards of them we dashed into the herd.
10030-8{_}1975{_}I soon had thirteen laid out on the ground, the last one of which I had driven down close to the wagons, where the ladies were.
10030-8{_}1976{_}It frightened some of the tender creatures to see the buffalo coming at full speed directly toward them; but when he had got within fifty yards of one of the wagons, I shot him dead in his tracks.
10030-8{_}1977{_}This made my sixty-ninth buffalo, and finished my third and last run, Comstock having killed forty-six.
10030-8{_}1978{_}As it was now late in the afternoon, Comstock and his backers gave up the idea that he could beat me, and thereupon the referees declared me the winner of the match, as well as the champion buffalo-hunter of the plains.
10030-8{_}1979{_}[A]  [Footnote A: Poor Billy Comstock was afterwards treacherously murdered by the Indians.
10030-8{_}1980{_}He and Sharpe Grover visited a village of Indians, supposed to be peaceably inclined, near Big Spring Station, in Western Kansas; and after spending several hours with the redskins in friendly conversation, they prepared to depart, having declined an invitation to pass the night there.
10030-8{_}1981{_}It appears that Comstock's beautiful white-handled revolver had attracted the attention of the Indians, who overtook him and his companion when they had gone about half a mile.
10030-8{_}1982{_}After surrounding the two men they suddenly attacked them.
10030-8{_}1983{_}They killed, scalped and robbed Comstock; but Grover, although severely wounded, made his escape, owing to the fleetness of the excellent horse which he was riding.
10030-8{_}1984{_}This sad event occurred August 27, 1868.]
10030-8{_}1985{_}On our way back to camp, we took with us some of the choice meat and finest heads.
10030-8{_}1986{_}In this connection it will not be out of place to state that during the time I was hunting for the Kansas Pacific, I always brought into camp the best buffalo heads, and turned them over to the company, who found a very good use for them.
10030-8{_}1987{_}They had them mounted in the best possible manner, and sent them to all the principal cities and railroad centers in the country, having them placed in prominent positions at the leading hotels, dépôts, and other public buildings, as a sort of trade-mark, or advertisement, of the Kansas Pacific Railroad; and to-day they attract the attention of the traveler almost everywhere.
10030-8{_}1988{_}Whenever I am traveling over the country and see one of these trade-marks, I feel pretty certain that I was the cause of the death of the old fellow whose body it once ornamented, and many a wild and exciting hunt is thus called to mind.
10030-8{_}1989{_}The end of the track finally reached Sheridan, in the month of May, 1868, and as the road was not to be built any farther just then, my services as a hunter were not any longer required.
10030-8{_}1990{_}At this time there was a general Indian war raging all along the western borders.
10030-8{_}1991{_}General Sheridan had taken up his headquarters at Fort Hayes, in order to be in the field to superintend the campaign in person.
10030-8{_}1992{_}As scouts and guides were in great demand, I concluded once more to take up my old avocation of scouting and guiding for the army.
10030-8{_}1993{_}Having no suitable place in which to leave my old and faithful buffalo-hunter Brigham, and not wishing to kill him by scouting, I determined to dispose of him.
10030-8{_}1994{_}I was very reluctant to part with him, but I consoled myself with the thought that he would not be likely to receive harder usage in other hands than he had in mine.
10030-8{_}1995{_}I had several good offers to sell him; but at the suggestion of some gentlemen in Sheridan, all of whom were anxious to obtain possession of the horse, I put him up at a raffle, in order to give them all an equal chance of becoming the owner of the famous steed.
10030-8{_}1996{_}There were ten chances at thirty dollars each, and they were all quickly taken.
10030-8{_}1997{_}Old Brigham was won by a gentleman--Mr. Ike Bonham,--who took him to Wyandotte, Kansas, where he soon added new laurels to his already brilliant record.
10030-8{_}1998{_}Although I am getting ahead of my story, I must now follow Brigham for a while.
10030-8{_}1999{_}A grand tournament came off four miles from Wyandotte, and Brigham took part in it.
10030-8{_}2000{_}As has already been stated, his appearance was not very prepossessing, and nobody suspected him of being anything but the most ordinary kind of a plug.
10030-8{_}2001{_}The friends of the rider laughed at him for being mounted on such a dizzy-looking steed.
10030-8{_}2002{_}When the exercises--which were of a very tame character, being more for style than speed--were over, and just as the crowd were about to return to the city, a purse of $250 was made up, to be given to the horse that could first reach Wyandotte, four miles distant.
10030-8{_}2003{_}The arrangement was carried out, and Brigham was entered as one of the contestants for the purse.
10030-8{_}2004{_}Everybody laughed at Mr. Bonham when it became known that he was to ride that poky-looking plug against the five thoroughbreds which were to take part in the race.
10030-8{_}2005{_}When all the preliminaries had been arranged, the signal was given, and off went the horses for Wyandotte.
10030-8{_}2006{_}For the first half-mile several of the horses led Brigham, but on the second mile he began passing them one after the other, and on the third mile he was in advance of them all, and was showing them all the road at a lively rate.
10030-8{_}2007{_}On the fourth mile his rider let him out, and arrived at the hotel--the home-station--in Wyandotte a long way ahead of his fastest competitor.
10030-8{_}2008{_}Everybody was surprised, as well as disgusted, that such a homely "critter" should be the winner.
10030-8{_}2009{_}Brigham, of course, had already acquired a wide reputation, and his name and exploits had often appeared in the newspapers, and when it was learned that this "critter" was none other than the identical buffalo-hunting Brigham, nearly the whole crowd admitted that they had heard of him before, and had they known him in the first place they certainly would have ruled him out.
10030-8{_}2010{_}I finally lost track of Brigham, and for several years I did not know what had become of him.
10030-8{_}2011{_}Three years ago, while I was at Memphis, Tennessee, I met a Mr. Wilcox, who had been one of the superintendents of construction of the Kansas Pacific Railroad, and he informed me that he owned Brigham, and that he was at that time on his farm, only a few miles out of town.
10030-8{_}2012{_}The next day I rode out with Mr. Wilcox and took a look at the gallant old horse.
10030-8{_}2013{_}He was comfortably cared for in Mr. Wilcox's stable, and looked the same clever pony that he always was.
10030-8{_}2014{_}It seemed as if he almost remembered me, and I put my arms around his neck, as though he had been a long-lost child.
10030-8{_}2015{_}Mr. Wilcox bought the horse at Wyandotte, from the gentleman who had won him at the raffle, and he intends to keep him as long as he lives.
10030-8{_}2016{_}I am grateful that he is in such good hands, and whenever I again visit Memphis I shall surely go and see Brigham if he is still alive.
10030-8{_}2017{_}But to return to the thread of my narrative, from which I have wandered.
10030-8{_}2018{_}Having received the appointment of guide and scout, and having been ordered to report at Fort Larned, then commanded by Captain Dangerfield Parker, I saw it was necessary to take my family--who had remained with me at Sheridan, after the buffalo-hunting match--to Leavenworth, and there leave them.
10030-8{_}2019{_}This I did at once, and after providing them with a comfortable little home, I returned and reported for duty at Fort Larned.
10030-8{_}2020{_}CHAPTER XVI.
10030-8{_}2021{_}A COURIER.
10030-8{_}2022{_}The scouts at Fort Larned, when I arrived there, were commanded by Dick Curtis--an old guide, frontiersman and Indian interpreter.
10030-8{_}2023{_}There were some three hundred lodges of Kiowa and Comanche Indians camped near the fort.
10030-8{_}2024{_}These Indians had not as yet gone upon the war-path, but were restless and discontented, and their leading chiefs, Satanta, Lone Wolf, Kicking Bird, Satank, Sittamore, and other noted warriors, were rather saucy.
10030-8{_}2025{_}The post at the time was garrisoned by only two companies of infantry and one of cavalry.
10030-8{_}2026{_}General Hazen, who was at the post, was endeavoring to pacify the Indians and keep them from going on the war-path.
10030-8{_}2027{_}I was appointed as his special scout, and one morning he notified me that he was going to Fort Harker, and wished me to accompany him as far as Fort Zarah, thirty miles distant.
10030-8{_}2028{_}The General usually traveled in an ambulance, but this trip he was to make in a six-mule wagon, under the escort of a squad of twenty infantrymen.
10030-8{_}2029{_}So, early one morning in August, we started; arriving safely at Fort Zarah at twelve o'clock.
10030-8{_}2030{_}General Hazen thought it unnecessary that we should go farther, and he proceeded on his way to Fort Harker without an escort, leaving instructions that we should return to Fort Larned the next day.
10030-8{_}2031{_}After the General had gone I went to the sergeant in command of the squad, and told him that I was going back that very afternoon, instead of waiting till the next morning; and I accordingly saddled up my mule and set out for Fort Larned.
10030-8{_}2032{_}I proceeded uninterruptedly until I got about half-way between the two posts, when at Pawnee Rock I was suddenly "jumped" by about forty Indians, who came dashing up to me, extending their hands and saying, "How!
10030-8{_}2033{_}How!"
10030-8{_}2034{_}They were some of the same Indians who had been hanging around Fort Larned in the morning.
10030-8{_}2035{_}I saw that they had on their war-paint, and were evidently now out on the war-path.
10030-8{_}2036{_}[Illustration: A BIG JOKE.]
10030-8{_}2037{_}My first impulse was to shake hands with them, as they seemed so desirous of it.
10030-8{_}2038{_}I accordingly reached out my hand to one of them, who grasped it with a tight grip, and jerked me violently forward; another pulled my mule by the bridle, and in a moment I was completely surrounded.
10030-8{_}2039{_}Before I could do anything at all, they had seized my revolvers from the holsters, and I received a blow on the head from a tomahawk which nearly rendered me senseless.
10030-8{_}2040{_}My gun, which was lying across the saddle, was snatched from its place, and finally the Indian, who had hold of the bridle, started off towards the Arkansas River, leading the mule, which was being lashed by the other Indians who were following.
10030-8{_}2041{_}The savages were all singing, yelling and whooping, as only Indians can do, when they are having their little game all their own way.
10030-8{_}2042{_}While looking towards the river I saw, on the opposite side, an immense village moving down along the bank, and then I became convinced that the Indians had left the post and were now starting out on the war-path.
10030-8{_}2043{_}My captors crossed the stream with me, and as we waded through the shallow water they continued to lash the mule and myself.
10030-8{_}2044{_}Finally they brought me before an important looking body of Indians, who proved to be the chiefs and principal warriors.
10030-8{_}2045{_}I soon recognized old Satanta among them, as well as others whom I knew, and I supposed it was all over with me.
10030-8{_}2046{_}The Indians were jabbering away so rapidly among themselves that I could not understand what they were saying.
10030-8{_}2047{_}Satanta at last asked me where I had been; and, as good luck would have it, a happy thought struck me.
10030-8{_}2048{_}I told him I had been after a herd of cattle or "whoa-haws," as they called them.
10030-8{_}2049{_}It so happened that the Indians had been out of meat for several weeks, as the large herd of cattle which had been promised them had not yet arrived, although expected by them.
10030-8{_}2050{_}The moment that I mentioned that I had been searching for the "whoa-haws," old Satanta began questioning me in a very eager manner.
10030-8{_}2051{_}He asked me where the cattle were, and I replied that they were back only a few miles, and that I had been sent by General Hazen to inform him that the cattle were coming, and that they were intended for his people.
10030-8{_}2052{_}This seemed to please the old rascal, who also wanted to know if there were any soldiers with the herd, and my reply was that there were.
10030-8{_}2053{_}Thereupon the chiefs held a consultation, and presently Satanta asked me if General Hazen had really said that they should have the cattle.
10030-8{_}2054{_}I replied in the affirmative, and added that I had been directed to bring the cattle to them.
10030-8{_}2055{_}I followed this up with a very dignified inquiry, asking why his young men had treated me so.
10030-8{_}2056{_}The old wretch intimated that it was only "a freak of the boys"; that the young men had wanted to see if I was brave; in fact, they had only meant to test my bravery, and that the whole thing was a joke.
10030-8{_}2057{_}The veteran liar was now beating me at my own game of lying; but I was very glad of it, as it was in my favor.
10030-8{_}2058{_}I did not let him suspect that I doubted his veracity, but I remarked that it was a rough way to treat friends.
10030-8{_}2059{_}He immediately ordered his young men to give me back my arms, and scolded them for what they had done.
10030-8{_}2060{_}Of course, the sly old dog was now playing it very fine, as he was anxious to get possession of the cattle, with which he believed "there was a heap of soldiers coming."
10030-8{_}2061{_}He had concluded it was not best to fight the soldiers if he could get the cattle peaceably.
10030-8{_}2062{_}Another council was held by the chiefs, and in a few minutes old Satanta came and asked me if I would go over and bring the cattle down to the opposite side of the river, so that they could get them.
10030-8{_}2063{_}I replied, "Of course; that's my instruction from General Hazen."
10030-8{_}2064{_}Satanta said I must not feel angry at his young men, for they had only been acting in fun.
10030-8{_}2065{_}He then inquired if I wished any of his men to accompany me to the cattle herd.
10030-8{_}2066{_}I replied that it would be better for me to go alone, and then the soldiers could keep right on to Fort Larned, while I could drive the herd down on the bottom.
10030-8{_}2067{_}So, wheeling my mule around, I was soon re-crossing the river, leaving old Satanta in the firm belief that I had told him a straight story, and was going for the cattle, which only existed in my imagination.
10030-8{_}2068{_}I hardly knew what to do, but thought that if I could get the river between the Indians and myself I would have a good three-quarters of a mile the start of them, and could then make a run for Fort Larned, as my mule was a good one.
10030-8{_}2069{_}Thus far my cattle story had panned out all right; but just as I reached the opposite bank of the river, I looked behind and saw that ten or fifteen Indians who had begun to suspect something crooked, were following me.
10030-8{_}2070{_}The moment that my mule secured a good foothold on the bank, I urged him into a gentle lope towards the place where, according to my statement, the cattle were to be brought.
10030-8{_}2071{_}Upon reaching a little ridge, and riding down the other side out of view, I turned my mule and headed him westward for Fort Larned.
10030-8{_}2072{_}I let him out for all that he was worth, and when I came out on a little rise of ground, I looked back, and saw the Indian village in plain sight.
10030-8{_}2073{_}My pursuers were now on the ridge which I had passed over, and were looking for me in every direction.
10030-8{_}2074{_}Presently they spied me, and seeing that I was running away, they struck out in swift pursuit, and in a few minutes it became painfully evident that they were gaining on me.
10030-8{_}2075{_}They kept up the chase as far as Ash Creek, six miles from Fort Larned.
10030-8{_}2076{_}I still led them half a mile, as their horses had not gained much during the last half of the race.
10030-8{_}2077{_}My mule seemed to have gotten his second wind, and as I was on the old road I had played the whip and spurs on him without much cessation.
10030-8{_}2078{_}The Indians likewise had urged their steeds to the utmost.
10030-8{_}2079{_}Finally, upon reaching the dividing ridge between Ash Greek and Pawnee Fork, I saw Fort Larned only four miles away.
10030-8{_}2080{_}It was now sundown, and I heard the evening gun at the fort.
10030-8{_}2081{_}The troops of the garrison little dreamed that there was a man flying for his life from the Indians and trying to reach the post.
10030-8{_}2082{_}The Indians were once more gaining on me, and when I crossed the Pawnee Fork, two miles from the post, two or three of them were only a quarter of a mile behind me.
10030-8{_}2083{_}Just as I had gained the opposite bank of the stream I was overjoyed to see some soldiers in a government wagon, only a short distance off.
10030-8{_}2084{_}I yelled at the top of my voice, and riding up to them, told them that the Indians were after me.
10030-8{_}2085{_}[Illustration: AMBUSHING THE INDIANS.]
10030-8{_}2086{_}Denver Jim, a well-known scout, asked how many there were, and upon my informing him that there were about a dozen, he said: "Let's drive the wagon into the trees, and we'll lay for 'em."
10030-8{_}2087{_}The team was hurriedly driven in among the trees and low box-elder bushes, and there secreted.
10030-8{_}2088{_}We did not have to wait long for the Indians, who came dashing up, lashing their horses, which were panting and blowing.
10030-8{_}2089{_}We let two of them pass by, but we opened a lively fire on the next three or four, killing two at the first crack.
10030-8{_}2090{_}The others following, discovered that they had run into an ambush, and whirling off into the brush they turned and ran back in the direction whence they had come.
10030-8{_}2091{_}The two who had passed heard the firing and made their escape.
10030-8{_}2092{_}We scalped the two that we had killed, and appropriated their arms and equipments; and then catching their horses, we made our way into the post.
10030-8{_}2093{_}The soldiers had heard us firing, and as we were approaching the fort the drums were being beaten, and the buglers were sounding the call to fall in.
10030-8{_}2094{_}The officers had thought that Satanta and his Indians were coming in to capture the fort.
10030-8{_}2095{_}It seems that on the morning of that day, two hours after General Hazen had taken his departure, old Satanta drove into the post in an ambulance, which he had received some months before as a present from the government.
10030-8{_}2096{_}He appeared to be angry and bent on mischief.
10030-8{_}2097{_}In an interview with Captain Parker, the commanding officer, he asked why General Hazen had left the post without supplying the beef cattle which had been promised to him.
10030-8{_}2098{_}The Captain told him that the cattle were surely on the road, but he could not explain why they were detained.
10030-8{_}2099{_}The interview proved to be a stormy one, and Satanta made numerous threats, saying that if he wished, he could capture the whole post with his warriors.
10030-8{_}2100{_}Captain Parker, who was a brave man, gave Satanta to understand that he was reckoning beyond his powers, and would find it a more difficult undertaking than he had any idea of, as they were prepared for him at any moment.
10030-8{_}2101{_}The interview finally terminated, and Satanta angrily left the officers presence.
10030-8{_}2102{_}Going over to the sutler's store he sold his ambulance to Mr. Tappan the past trader, and with a portion of the proceeds he secretly managed to secure some whisky from some bad men around the fort.
10030-8{_}2103{_}There are always to be found around every frontier post some men who will sell whisky to the Indians at any time and under any circumstances, notwithstanding it is a flagrant violation of both civil and military regulations.
10030-8{_}2104{_}Satanta mounted his horse, and taking the whisky with him, he rode rapidly away and proceeded straight to his village.
10030-8{_}2105{_}He had not been gone over an hour, when he returned to the vicinity of the post accompanied by his warriors who came in from every direction, to the number of seven or eight hundred.
10030-8{_}2106{_}It was evident that the irate old rascal was "on his ear," so to speak, and it looked as if he intended to carry out his threat of capturing the fort.
10030-8{_}2107{_}The garrison at once turned out and prepared to receive the red-skins, who, when within half a mile, circled around the fort and fired numerous shots into it, instead of trying to take it by assault.
10030-8{_}2108{_}While this circular movement was going on, it was observed that the Indian village in the distance was packing up, preparatory to leaving, and it was soon under way.
10030-8{_}2109{_}The mounted warriors remained behind some little time, to give their families an opportunity to get away, as they feared that the troops might possibly in some manner intercept them.
10030-8{_}2110{_}Finally, they encircled the post several times, fired some farewell rounds, and then galloped away over the prairie to overtake their fast departing village.
10030-8{_}2111{_}On their way thither, they surprised and killed a party of wood-choppers down on the Pawnee Fork, as well as some herders who were guarding beef cattle; some seven or eight men in all, were killed, and it was evident that the Indians meant business.
10030-8{_}2112{_}The soldiers with the wagon--whom I had met at the crossing of the Pawnee Fork--had been out for the bodies of the men.
10030-8{_}2113{_}Under the circumstances it was no wonder that the garrison, upon hearing the reports of our guns when we fired upon the party whom we ambushed, should have thought the Indians were coming back to give them another "turn."
10030-8{_}2114{_}We found that all was excitement at the post; double guards had been put on duty, and Captain Parker had all the scouts at his headquarters.
10030-8{_}2115{_}He was endeavoring to get some one to take some important dispatches to General Sheridan at Fort Hays.
10030-8{_}2116{_}I reported to him at once, and stated where I had met the Indians and how I had escaped from them.
10030-8{_}2117{_}"You was very fortunate, Cody, in thinking of that cattle story; but for that little game your hair would now be an ornament to a Kiowa's lodge," said he.
10030-8{_}2118{_}Just then Dick Curtis spoke up and said: "Cody, the Captain is anxious to send some dispatches to General Sheridan, at Fort Hays, and none of the scouts here seem to be very willing to undertake the trip.
10030-8{_}2119{_}They say they are not well enough acquainted with the country to find the way at night."
10030-8{_}2120{_}As a storm was coming up it was quite dark, and the scouts feared that they would lose the way; besides it was a dangerous ride, as a large party of Indians were known to be camped on Walnut Creek, on the direct road to Fort Hays.
10030-8{_}2121{_}It was evident that Curtis was trying to induce me to volunteer.
10030-8{_}2122{_}I made some evasive answer to Curtis, for I did not care to volunteer after my long day's ride.
10030-8{_}2123{_}But Curtis did not let the matter drop.
10030-8{_}2124{_}Said he:  "I wish, Bill, that you were not so tired by your chase of to-day, for you know the country better than the rest of the boys, and I am certain that you could go through."
10030-8{_}2125{_}"As far as the ride to Fort Hays is concerned, that alone would matter but little to me," I said, "but it is a risky piece of work just now, as the country is full of hostile Indians; still if no other scout is willing to volunteer, I will chance it.
10030-8{_}2126{_}I'll go, provided I am furnished with a good horse.
10030-8{_}2127{_}I am tired of being chased on a government mule by Indians."
10030-8{_}2128{_}At this Captain Nolan, who had been listening to our conversation, said:  "Bill, you may have the best horse in my company.
10030-8{_}2129{_}You can take your choice if you will carry these dispatches.
10030-8{_}2130{_}Although it is against regulations to dismount an enlisted man, I have no hesitancy in such a case of urgent necessity as this is, in telling you that you may have any horse you may wish."
10030-8{_}2131{_}"Captain, your first sergeant has a splendid horse, and that's the one I want.
10030-8{_}2132{_}If he'll let me ride that horse, I'll be ready to start in one hour, storm or no storm," said I.
10030-8{_}2133{_}"Good enough, Bill; you shall have the horse; but are you sure you can find your way on such a dark night as this?"
10030-8{_}2134{_}"I have hunted on nearly every acre of ground between here and Fort Hays, and I can almost keep my route by the bones of the dead buffaloes."
10030-8{_}2135{_}I confidently replied.
10030-8{_}2136{_}"Never fear, Captain, about Cody not finding the way; he is as good in the dark as he is in the daylight," said Curtis.
10030-8{_}2137{_}An orderly was sent for the horse, and the animal was soon brought up, although the sergeant "kicked" a little against letting him go.
10030-8{_}2138{_}After eating a lunch and filling a canteen with brandy, I went to headquarters and put my own saddle and bridle on the horse I was to ride.
10030-8{_}2139{_}I then got the dispatches, and by ten o'clock was on the road to Fort Hays, which was sixty-five miles distant across the country.
10030-8{_}2140{_}The scouts had all bidden me a hearty good-bye, and wished me success, not knowing when, if ever, they would again gaze upon "my warlike form," as the poet would say.
10030-8{_}2141{_}It was dark as pitch, but this I rather liked, as there was little probability of any of the red-skins seeing me unless I stumbled upon them accidentally.
10030-8{_}2142{_}My greatest danger was that my horse might run into a hole and fall down, and in this way get away from me.
10030-8{_}2143{_}To avoid any such accident, I tied one end of my rawhide lariat to the bridle and the other end to my belt.
10030-8{_}2144{_}I didn't propose to be left on foot, alone out on the prairie.
10030-8{_}2145{_}[Illustration: WHOA THERE!]
10030-8{_}2146{_}It was, indeed, a wise precaution that I had taken, for within the next three miles the horse, sure enough, stepped into a prairie-dog's hole, and down he went, throwing me clear over his head.
10030-8{_}2147{_}Springing to his feet, before I could catch hold of the bridle, he galloped away into the darkness; but when he reached the full length of the lariat, he found that he was picketed to Bison William.
10030-8{_}2148{_}I brought him up standing, and after finding my gun, which had dropped to the ground, I went up to him and in a moment was in the saddle again, and went on my way rejoicing keeping straight on my course until I came to the ravines leading into Walnut Creek, twenty-five miles from Fort Larned, where the country became rougher, requiring me to travel slower and more carefully, as I feared the horse might fall over the bank, it being difficult to see anything five feet ahead.
10030-8{_}2149{_}As a good horse is not very apt to jump over a bank, if left to guide himself, I let mine pick his own way.
10030-8{_}2150{_}I was now proceeding as quietly as possible, for I was in the vicinity of a band of Indians who had recently camped in that locality.
10030-8{_}2151{_}I thought that I had passed somewhat above the spot, having made a little circuit to the west with that intention; but as bad luck would have it this time, when I came up near the creek I suddenly rode in among a herd of horses.
10030-8{_}2152{_}The animals became frightened and ran off in every direction.
10030-8{_}2153{_}I knew at once that I was among Indian horses, and had walked into the wrong pew; so without waiting to apologize, I backed out as quickly as possible.
10030-8{_}2154{_}At this moment a dog, not fifty yards away, set up a howl, and then I heard some Indians engaged in conversation;--they were guarding the horses, and had been sleeping.
10030-8{_}2155{_}Hearing my horse's retreating footsteps toward the hills, and thus becoming aware that there had been an enemy in their camp, they mounted their steeds and started for me.
10030-8{_}2156{_}I urged my horse to his full speed, taking the chances of his falling into holes, and guided him up the creek bottom.
10030-8{_}2157{_}The Indians followed me as fast as they could by the noise I made, but I soon distanced them; and then crossed the creek.
10030-8{_}2158{_}When I had traveled several miles in a straight course, as I supposed, I took out my compass and by the light of a match saw that I was bearing two points to the east of north.
10030-8{_}2159{_}At once changing my course to the direct route, I pushed rapidly on through the darkness towards Smoky Hill River.
10030-8{_}2160{_}At about three o'clock in the morning I began traveling more cautiously, as I was afraid of running into another band of Indians.
10030-8{_}2161{_}Occasionally I scared up a herd of buffaloes or antelopes, or coyotes, or deer, which would frighten my horse for a moment, but with the exception of these slight alarms I got along all right.
10030-8{_}2162{_}After crossing Smoky Hill River, I felt comparatively safe as this was the last stream I had to cross.
10030-8{_}2163{_}Riding on to the northward I struck the old Santa Fe trail, ten miles from Fort Hays, just at break of day.
10030-8{_}2164{_}My horse did not seem much fatigued, and being anxious to make good time and get as near the post as possible before it was fairly daylight as there might be bands of Indians camped along Big Creek, I urged him forward as fast as he could go.
10030-8{_}2165{_}As I had not "lost" any Indians, I was not now anxious to make their acquaintance, and shortly after _reveille_ rode into the post.
10030-8{_}2166{_}I proceeded directly to General Sheridan's headquarters, and, was met at the door, by Colonel Moore, _aid-de-camp_ on General Sheridan's staff who asked me on what business I had come.
10030-8{_}2167{_}"I have dispatches for General Sheridan, and my instructions from Captain Parker, commanding Fort Larned, are that they shall be delivered to the General as soon as possible," said I.
10030-8{_}2168{_}Colonel Moore invited me into one of the offices, and said he would hand the dispatches to t h e General as soon as he got up.
10030-8{_}2169{_}[Illustration: DELIVERING DISPATCHES TO SHERIDAN.]
10030-8{_}2170{_}"I prefer to give these dispatches to General Sheridan myself, and at once," was my reply.
10030-8{_}2171{_}The General, who was sleeping in the same building, hearing our voices, called out, "Send the man in with the dispatches."
10030-8{_}2172{_}I was ushered into the General's presence, and as we had met before he recognized me and said:  "Hello, Cody, is that you?"
10030-8{_}2173{_}"Yes, sir; I have some dispatches here for you, from Captain Parker," said I, as I handed the package over to him.
10030-8{_}2174{_}He hurriedly read them, and said they were important; and then he asked me all about General Hazen and where he had gone, and about the breaking out of the Kiowas and Comanches.
10030-8{_}2175{_}I gave him all the information that I possessed, and related the events and adventures of the previous day and night.
10030-8{_}2176{_}"Bill," said he, "you must have had a pretty lively ride.
10030-8{_}2177{_}You certainly had a close call when you ran into the Indians on Walnut Creek.
10030-8{_}2178{_}That was a good joke that you played on old Satanta.
10030-8{_}2179{_}I suppose you're pretty tired after your long journey?"
10030-8{_}2180{_}"I am rather weary, General, that's a fact, as I have been in the saddle since yesterday morning;" was my reply, "but my horse is more tired than I am, and needs attention full as much if not more," I added.
10030-8{_}2181{_}Thereupon the General called an orderly and gave instructions to have my animal well taken care of, and then he said, "Cody, come in and have some breakfast with me."
10030-8{_}2182{_}"No, thank you, General," said I, "Hays City is only a mile from here, and I prefer riding over there, as I know about every one in the town, and want to see some of my friends."
10030-8{_}2183{_}"Very well; do as you please, and come to the post afterwards as I want to see you," said he.
10030-8{_}2184{_}Bidding him good-morning, and telling him that I would return in a few hours, I rode over to Hays City, and at the Perry House I met many of my old friends who were of course all glad to see me.
10030-8{_}2185{_}I took some refreshments and a two hours nap, and afterward returned to Fort Hays, as I was requested.
10030-8{_}2186{_}As I rode up to the headquarters I noticed several scouts in a little group, evidently engaged in conversation on some important matter.
10030-8{_}2187{_}Upon inquiry I learned that General Sheridan had informed them that he was desirous of sending a dispatch to Fort Dodge, a distance of ninety-five miles.
10030-8{_}2188{_}The Indians had recently killed two or three men while they were carrying dispatches between Fort Hays and Fort Dodge, and on this account none of the scouts seemed at all anxious to volunteer, although a reward of several hundred dollars was offered to any one who would carry the dispatches.
10030-8{_}2189{_}They had learned of my experiences of the previous day, and asked me if I did not think it would be a dangerous trip.
10030-8{_}2190{_}I gave it as my opinion that a man might possibly go through without seeing an Indian, but that the chances were ten to one that he would have an exceedingly lively run and a hard time before he reached his destination, if he ever got there at all.
10030-8{_}2191{_}Leaving the scouts to decide among themselves as to who was to go, I reported to General Sheridan, who also informed me that he wished some one to carry dispatches to Fort Dodge.
10030-8{_}2192{_}While we were talking, his chief of scouts Dick Parr, entered and stated that none of the scouts had yet volunteered.
10030-8{_}2193{_}Upon hearing this I got my "brave" up a little, and said:  "General, if there is no one ready to volunteer, I'll carry your dispatches myself."
10030-8{_}2194{_}"I had not thought of asking you to do this duty, Cody, as you are already pretty hard worked.
10030-8{_}2195{_}But it is really important that these dispatches should go through," said the General.
10030-8{_}2196{_}"Well, if you don't get a courier by four o'clock this afternoon, I'll be ready for business at that time.
10030-8{_}2197{_}All I want is a fresh horse," said I; "meantime I'll take a little more rest."
10030-8{_}2198{_}It was not much of a rest, however, that I got, for I went over to Hays City again and had "a time with the boys."
10030-8{_}2199{_}I came back to the post at the appointed hour, and finding that no one had volunteered, I reported to General Sheridan.
10030-8{_}2200{_}He had selected an excellent horse for me, and on handing me the dispatches he said:  "You can start as soon as you wish--the sooner the better; and good luck go with you, my boy."
10030-8{_}2201{_}In about an hour afterwards I was on the road, and just before dark I crossed Smoky Hill River.
10030-8{_}2202{_}I had not yet urged my horse much, as I was saving his strength for the latter end of the route, and for any run that I might have to make in case the "wild-boys" should "jump" me.
10030-8{_}2203{_}So far I had not seen a sign of Indians, and as evening came on I felt comparatively safe.
10030-8{_}2204{_}I had no adventures worth relating during the night, and just before daylight I found myself approaching Saw-log Crossing, on the Pawnee Fork, having then ridden about seventy miles.
10030-8{_}2205{_}A company of colored cavalry, commanded by Major Cox, was stationed at this point, and I approached their camp cautiously, for fear that the pickets might fire upon me--as the darkey soldiers were liable to shoot first and cry "halt" afterwards.
10030-8{_}2206{_}When within hearing distance I yelled out at the top of my voice, and was answered by one of the pickets.
10030-8{_}2207{_}I told him not to shoot, as I was a scout from Fort Hays; and then, calling the sergeant of the guard, I went up to the vidette of the post, who readily recognized me.
10030-8{_}2208{_}I entered the camp and proceeded to the tent of Major Cox, to whom I handed a letter from General Sheridan requesting him to give me a fresh horse.
10030-8{_}2209{_}He at once complied with the request.
10030-8{_}2210{_}After I had slept an hour and had eaten a lunch, I again jumped into the saddle, and before sunrise I was once more on the road.
10030-8{_}2211{_}It was twenty-five miles to Fort Dodge, and I arrived there between nine and ten o'clock, without having seen a single Indian.
10030-8{_}2212{_}After delivering the dispatches to the commanding officer, I met Johnny Austin, chief of scouts at this post, who was an old friend of mine.
10030-8{_}2213{_}Upon his invitation I took a nap at his house, and when I awoke, fresh for business once more, he informed me that the Indians had been all around the post for the past two or three days, running off cattle and horses, and occasionally killing a stray man.
10030-8{_}2214{_}It was a wonder to him that I had met with none of the red-skins on the way there.
10030-8{_}2215{_}The Indians, he said, were also very thick on the Arkansas River, between Fort Dodge and Fort Larned, and making considerable trouble.
10030-8{_}2216{_}Fort Dodge was located sixty-five miles west of Fort Larned, the latter post being on the Pawnee Fork, about five miles from its junction with the Arkansas River.
10030-8{_}2217{_}The commanding officer at Fort Dodge was anxious to send some dispatches to Fort Larned, but the scouts, like those at Fort Hays, were rather backward about volunteering, as it was considered a very dangerous undertaking to make the trip.
10030-8{_}2218{_}As Fort Larned was my post, and as I wanted to go there anyhow, I said to Austin that I would carry the dispatches, and if any of the boys wished to go along, I would like to have them for company's sake.
10030-8{_}2219{_}Austin reported my offer to the commanding officer, who sent for me and said he would be happy to have me take his dispatches, if I could stand the trip on top of all that I had already done.
10030-8{_}2220{_}"All I want is a good fresh horse, sir," said I.
10030-8{_}2221{_}"I am sorry to say that we haven't a decent horse here, but we have a reliable and honest government mule, if that will do you," said the officer.
10030-8{_}2222{_}"Trot out your mule," said I, "that's good enough for me.
10030-8{_}2223{_}I am ready at any time, sir."
10030-8{_}2224{_}The mule was forthcoming, and at dark I pulled out for Fort Larned, and proceeded uninterruptedly to Coon Creek, thirty miles out from Dodge.
10030-8{_}2225{_}I had left the main wagon road some distance to the south, and had traveled parallel with it, thinking this to be a safer course, as the Indians might be lying in wait on the main road for dispatch bearers and scouts.
10030-8{_}2226{_}At Coon Creek I dismounted and led the mule by the bridle down to the water, where I took a drink, using my hat for a dipper.
10030-8{_}2227{_}While I was engaged in getting the water, the mule jerked loose and struck out down the creek.
10030-8{_}2228{_}I followed him in hopes that he would catch his foot in the bridle rein and stop, but this he seemed to have no idea of doing.
10030-8{_}2229{_}He was making straight for the wagon road, and I did not know what minute he might run into a band of Indians.
10030-8{_}2230{_}He finally got on the road, but instead of going back toward Fort Dodge, as I naturally expected he would do, he turned eastward toward Fort Larned, and kept up a little jog trot just ahead of me, but would not let me come up to him, although I tried it again and again.
10030-8{_}2231{_}I had my gun in my hand, and several times I was strongly tempted to shoot him, and would probably have done so had it not been for fear of bringing Indians down upon me, and besides he was carrying the saddle for me.
10030-8{_}2232{_}So I trudged on after the obstinate "critter," and if there ever was a government mule that deserved and received a good round cursing it was that one.
10030-8{_}2233{_}I had neglected the precaution of tying one end of my lariat to his bit and the other to my belt, as I had done a few nights before, and I blamed myself for this gross piece of negligence.
10030-8{_}2234{_}Mile after mile I kept on after that mule, and every once in a while I indulged in strong language respecting the whole mule fraternity.
10030-8{_}2235{_}From Coon Creek to Fort Larned it was thirty-five miles, and I finally concluded that my prospects were good for "hoofing" the whole distance.
10030-8{_}2236{_}We--that is to say, the confounded mule and myself--were making pretty good time.
10030-8{_}2237{_}There was nothing to hold the mule, and I was all the time trying to catch him--which urged him on.
10030-8{_}2238{_}I made every step count, for I wanted to reach Fort Larned before daylight, in order to avoid if possible the Indians, to whom it would have been "pie" to have caught me there on foot.
10030-8{_}2239{_}The mule stuck to the road and kept on for Larned, and I did the same thing.
10030-8{_}2240{_}Just as day was beginning to break, we--that is the mule and myself--found ourselves on a hill looking down into the valley of the Pawnee Fork, in which Fort Larned was located, only four miles away; and when the morning gun belched forth we were within half a mile of the post.
10030-8{_}2241{_}"Now," said I, "Mr. Mule, it is my turn," and raising my gun to my shoulder, in "dead earnest" this time, I blazed away, hitting the animal in the hip.
10030-8{_}2242{_}Throwing a second cartridge into the gun, I let him have another shot, and I continued to pour the lead into him until I had him completely laid out.
10030-8{_}2243{_}Like the great majority of government mules, he was a tough one to kill, and he clung to life with all the tenaciousness of his obstinate nature.
10030-8{_}2244{_}He was, without doubt, the toughest and meanest mule I ever saw, and he died hard.
10030-8{_}2245{_}The troops, hearing the reports of the gun, came rushing out to see what was the matter.
10030-8{_}2246{_}They found that the mule had passed in his chips, and when they learned the cause they all agreed that I had served him just right.
10030-8{_}2247{_}Taking the saddle and bridle from the dead body, I proceeded into the post and delivered the dispatches to Captain Parker.
10030-8{_}2248{_}I then went over to Dick Curtis' house, which was headquarters for the scouts, and there put in several hours of solid sleep.
10030-8{_}2249{_}During the day General Hazen returned from Fort Harker, and he also had some important dispatches to send to General Sheridan.
10030-8{_}2250{_}I was feeling quite elated over my big ride; and seeing that I was getting the best of the other scouts in regard to making a record, I volunteered to carry General Hazen's dispatches to Fort Hays.
10030-8{_}2251{_}The General accepted my services, although he thought it was unnecessary for me to kill myself.
10030-8{_}2252{_}I told him that I had business at Fort Hays, and wished to go there anyway, and it would make no difference to the other scouts, for none of them appeared willing to undertake the trip.
10030-8{_}2253{_}Accordingly, that night I left Fort Larned on an excellent horse, and next morning at daylight found myself once more in General Sheridan's headquarters at Fort Hays.
10030-8{_}2254{_}The General was surprised to see me, and still more so when I told him of the time I had made in riding to Fort Dodge, and that I had taken dispatches from Fort Dodge to Fort Larned; and when, in addition to this, I mentioned my journey of the night previous, General Sheridan thought my ride from post to post, taken as a whole, was a remarkable one, and he said that he did not know of its equal.
10030-8{_}2255{_}I can safely say that I have never heard of its being beaten in a country infested with hostile Indians.
10030-8{_}2256{_}To recapitulate: I had ridden from Fort Larned to Fort Zarah (a distance of sixty-five miles) and back in twelve hours, including the time when I was taken across the Arkansas by the Indians.
10030-8{_}2257{_}In the succeeding twelve hours I had gone from Fort Larned to Fort Hays, a distance of sixty-five miles.
10030-8{_}2258{_}In the next twenty-four hours I had gone from Fort Hays to Fort Dodge, a distance of ninety-five miles.
10030-8{_}2259{_}The following night I had traveled from Fort Dodge thirty miles on muleback and thirty-five miles on foot to Fort Larned; and the next night sixty-five miles more to Fort Hays.
10030-8{_}2260{_}Altogether I had ridden (and walked) 355 miles in fifty-eight riding hours, or an average of over six miles an hour.
10030-8{_}2261{_}Of course, this may not be regarded as very fast riding, but taking into consideration the fact that it was mostly done in the night and over a wild country, with no roads to follow, and that I had to be continually on the look out for Indians, it was thought at the time to be a big ride, as well as a most dangerous one.
10030-8{_}2262{_}CHAPTER XVII.
10030-8{_}2263{_}AN APPOINTMENT.
10030-8{_}2264{_}General Sheridan highly complimented me for what I had done, and informed me that I need not report back to General Hazen, as he had more important work for me to do.
10030-8{_}2265{_}He told me that the Fifth Cavalry--one of the finest regiments in the army--was on its way to the Department of the Missouri, and that he was going to send it on an expedition against the Dog Soldier Indians, who were infesting the Republican River region.
10030-8{_}2266{_}"Cody," continued he, "I have decided to appoint you as guide and chief of scouts with the command.
10030-8{_}2267{_}How does that suit you?"
10030-8{_}2268{_}"First-rate, General, and I thank you for the honor," I replied, as gracefully as I knew how.
10030-8{_}2269{_}The Dog Soldier Indians were a band of Cheyennes and unruly, turbulent members of other tribes, who would not enter into any treaty, or keep a treaty if they made one, and who had always refused to go upon a reservation.
10030-8{_}2270{_}They were a warlike body of well-built, daring and restless braves, and were determined to hold possession of the country in the vicinity of the Republican and Solomon Rivers.
10030-8{_}2271{_}They were called "Dog Soldiers" because they were principally Cheyennes--a name derived from the French _chien_, a dog.
10030-8{_}2272{_}After my conversation with the General, I went over to Hays City, where I met some of General Forsyth's scouts, who had just returned from one of the severest battles ever fought with the Indians.
10030-8{_}2273{_}As it will not be out of place in this connection, I will here give a brief history of that memorable event.
10030-8{_}2274{_}[Illustration: GENERAL PHIL.
10030-8{_}2275{_}SHERIDAN.]
10030-8{_}2276{_}The Indians had become quite troublesome, and General Sheridan had selected General George A. Forsyth to go out on an expedition, and punish them for their recent depredations.
10030-8{_}2277{_}There was a scarcity of troops at Fort Hays at that time, so General Forsyth recruited a company of frontiersmen who could move rapidly, as they were to carry no luggage, and were to travel without the ordinary transportation.
10030-8{_}2278{_}Thirty of these frontiersmen came from Fort Harker, and twenty from Fort Hays.
10030-8{_}2279{_}It was certainly a small body of men, but nearly every one of them was an experienced hunter, guide, scout and Indian-fighter, and they could fight the red-skins in their own way.
10030-8{_}2280{_}In four days they were prepared to take the field, and on the morning of the 29th of August, 1868, they rode out of Fort Hays to meet the Indians.
10030-8{_}2281{_}Lieutenant F.H.
10030-8{_}2282{_}Beecher, of the Third Infantry, nephew of Henry Ward Beecher, was second in command; Brevet Major-General W.H.H.
10030-8{_}2283{_}McCall, who had been in the volunteer army, acted as first sergeant; Dr. John Mowers, of Hays City, who had been a volunteer army surgeon, was the surgeon of the expedition; and Sharpe Grover was the chief guide.
10030-8{_}2284{_}Resting at Fort Wallace, they started September 10th, for the town of Sheridan, thirteen miles distant, where a band of Indians had attacked a train, killed two teamsters, and stolen some cattle.
10030-8{_}2285{_}Arriving at Sheridan they easily found the Indian trail, and followed it for some distance.
10030-8{_}2286{_}On the eighth day out from Fort Wallace, the command went into camp late in the afternoon, on the Arickaree, which was then not more than eight or nine feet wide at that point, and only two or three inches deep.
10030-8{_}2287{_}It was evident to the men that they were not far from the Indians, and it was decided that the next day they would find them and give them a fight.
10030-8{_}2288{_}Early next morning, September 19th, the cry of "Indians" startled the command.
10030-8{_}2289{_}Every man jumped for his horse.
10030-8{_}2290{_}A half-dozen red-skins, yelling and whooping and making a hideous racket, and firing their guns, rode up and attempted to stampede the horses, several of which, together with the four pack-mules, were so frightened that they broke loose and got away.
10030-8{_}2291{_}The Indians then rode off, followed by a few shots.
10030-8{_}2292{_}In a minute afterwards, hundreds of Indian warriors--it was estimated that there were nearly one thousand--came galloping down upon the command from every quarter, completely hemming them in.
10030-8{_}2293{_}Acting under the order of General Forsyth, the men retreated to a small island, tied their horses in a circle to the bushes, and then, throwing themselves upon the ground, they began the defense by firing at the approaching enemy, who came pretty close and gave them a raking fire.
10030-8{_}2294{_}The besieged scouts at the first opportunity threw up a small breastwork with their knives.
10030-8{_}2295{_}The firing, however, continued back and forth, and early in the fight Forsyth was twice seriously wounded--once in the right thigh, and once in the left leg.
10030-8{_}2296{_}Dr. Mowers was also wounded in the head, and soon died.
10030-8{_}2297{_}Two other men had been killed, and several wounded.
10030-8{_}2298{_}All the horses of the command were killed by nine o'clock in the morning.
10030-8{_}2299{_}Shortly afterwards over three hundred Dog-Soldier Indians commanded by old "Roman Nose," charged down upon the little band of heroes, giving them volley after volley; but finally the scouts, at a favorable opportunity, returned their fire with telling effect.
10030-8{_}2300{_}"Roman Nose" and "Medicine Man" were killed, and fell from their horses when within less than one rod of the scouts, who thereupon sent up a triumphant shout.
10030-8{_}2301{_}The charging braves now weakened, and in a few moments they were driven back.
10030-8{_}2302{_}It was a brilliant charge, and was most nobly and bravely repulsed.
10030-8{_}2303{_}The scouts had again suffered severely, having several men wounded, among the number being Lieutenant Beecher who died that night.
