10030-8{_}3359{_}In a few days the command left for its destination, taking the cars at McPherson Station, where I bade my old friends adieu.
10030-8{_}3360{_}During the next few weeks I had but little to do, as the post was garrisoned by infantry, awaiting the arrival of the Third Cavalry.
10030-8{_}3361{_}[Illustration: FINDING THE REMAINS OF THE BUCK PARTY.]
10030-8{_}3362{_}CHAPTER XXV.
10030-8{_}3363{_}HUNTING WITH A GRAND DUKE.
10030-8{_}3364{_}About the first of January, 1872, General Forsyth and Dr. Asch, of Sheridan's staff came out to Fort McPherson to make preparations for a big buffalo hunt for the Grand Duke Alexis, of Russia; and as this was to be no ordinary affair, these officers had been sent by General Sheridan to have all the necessary arrangements perfected by the time the Grand Duke should arrive.
10030-8{_}3365{_}They learned from me that there were plenty of buffaloes in the vicinity and especially on the Red Willow, sixty miles distant.
10030-8{_}3366{_}They said they would like to go over on the Red Willow and pick out a suitable place for the camp; they also inquired the location of the Spotted Tail, Sioux Indians.
10030-8{_}3367{_}Spotted Tail had permission from the Government to hunt the buffalo, with his people during the winter, in the Republican river country.
10030-8{_}3368{_}It was my opinion that they were located somewhere on the Frenchman's Fork about one hundred and fifty miles from Fort McPherson.
10030-8{_}3369{_}General Sheridan's commissioners informed me, that he wished me to visit Spotted Tail's camp, and induce about one hundred of the leading warriors and chiefs, to come to the point where it should be decided to locate the Alexis hunting camp, and to be there by the time the Grand Duke should arrive, so that he could see a body of American Indians and observe the manner in which they killed buffaloes.
10030-8{_}3370{_}The Indians would also be called upon to give a grand war dance in honor of the distinguished visitor.
10030-8{_}3371{_}Next morning General Forsyth and Dr. Asch, accompanied by Captain Hays, who had been left at Fort McPherson in charge of the Fifth Cavalry horses, taking an ambulance and a light wagon, to carry their tents, and provisions sufficient to last them two or three days; started, under my guidance, with a small escort, for Red Willow Creek, arriving there at night.
10030-8{_}3372{_}The next day we selected a pleasant camping place on a little knoll in the valley of the Red Willow.
10030-8{_}3373{_}General Forsyth and his party returned to the post the next day while I left for Spotted Tail's camp.
10030-8{_}3374{_}The weather was very cold and I found my journey by no means a pleasant one as I was obliged to camp out with only my saddle blankets; and besides, there was more or less danger from the Indians themselves; for, although Spotted Tail himself was friendly, I was afraid I might have difficulty in getting into his camp.
10030-8{_}3375{_}I was liable at any moment to run into a party of his young men who might be out hunting, and as I had many enemies among the Sioux, I would be running considerable risk in meeting them.
10030-8{_}3376{_}At the end of the first day I camped on Stinking Water, a tributary of the Frenchman's Fork, where I built a little fire in the timber; but it was so very cold I was not able to sleep much.
10030-8{_}3377{_}Getting an early start in the morning I followed up the Frenchman's Fork and late in the afternoon I could see, from the fresh horse tracks and from the dead buffaloes lying here and there, recently killed, that I was nearing Spotted Tail's camp.
10030-8{_}3378{_}I rode on for a few miles further, and then hiding my horse in a low ravine, I crawled up a high hill, where I obtained a good view of the country.
10030-8{_}3379{_}I could see for four or five miles up the creek, and got sight of a village and of two or three hundred ponies in its vicinity.
10030-8{_}3380{_}I waited until night came and then I succeeded in riding into the Indian camp unobserved.
10030-8{_}3381{_}[Illustration: SPOTTED TAIL.]
10030-8{_}3382{_}I had seen Spotted Tail's camp when he came from the north and I knew the kind of lodge he was living in.
10030-8{_}3383{_}As I entered the village I wrapped a blanket around my head so that the Indians could not tell whether I was a white or a red man.
10030-8{_}3384{_}In this way I rode around until I found Spotted Tail's lodge.
10030-8{_}3385{_}Dismounting from my horse I opened his tent door and looked in and saw the old chief lying on some robes.
10030-8{_}3386{_}I spoke to him and he recognized me at once and invited me to enter.
10030-8{_}3387{_}Inside the lodge I found a white man, an old frontiersman, Todd Randall, who was Spotted Tail's agent and who had lived a great many years with the Indians.
10030-8{_}3388{_}He understood their language perfectly and did all the interpreting for Spotted Tail.
10030-8{_}3389{_}Through him I readily communicated with the chief and informed him of my errand.
10030-8{_}3390{_}I told him that the warriors and chiefs would greatly please General Sheridan if they would meet him in about ten sleeps at the old Government crossing of the Red Willow.
10030-8{_}3391{_}I further informed him that there was a great chief from across the water who was coming there to visit him.
10030-8{_}3392{_}Spotted Tail replied that he would be very glad to go; that the next morning he would call his people together and select those who would accompany him.
10030-8{_}3393{_}I told Spotted Tail how I had entered his camp.
10030-8{_}3394{_}He replied that I had acted wisely; that although his people were friendly, yet some of his young men had a grudge against me, and I might have had difficulty with them had I met them away from the village.
10030-8{_}3395{_}He directed his squaw to get me something to eat, and ordered that my horse be taken care of, and upon his invitation I spent the remainder of the night in his lodge.
10030-8{_}3396{_}Next morning the chiefs and warriors assembled according to orders, and to them was stated the object of my visit.
10030-8{_}3397{_}They were asked:  "Do you know who this man is?"
10030-8{_}3398{_}"Yes, we know him well," replied one, "that is Pa-he-haska," (that being my name among the Sioux, which translated means "Long Hair") "that is our old enemy," a great many of the Indians, who were with Spotted Tail at this time, had been driven out of the Republican country.
10030-8{_}3399{_}"That is he," said Spotted Tail.
10030-8{_}3400{_}"I want all my people to be kind to him and treat him as my friend."
10030-8{_}3401{_}I noticed that several of them were looking daggers at me.
10030-8{_}3402{_}They appeared as if they wished to raise my hair then and there.
10030-8{_}3403{_}Spotted Tail motioned and I followed him into his lodge, and thereupon the Indians dispersed.
10030-8{_}3404{_}Having the assurance of Spotted Tail that none of the young men would follow me I started back for the Red Willow, arriving the second night.
10030-8{_}3405{_}There I found Captain Egan with a company of the Second Cavalry and a wagon train loaded with tents, grain, provisions, etc.
10030-8{_}3406{_}The men were leveling off the ground and were making preparations to put up large wall tents for the Grand Duke Alexis and his _suite_, and for General Sheridan, his staff and other officers, and invited guests of the party.
10030-8{_}3407{_}Proceeding to Fort McPherson I reported what had been done.
10030-8{_}3408{_}Thereupon Quartermaster Hays selected from the five or six hundred horses in his charge, seventy-five of the very best, which were sent to the Red Willow, to be used by Alexis and his party at the coming hunt.
10030-8{_}3409{_}In a day or two a large supply of provisions, liquors, etc., arrived from Chicago, together with bedding and furniture for the tents; all of which were sent over to Camp Alexis.
10030-8{_}3410{_}[Illustration: GRAND DUKE ALEXIS.]
10030-8{_}3411{_}At last, on the morning of the 12th of January, 1872, the Grand Duke and party arrived at North Platte by special train; in charge of a Mr. Francis Thompson.
10030-8{_}3412{_}Captain Hays and myself, with five or six ambulances, fifteen or twenty extra saddle-horses and a company of cavalry under Captain Egan, were at the dépôt in time to receive them.
10030-8{_}3413{_}Presently General Sheridan and a large, fine-looking young man, whom we at once concluded to be the Grand Duke came out of the cars and approached us.
10030-8{_}3414{_}General Sheridan at once introduced me to the Grand Duke as Buffalo Bill, for he it was, and said that I was to take charge of him and show him how to kill buffalo.
10030-8{_}3415{_}In less than half an hour the whole party were dashing away towards the south, across the South Platte and towards the Medicine; upon reaching which point we halted for a change of horses and a lunch.
10030-8{_}3416{_}Resuming our ride we reached Camp Alexis in the afternoon.
10030-8{_}3417{_}General Sheridan was well pleased with the arrangements that had been made and was delighted to find that Spotted Tail and his Indians had arrived on time.
10030-8{_}3418{_}They were objects of great curiosity to the Grand Duke, who spent considerable time in looking at them, and watching their exhibitions of horsemanship, sham fights, etc.
10030-8{_}3419{_}That evening the Indians gave the grand war dance, which I had arranged for.
10030-8{_}3420{_}[Illustration: INDIAN EXERCISES.]
10030-8{_}3421{_}General Custer, who was one of the hunting party, carried on a mild flirtation with one of Spotted Tail's daughters, who had accompanied her father thither, and it was noticed also that the Duke Alexis paid considerable attention to another handsome red-skin maiden.
10030-8{_}3422{_}The night passed pleasantly, and all retired with great expectations of having a most enjoyable and successful buffalo hunt.
10030-8{_}3423{_}The Duke Alexis asked me a great many questions as to how we shot buffaloes, and what kind of a gun or pistol we used, and if he was going to have a good horse.
10030-8{_}3424{_}I told him that he was to have my celebrated buffalo horse Buckskin Joe, and when we went into a buffalo herd all he would have to do was to sit on the horse's back and fire away.
10030-8{_}3425{_}At nine o'clock next morning we were all in our saddles, and in a few minutes were galloping over the prairies in search of a buffalo herd.
10030-8{_}3426{_}We had not gone far before we observed a herd some distance ahead of us crossing our way; after that we proceeded cautiously, so as to keep out of sight until we were ready to make a charge.
10030-8{_}3427{_}Of course the main thing was to give Alexis the first chance and the best shot at the buffaloes, and when all was in readiness we dashed over a little knoll that had hidden us from view, and in a few minutes we were among them.
10030-8{_}3428{_}Alexis at first preferred to use his pistol instead of a gun.
10030-8{_}3429{_}He fired six shots from this weapon at buffaloes only twenty feet away from him, but as he shot wildly, not one of his bullets took effect.
10030-8{_}3430{_}Riding up to his side and seeing that his weapon was empty, I exchanged pistols with him.
10030-8{_}3431{_}He again fired six shots, without dropping a buffalo.
10030-8{_}3432{_}Seeing that the animals were bound to make their escape without his killing one of them, unless he had a better weapon, I rode up to him, gave him my old reliable "Lucretia," and told him to urge his horse close to the buffaloes, and I would then give him the word when to shoot.
10030-8{_}3433{_}At the same time I gave old Buckskin Joe a blow with my whip, and with a few jumps the horse carried the Grand Duke to within about ten feet of a big buffalo bull.
10030-8{_}3434{_}"Now is your time," said I.
10030-8{_}3435{_}He fired, and down went the buffalo.
10030-8{_}3436{_}The Grand Duke stopped his horse, dropped his gun on the ground, and commenced waving his hat.
10030-8{_}3437{_}When his _suite_ came galloping up, he began talking to them in a tongue which I could not understand.
10030-8{_}3438{_}Presently General Sheridan joined the group, and the ambulances were brought up.
10030-8{_}3439{_}Very soon the corks began to fly from the champagne bottles, in honor of the Grand Duke Alexis, who had killed the first buffalo.
10030-8{_}3440{_}It was reported in a great many of the newspapers that I shot the first buffalo for Alexis, while in some it was stated that I held the buffalo while His Royal Highness killed it.
10030-8{_}3441{_}But the way I have related the affair is the correct version.
10030-8{_}3442{_}It was thought that we had had about sport enough for one day, and accordingly I was directed by General Sheridan to guide the party back to camp, and we were soon on our way thither.
10030-8{_}3443{_}Several of the party, however, concluded to have a little hunt on their own account, and presently we saw them galloping over the prairie in different directions in pursuit of buffaloes.
10030-8{_}3444{_}While we were crossing a deep ravine, on our way to camp, we ran into a small band of buffaloes that had been frightened by some of the hunters.
10030-8{_}3445{_}As they rushed past us, not more than thirty yards distant, Alexis raised his pistol, fired and killed a buffalo cow.
10030-8{_}3446{_}It was either an extraordinary good shot or a "scratch"--probably the latter, for it surprised the Grand Duke as well as everybody else.
10030-8{_}3447{_}We gave him three cheers, and when the ambulance came up we took a pull at the champagne in honor of the Grand Duke's success.
10030-8{_}3448{_}I was in hopes that he would kill five or six more buffaloes before we reached camp, especially if a basket of champagne was to be opened every time he dropped one.
10030-8{_}3449{_}General Sheridan directed me to take care of the hides and heads of the buffaloes which Alexis had killed, as the Duke wished to keep them as souvenirs of the hunt.
10030-8{_}3450{_}I also cut out the choice meat from the cow and brought it into camp, and that night at supper Alexis had the pleasure of dining on broiled buffalo steak obtained from the animal which he had shot himself.
10030-8{_}3451{_}We remained at this camp two or three days, during which we hunted most of the time, the Grand Duke himself killing eight buffaloes.
10030-8{_}3452{_}One day Alexis desired to see how the Indians hunted buffaloes and killed them with bow and arrow; so Spotted Tail, selecting some of his best hunters, had them surround a herd, and bring the animals down, not only with arrows, but with lances.
10030-8{_}3453{_}The Grand Duke was told to follow upon the heels of one celebrated Indian hunter, whose name was "Two Lance," and watch him bring down the game; for this chief had the reputation of being able to send an arrow through and through the body of a buffalo.
10030-8{_}3454{_}Upon this occasion he did not belie his reputation, for he sent an arrow _through_ a buffalo, which fell dead at the shot, and the arrow was given to Alexis as a souvenir of his hunt on the American Plains.
10030-8{_}3455{_}[Illustration: TWO LANCE KILLING A BUFFALO.]
10030-8{_}3456{_}When the Grand Duke was satisfied with the sport, orders were given for the return to the railroad.
10030-8{_}3457{_}The conveyance provided for the Grand Duke and General Sheridan was a heavy double-seated open carriage, or rather an Irish dog-cart, and it was drawn by four spirited cavalry horses which were not much used to the harness.
10030-8{_}3458{_}The driver was Bill Reed, an old overland stage driver and wagon master; on our way in, the Grand Duke frequently expressed his admiration of the skillful manner in which Reed handled the reins.
10030-8{_}3459{_}General Sheridan informed the Duke that I also had been a stage-driver in the Rocky Mountains, and thereupon His Royal Highness expressed a desire to see me drive.
10030-8{_}3460{_}I was in advance at the time, and General Sheridan sang out to me:  "Cody, get in here and show the Duke how you can drive.
10030-8{_}3461{_}Mr. Reed will exchange places with you and ride your horse."
10030-8{_}3462{_}"All right, General," said I, and in a few moments I had the reins and we were rattling away over the prairie.
10030-8{_}3463{_}When we were approaching Medicine Creek, General Sheridan said: "Shake 'em up a little, Bill, and give us some old-time stage-driving."
10030-8{_}3464{_}I gave the horses a crack or two of the whip, and they started off at a very rapid gait.
10030-8{_}3465{_}They had a light load to pull, and kept increasing their speed at every jump, and I found it difficult to hold them.
10030-8{_}3466{_}They fairly flew over the ground, and at last we reached a steep hill, or divide, which, led down into the valley of the Medicine.
10030-8{_}3467{_}There was no brake on the wagon, and the horses were not much on the hold-back.
10030-8{_}3468{_}I saw that it would be impossible to stop them.
10030-8{_}3469{_}All I could do was to keep them straight in the track and let them go it down the hill, for three miles; which distance, I believe, was made in about six minutes.
10030-8{_}3470{_}Every once in a while the hind wheels would strike a rut and take a bound, and not touch the ground again for fifteen or twenty feet.
10030-8{_}3471{_}The Duke and the General were kept rather busy in holding their positions on the seats, and when they saw that I was keeping the horses straight in the road, they seemed to enjoy the dash which we were making.
10030-8{_}3472{_}I was unable to stop the team until they ran into the camp where we were to obtain a fresh relay, and there I succeeded in checking them.
10030-8{_}3473{_}The Grand Duke said he didn't want any more of that kind of driving, as he preferred to go a little slower.
10030-8{_}3474{_}On arriving at the railroad, the Duke invited me into his car, and made me some valuable presents, at the same time giving me a cordial invitation to visit him, if ever I should come to his country.
10030-8{_}3475{_}General Sheridan took occasion to remind me of an invitation to visit New York which I had received from some of the gentlemen who accompanied the General on the hunt from Fort McPherson to Hays City, in September of the previous year.
10030-8{_}3476{_}Said he:  "You will never have a better opportunity to accept that invitation than now.
10030-8{_}3477{_}I have had a talk with General Ord concerning you, and he will give you a leave of absence whenever you are ready to start.
10030-8{_}3478{_}Write a letter to General Stager, of Chicago, that you are now prepared to accept the invitation, and he will send you a pass."
10030-8{_}3479{_}Thanking the General for his kindness, I then bade him and the Grand Duke good-bye, and soon their train was out of sight.
10030-8{_}3480{_}CHAPTER XXVI.
10030-8{_}3481{_}SIGHT-SEEING.
10030-8{_}3482{_}General Ord, commanding the Department of the Platte at the time, and who had been out on the Alexis hunt, had some business to attend to at Fort McPherson, and I accepted his invitation to ride over to the post with him in an ambulance.
10030-8{_}3483{_}On the way thither he asked me how I would like to have an officer's commission in the regular army.
10030-8{_}3484{_}He said that General Sheridan and himself had had some conversation about the matter, and if I wanted a commission, one could easily be procured for me.
10030-8{_}3485{_}I thanked General Ord for his kindness, and said that although an officer's commission in the regular army was a tempting prize, yet I preferred to remain in the position I was then holding.
10030-8{_}3486{_}He concluded by stating that if at any time I should wish a commission, all that I would have to do to secure it would be to inform him of my desire.
10030-8{_}3487{_}Having determined to visit New York, I acted upon General Sheridan's suggestion and wrote to General Stager, from whom in a few days I received my railroad passes.
10030-8{_}3488{_}Obtaining thirty days' leave of absence from the department, I struck out for the East.
10030-8{_}3489{_}On arriving in Chicago, in February, 1872, I was met at the dépôt by Colonel M.V.
10030-8{_}3490{_}Sheridan, who said that his brother, the General, had not yet returned, but had sent word that I was to be his and the Colonel's guest, at their house, while I remained in Chicago.
10030-8{_}3491{_}I spent two or three days very pleasantly in the great city of the West, meeting several of the gentlemen who had been out on the Sheridan hunt in September--General Stager, Colonel Wilson, editor of the _Journal_; Mr. Sam Johnson, General Rucker and others--by all of whom I was most cordially received and well entertained.
10030-8{_}3492{_}I was introduced to quite a number of the best people of the city, and was invited to several "swell" dinners.
10030-8{_}3493{_}I also accompanied General Sheridan--who meantime had returned to the city--to a ball at Riverside--an aristocratic suburb.
10030-8{_}3494{_}[Illustration: AN EMBARRASSING SITUATION]  On this occasion I became so embarrassed that it was more difficult for me to face the throng of beautiful ladies, than it would have been to confront a hundred hostile Indians.
10030-8{_}3495{_}This was my first trip to the East, and I had not yet become accustomed to being stared at.
10030-8{_}3496{_}And besides this, the hundreds of questions which I was called upon to answer further embarrassed and perplexed me.
10030-8{_}3497{_}According to the route laid out for me by General Stager, I was to stop at Niagara Falls, Buffalo and Rochester on my way to New York, and he provided me with all the necessary railroad passes.
10030-8{_}3498{_}Just as I was about to leave Chicago I met Professor Henry A.
10030-8{_}3499{_}Ward, of Rochester, for whom during the previous year or two I had collected a large number of specimens of wild animals.
10030-8{_}3500{_}He was on his way to Rochester, and kindly volunteered to act as my guide until we reached that point.
10030-8{_}3501{_}We spent one day in viewing the wonders of Niagara, and I stopped one day at Rochester and was shown the beauties of that handsome city by Professor Ward, and I had the honor of receiving an invitation to dine with the Mayor.
10030-8{_}3502{_}On arriving at New York I was met at the dépôt by Mr. J.G.
10030-8{_}3503{_}Hecksher, who had been appointed as "a committee of one" to escort me to the Union Club, where James Gordon Bennett, Leonard W. Jerome and others were to give me an informal reception, and where I was to make my headquarters during my visit in the great metropolis.
10030-8{_}3504{_}I had an elegant dinner at the club rooms, with the gentlemen who had been out on the September hunt, and other members of the club.
10030-8{_}3505{_}After dinner, in company with Mr. Hecksher--who acted as my guide--I started out on the trail of my friend, Ned Buntline, whom we found at the Brevoort Place Hotel.
10030-8{_}3506{_}He was delighted to see me, and insisted on my becoming his guest.
10030-8{_}3507{_}He would listen to no excuses, and on introducing me to Messrs. Overton & Blair, proprietors of the Brevoort, they also gave me a pressing invitation to make my home at their house.
10030-8{_}3508{_}I finally compromised the matter by agreeing to divide my time between the Union Club, the Brevoort House, and Ned Buntline's quarters.
10030-8{_}3509{_}The next few days I spent in viewing the sights of New York, everything being new and startling, convincing me that as yet I had seen but a small portion of the world.
10030-8{_}3510{_}I received numerous dinner invitations, as well as invitations to visit different places of amusement and interest; but as they came in so thick and fast, I soon became badly demoralized and confused.
10030-8{_}3511{_}I found I had accepted invitations to dine at half a dozen or more houses on the same day and at the same hour.
10030-8{_}3512{_}James Gordon Bennett had prepared a dinner for me, at which quite a large number of his friends were to be present, but owing to my confusion, arising from the many other invitations I had received, I forgot all about it, and dined elsewhere.
10030-8{_}3513{_}This was "a bad break," but I did not learn of my mistake until next day, when at the Union Club House several gentlemen, among them Lawrence Jerome, inquired "where in the world I had been," and why I had not put in an appearance at Bennett's dinner.
10030-8{_}3514{_}They said that Bennett had taken great pains to give me a splendid reception, that the party had waited till nine o'clock for me, and that my non-arrival caused considerable disappointment.
10030-8{_}3515{_}I apologized as well as I could, by saying that I had been out on a scout and had got lost, and had forgotten all about the dinner; and expressed my regret for the disappointment I had created by my forgetfulness.
10030-8{_}3516{_}August Belmont, the banker, being near said:  "Never mind, gentlemen, I'll give Cody a dinner at my house."
10030-8{_}3517{_}"Thank you, sir," said I; "I see you are determined that I shall not run short of rations while I am in the city.
10030-8{_}3518{_}I'll be there, sure."
10030-8{_}3519{_}Both Mr. Jerome and Mr. Hecksher told me that I must not disappoint Mr. Belmont, for his dinners were splendid affairs.
10030-8{_}3520{_}I made a note of the date, and at the appointed time I was promptly at Mr. Belmont's mansion, where I spent a very enjoyable evening.
10030-8{_}3521{_}Mr. Bennett, who was among the guests, having forgiven my carelessness, invited me to accompany him to the Liederkranz masked ball, which was to take place in a few evenings, and would be a grand spectacle.
10030-8{_}3522{_}Together we attended the ball, and during the evening I was well entertained.
10030-8{_}3523{_}The dancers kept on their masks until midnight, and the merry and motley throng presented a brilliant scene, moving gracefully beneath the bright gas-light to the inspiriting music.
10030-8{_}3524{_}To me it was a novel and entertaining sight, and in many respects reminded me greatly of an Indian war-dance.
10030-8{_}3525{_}Acting upon the suggestion of Mr. Bennett, I had dressed myself in my buckskin suit, and I naturally attracted considerable attention; especially when I took part in the dancing and exhibited some of my backwoods steps, which, although not as graceful as some, were a great deal more emphatic.
10030-8{_}3526{_}But when I undertook to do artistic dancing, I found I was decidedly out of place in that crowd, and I accordingly withdrew from the floor.
10030-8{_}3527{_}I occasionally passed an evening at Niblo's Garden, viewing the many beauties of "The Black Crook," which was then having its long run, under the management of Jarrett & Palmer, whose acquaintance I had made, and who extended to me the freedom of the theater.
10030-8{_}3528{_}Ned Buntline and Fred Maeder had dramatized one of the stories which the former had written about me for the _New York Weekly_.
10030-8{_}3529{_}The drama was called "Buffalo Bill, the King of Border Men."
10030-8{_}3530{_}While I was in New York it was produced at the Bowery Theater; J.B. Studley, an excellent actor, appearing in the character of "Buffalo Bill," and Mrs. W.G.
10030-8{_}3531{_}Jones, a fine actress, taking the part of my sister, a leading _rôle_.
10030-8{_}3532{_}I was curious to see how I would look when represented by some one else, and of course I was present on the opening night, a private box having been reserved for me.
10030-8{_}3533{_}The theater was packed, every seat being occupied as well as the standing-room.
10030-8{_}3534{_}The drama was played smoothly, and created a great deal of enthusiasm.
10030-8{_}3535{_}The audience, upon learning that the real "Buffalo Bill" was present, gave several cheers between the acts, and I was called on to come out on the stage and make a speech.
10030-8{_}3536{_}Mr. Freleigh, the manager, insisted that I should comply with the request, and that I should be introduced to Mr. Studley.
10030-8{_}3537{_}I finally consented, and the next moment I found myself standing behind the footlights and in front of an audience for the first time in my life.
10030-8{_}3538{_}I looked up, then down, then on each side, and everywhere I saw a sea of human faces, and thousands of eyes all staring at me.
10030-8{_}3539{_}I confess that I felt very much embarrassed--never more so in my life--and I knew not what to say.
10030-8{_}3540{_}I made a desperate effort, and a few words escaped me, but what they were I could not for the life of me tell, nor could any one else in the house.
10030-8{_}3541{_}My utterances were inaudible even to the leader of the orchestra, Mr. Dean, who was sitting only a few feet in front of me.
10030-8{_}3542{_}Bowing to the audience, I beat a hasty retreat into one of the cañons of the stage.
10030-8{_}3543{_}I never felt more relieved in my life than when I got out of the view of that immense crowd.
10030-8{_}3544{_}That evening Mr. Freleigh offered to give me five hundred dollars a week to play the part of "Buffalo Bill" myself.
10030-8{_}3545{_}I thought that he was certainly joking, especially as he had witnessed my awkward performance; but when he assured me that he was in earnest, I told him that it would be useless for me to attempt anything of the kind, for I never could talk to a crowd of people like that, even if it was to save my neck, and that he might as well try to make an actor out of a government mule.
10030-8{_}3546{_}I thanked him for the generous offer, which I had to decline owing to a lack of confidence in myself; or as some people might express it, I didn't have the requisite cheek to undertake a thing of that sort.
10030-8{_}3547{_}The play of "Buffalo Bill" had a very successful run of six or eight weeks, and was afterwards produced in all the principal cities of the country, everywhere being received with genuine enthusiasm.
10030-8{_}3548{_}I had been in New York about twenty days when General Sheridan arrived in the city.
10030-8{_}3549{_}I met him soon after he got into town.
10030-8{_}3550{_}In answer to a question how I was enjoying myself, I replied that I had struck the best camp I had ever seen, and if he didn't have any objections I would like to have my leave of absence extended about ten days.
10030-8{_}3551{_}This he willingly did, and then informed me that my services would soon be required at Fort McPherson, as there was to be an expedition sent out from that point.
10030-8{_}3552{_}At Westchester, Pennsylvania, I had some relatives living whom I had never seen, and now being so near, I determined to make them a visit.
10030-8{_}3553{_}Upon mentioning the matter to Buntline, he suggested that we should together take a trip to Philadelphia, and thence run out to Westchester.
10030-8{_}3554{_}Accordingly the next day found us in the "City of Brotherly Love," and in a few hours we arrived at the home of my uncle, General Henry R. Guss, the proprietor of the Green Tree Hotel, who gave us a cordial reception.
10030-8{_}3555{_}Inviting us into the parlor, my uncle brought in the members of his family, among them an elderly lady, who was my grandmother, as he informed me.
10030-8{_}3556{_}He told me that my Aunt Eliza, his first wife, was dead, and that he had married a second time; Lizzie Guss, my cousin, I thought was the most beautiful girl I had ever seen.
10030-8{_}3557{_}They were all very anxious to have us remain several days, but as I had some business to attend to in New York, I was obliged to return that day.
10030-8{_}3558{_}Assuring them, however, that I would visit them again soon, I bade them adieu, and with Buntline took the train for New York.
10030-8{_}3559{_}The time soon arrived for my departure for the West; so packing up my traps I started for home, and on the way thither I spent a day with my Westchester relatives, who did everything in their power to entertain me during my brief stay with them.
10030-8{_}3560{_}CHAPTER XXVII.
10030-8{_}3561{_}HONORS.
10030-8{_}3562{_}Upon reaching Fort McPherson, I found that the Third Cavalry, commanded by General Reynolds, had arrived from Arizona, in which Territory they had been on duty for some time, and where they had acquired quite a reputation on account of their Indian fighting qualities.
10030-8{_}3563{_}Shortly after my return, a small party of Indians made a dash on McPherson Station, about five miles from the fort, killing two or three men and running off quite a large number of horses.
10030-8{_}3564{_}Captain Meinhold and Lieutenant Lawson with their company were ordered out to pursue and punish the Indians if possible.
10030-8{_}3565{_}I was the guide of the expedition and had as an assistant T.B.
10030-8{_}3566{_}Omohundro, better known as "Texas Jack" and who was a scout at the post.
10030-8{_}3567{_}Finding the trail, I followed it for two days, although it was difficult trailing because the red-skins had taken every possible precaution to conceal their tracks.
10030-8{_}3568{_}On the second day Captain Meinhold went into camp on the South Fork of the Loupe, at a point where the trail was badly scattered.
10030-8{_}3569{_}Six men were detailed to accompany me on a scout in search of the camp of the fugitives.
10030-8{_}3570{_}We had gone but a short distance when we discovered Indians camped, not more than a mile away, with horses grazing near by.
10030-8{_}3571{_}They were only a small party, and I determined to charge upon them with my six men, rather than return to the command, because I feared they would see us as we went back and then they would get away from us entirely.
10030-8{_}3572{_}I asked the men if they were willing to attempt it, and they replied that they would follow me wherever I would lead them.
10030-8{_}3573{_}That was the kind of spirit that pleased me, and we immediately moved forward on the enemy, getting as close to them as possible without being seen.
10030-8{_}3574{_}I finally gave the signal to charge, and we dashed into the little camp with a yell.
10030-8{_}3575{_}Five Indians sprang out of a willow tepee, and greeted us with a volley, and we returned the fire.
10030-8{_}3576{_}I was riding Buckskin Joe, who with a few jumps brought me up to the tepee, followed by my men.
10030-8{_}3577{_}We nearly ran over the Indians who were endeavoring to reach their horses on the opposite side of the creek.
10030-8{_}3578{_}Just as one was jumping the narrow stream a bullet from my old "Lucretia" overtook him.
10030-8{_}3579{_}He never reached the other bank, but dropped dead in the water.
10030-8{_}3580{_}Those of the Indians who were guarding the horses, seeing what was going on at the camp, came rushing to the rescue of their friends.
10030-8{_}3581{_}I now counted thirteen braves, but as we had already disposed of two, we had only eleven to take care of.
10030-8{_}3582{_}The odds were nearly two to one against us.
10030-8{_}3583{_}While the Indian reinforcements were approaching the camp I jumped the creek with Buckskin Joe to meet them, expecting our party would follow me; but as they could not induce their horses to make the leap, I was the only one who got over.
10030-8{_}3584{_}I ordered the sergeant to dismount his men, and leaving one to hold the horses, to come over with the rest and help me drive the Indians off.
10030-8{_}3585{_}Before they could do this, two mounted warriors closed in on me and were shooting at short range.
10030-8{_}3586{_}I returned their fire and had the satisfaction of seeing one of them fall from his horse.
10030-8{_}3587{_}At this moment I felt blood trickling down my forehead, and hastily running my hand through my hair I discovered that I had received a scalp wound.
10030-8{_}3588{_}The Indian, who had shot me, was not more than ten yards away, and when he saw his partner tumble from his saddle, he turned to run.
10030-8{_}3589{_}By this time the soldiers had crossed the creek to assist me, and were blazing away at the other Indians.
10030-8{_}3590{_}Urging Buckskin Joe forward, I was soon alongside of the chap who had wounded me, when raising myself in the stirrups I shot him through the head.
10030-8{_}3591{_}The reports of our guns had been heard by Captain Meinhold, who at once started with his company up the creek to our aid, and when the remaining Indians, whom we were still fighting, saw these reinforcements coming they whirled their horses and fled; as their steeds were quite fresh they made their escape.
10030-8{_}3592{_}However, we killed six out of the thirteen Indians, and captured most of their stolen stock.
10030-8{_}3593{_}Our loss was one man killed, and one man--myself--slightly wounded.
10030-8{_}3594{_}One of our horses was killed, and Buckskin Joe was wounded, but I didn't discover the fact until some time afterwards as he had been shot in the breast and showed no signs of having received a scratch of any kind.
10030-8{_}3595{_}Securing the scalps of the dead Indians and other trophies we returned to the fort.
10030-8{_}3596{_}I made several other scouts during the summer with different officers of the Third Cavalry, one being with Major Alick Moore, a good officer, with whom I was out for thirty days.
10030-8{_}3597{_}Another long one was with Major Curtis, with whom I followed some Indians from the South Platte river to Fort Randall on the Missouri river in Dakota, on which trip the command ran out of rations and for fifteen days subsisted entirely upon the game we killed.
10030-8{_}3598{_}In the fall of 1872 the Earl of Dunraven and Dr. Kingsley with several friends came to Fort McPherson with a letter from General Sheridan, asking me to accompany them on an elk hunt.
10030-8{_}3599{_}I did so, and I afterwards spent several weeks in hunting with the Earl of Dunraven, who was a thorough sportsman and an excellent hunter.
10030-8{_}3600{_}It was while I was out with the Earl, that a Chicago party--friends of General Sheridan--arrived at Fort McPherson for the purpose of going out on a hunt.
10030-8{_}3601{_}They, too, had a letter from, the General requesting me to go with them.
10030-8{_}3602{_}The Earl had not yet finished his hunt, but as I had been out with him for several weeks, and he had by this time learned where to find plenty of elks and other game, I concluded to leave him and accompany the Chicago party.
10030-8{_}3603{_}I informed him of my intention and gave him my reasons for going, at the same time telling him that I would send him one of my scouts, Texas Jack, who was a good hunter, and would be glad to accompany him.
10030-8{_}3604{_}The Earl seemed to be somewhat offended at this, and I don't think he has ever forgiven me for "going back on him."
10030-8{_}3605{_}Let that be as it may, he found Texas Jack a splendid hunter and guide, and Jack has been his guide on several hunts since.
10030-8{_}3606{_}[Illustration: TEXAS JACK]  Among the gentlemen who composed the Chicago party were E.P.
10030-8{_}3607{_}Green,--son-in-law of Remington, the rifle manufacturer,--Alexander Sample, Mr. Milligan, of the firm of Heath & Milligan, of Chicago, and several others, whose names I do not now remember.
10030-8{_}3608{_}Mr. Milligan was a man full of life, and was continually "boiling over with fun."
10030-8{_}3609{_}He was a regular velocipede, so to speak, and was here, there, and everywhere.
10030-8{_}3610{_}He was exceedingly desirous of having an Indian fight on the trip, not that he was naturally a blood-thirsty man but just for variety he wanted a little "Indian pie."
10030-8{_}3611{_}He was in every respect the life of the party, during the entire time that we were out.
10030-8{_}3612{_}One day while he was hunting with Sample and myself we came in sight of a band of thirty mounted Indians.
10030-8{_}3613{_}"Milligan, here's what you've been wanting for some time," said I, "for yonder is a war party of Indians and no mistake; and they'll come for us, you bet."
10030-8{_}3614{_}"I don't believe this is one of my fighting days," replied Milligan, "and it occurs to me that I have urgent business at the camp."
10030-8{_}3615{_}Our camp was five or six miles distant on the Dismal river, and our escort consisted of a company of cavalry commanded by Captain Russell.
10030-8{_}3616{_}The soldiers were in camp, and Milligan thought that Captain Russell ought to be at once notified of the appearance of these Indians.
10030-8{_}3617{_}Knowing that we could reach the camp in safety, for we were well mounted, I continued to have considerable amusement at Milligan's expense, who finally said:  "Cody, what's making my hat raise up so.
10030-8{_}3618{_}I can hardly keep it on my head."
10030-8{_}3619{_}Sample, who was as cool as a cucumber, said to Milligan: "There must be something wrong with your hair.
10030-8{_}3620{_}It must be trying to get on end."
10030-8{_}3621{_}"It's all very fine for you fellows to stand here and talk," replied Milligan, "but I am not doing justice to my family by remaining.
10030-8{_}3622{_}Sample, I think we are a couple of old fools to have come out here, and I never would have done so if it had not been for you."
10030-8{_}3623{_}By this time the Indians had discovered us and were holding a consultation, and Milligan turned his horse in the direction of the camp.
10030-8{_}3624{_}I never believed that he was half as scared as he seemed to be, but that he was merely pretending so that we could enjoy our joke.
10030-8{_}3625{_}However, we did not wait any longer but rode into camp and notified Captain Russell, who immediately started with his company to pursue the band.
10030-8{_}3626{_}While we were riding along with the company Milligan said to Sample: "Now, Alick, let them come on.
10030-8{_}3627{_}We may yet go back to Chicago covered with glory."
10030-8{_}3628{_}We struck the trail going north, but as we had not come out on a scout for Indians, we concluded not to follow them; although Milligan was now very anxious to proceed and clean them out.
10030-8{_}3629{_}The hunt came to an end in a day or two, and we escorted the visiting hunters to North Platte, where they took the train for Chicago.
10030-8{_}3630{_}Before their departure they extended to me a very cordial invitation to come to their city on a visit, promising that I should be well taken care of.
10030-8{_}3631{_}Soon after this I had the pleasure of guiding a party of gentlemen from Omaha on a buffalo hunt.
10030-8{_}3632{_}Among the number were Judge Dundy, Colonel Watson B. Smith, and U.S. District Attorney Neville.
10030-8{_}3633{_}We left Fort McPherson in good trim.
10030-8{_}3634{_}I was greatly amused at the "style" of Mr. Neville, who wore a stove-pipe hat and a swallow-tail coat, which made up a very comical rig for a buffalo hunter.
10030-8{_}3635{_}As we galloped over the prairie, he jammed his hat down over his ears to keep it from being shaken off his head, and in order to stick to his horse, he clung to the pommel of his saddle.
10030-8{_}3636{_}He was not much of a rider, and he went bouncing up and down, with his swallow-tails flopping in the air.
10030-8{_}3637{_}The sight I shall never forget, for it was enough to make a "horse laugh," and I actually believe old Buckskin Joe did laugh.
10030-8{_}3638{_}However, we had a splendid hunt, and on the second day I lariated, or roped, a big buffalo bull and tied him to a tree,--a feat which I had often performed, and which the gentlemen requested me to do on this occasion for their benefit, as they had heard of my skill with the lariat.
10030-8{_}3639{_}I captured several other buffaloes in the same way.
10030-8{_}3640{_}The gentlemen returned to Omaha well pleased with their hunt.
10030-8{_}3641{_}In the fall of the year, 1872, a convention was held at Grand Island, when some of my friends made me their candidate to represent the Twenty-sixth District in the legislature of Nebraska; but as I had always been a Democrat and the State was largely Republican, I had no idea of being elected.
10030-8{_}3642{_}In fact I cared very little about it, and therefore made no effort whatever to secure an election.
10030-8{_}3643{_}However, I was elected and that is the way in which I acquired my title of Honorable.
10030-8{_}3644{_}CHAPTER XXVIII.
10030-8{_}3645{_}AN ACTOR.
10030-8{_}3646{_}During the summer and fall of 1872, I received numerous letters from Ned Buntline, urging me to come East and go upon the stage to represent my own character.
10030-8{_}3647{_}"There's money in it," he wrote, "and you will prove a big card, as your character is a novelty on the stage."
10030-8{_}3648{_}At times I almost determined to make the venture; but the recollection of that night when I stood on the stage of the Bowery Theatre and was unable to utter a word above a whisper, would cause me to stop and think and become irresolute.
10030-8{_}3649{_}I feared that I would be a total failure, and wrote Buntline to that effect.
10030-8{_}3650{_}But he insisted that I would soon get over all that embarrassment, and become accustomed to the stage, so that I would think no more of appearing before five thousand people than I would before half a dozen.
10030-8{_}3651{_}He proposed to organize a good company, and wished me to meet him in Chicago, where the opening performance would be given.
10030-8{_}3652{_}I remained undecided as to what I ought to do.
10030-8{_}3653{_}The officers at the fort as well as my family and friends to whom I had mentioned the matter, laughed at the idea of my ever becoming an actor.
10030-8{_}3654{_}That I, an old scout who had never seen more than twenty or thirty theatrical performances in my life, should think of going upon the stage, was ridiculous in the extreme--so they all said.
10030-8{_}3655{_}A few days after my election to the legislature a happy event occurred in my family circle, in the birth of a daughter whom we named Ora; about the same time I received another letter from Buntline, in which he requested me to appear on the stage for a few months as an experiment; and he said that if I made a failure or did not like the business, I could easily return to my old life.
10030-8{_}3656{_}My two sisters who had been living with us had married,--Nellie, to A. C. Jester, a cattle man, and May, to Ed.
10030-8{_}3657{_}Bradford, a railroad engineer--and consequently left us; and my wife had been wishing for a long time to visit her parents in St. Louis.
10030-8{_}3658{_}Taking these and other things into consideration I finally resolved to resign my seat in the legislature and try my luck behind the footlights.
10030-8{_}3659{_}I informed General Reynolds of my determination, telling him at the same time that at the end of the month, November, I would resign my position under him.
10030-8{_}3660{_}The General regretted to hear this, and advised me not to take the step, for I was leaving a comfortable little home, where I was sure of making a good living for my family; while, on the other hand, I was embarking upon a sea of uncertainty.
10030-8{_}3661{_}Having once made up my mind, however, nothing could change it.
10030-8{_}3662{_}While I was selling my horses and other effects, preparatory to leaving the fort, one of my brother scouts, Texas Jack, said that he would like to accompany me.
10030-8{_}3663{_}Now as Jack had also appeared as the hero in one of Ned Buntline's stories, I thought that he would make as good a "star" as myself, and it was accordingly arranged that Jack should go with me.
10030-8{_}3664{_}On our way East we stopped in Omaha a day or two to visit General Augur and other officers, and also the gentlemen who were out on the Judge Dundy hunt.
10030-8{_}3665{_}Judge Dundy and his friends gave a dinner party in my honor at the leading restaurant and entertained me very handsomely during my stay in the city.
10030-8{_}3666{_}At Omaha I parted with my family, who went to St. Louis, while Jack and myself proceeded to Chicago.
10030-8{_}3667{_}Ned Buntline and Mr. Milligan, having been apprised of our coming by a telegram, met us at the dépôt.
10030-8{_}3668{_}Mr. Milligan accompanied us to the Sherman House, where he had made arrangements for us to be his guests while we remained in the city.
10030-8{_}3669{_}I didn't see much of Buntline that evening, as he hurried off to deliver a temperance lecture in one of the public halls.
10030-8{_}3670{_}The next day we met him by appointment, and the first thing he said, was:  "Boys, are you ready for business?"
10030-8{_}3671{_}"I can't answer that," replied I, "for we don't know what we are going to do."
10030-8{_}3672{_}"It's all arranged," said he, "and you'll have no trouble whatever.
10030-8{_}3673{_}Come with me.
10030-8{_}3674{_}We'll go and see Nixon, manager of the Amphitheatre.
10030-8{_}3675{_}That's the place where we are to play.
10030-8{_}3676{_}We'll open there next Monday night."
10030-8{_}3677{_}Jack and myself accordingly accompanied him to manager Nixon's office without saying a word, as we didn't know what to say.
10030-8{_}3678{_}"Here we are, Mr. Nixon," said Buntline; "here are the stars for you.
10030-8{_}3679{_}Here are the boys; and they are a fine pair to draw too.
10030-8{_}3680{_}Now, Nixon, I am prepared for business."
10030-8{_}3681{_}Nixon and Buntline had evidently had a talk about the terms of our engagement.
10030-8{_}3682{_}Buntline, it seems, was to furnish the company, the drama, and the pictorial printing, and was to receive sixty per cent.
10030-8{_}3683{_}of the gross receipts for his share; while Nixon was to furnish the theater, the _attachés_, the orchestra, and the local printing; and receive forty per cent.
10030-8{_}3684{_}of the gross receipts.
10030-8{_}3685{_}"I am ready for you, Buntline.
10030-8{_}3686{_}Have you got your company yet?"
10030-8{_}3687{_}asked Nixon.
10030-8{_}3688{_}"No, sir; but there are plenty of idle theatrical people in town, and I can raise a company in two hours," was his reply.
10030-8{_}3689{_}"You haven't much time to spare, if you open on Monday night," said Nixon.
10030-8{_}3690{_}"If you will allow me to look at your drama, to see what kind of people you want, I'll assist you in organizing your company."
10030-8{_}3691{_}"I have not yet written the drama," said Buntline.
10030-8{_}3692{_}"What the deuce do you mean?
10030-8{_}3693{_}This is Wednesday, and you propose to open on next Monday night.
10030-8{_}3694{_}The idea is ridiculous.
10030-8{_}3695{_}Here you are at this late hour without a company and without a drama.
10030-8{_}3696{_}This will never do, Buntline.
10030-8{_}3697{_}I shall have to break my contract with you, for you can't possibly write a drama, cast it, and rehearse it properly for Monday night.
10030-8{_}3698{_}Furthermore, you have no pictorial printing as yet.
10030-8{_}3699{_}These two gentlemen, whom you have with you, have never been on the stage, and they certainly must have time to study their parts.
10030-8{_}3700{_}It is preposterous to think of opening on Monday night, and I'll cancel the engagement."
10030-8{_}3701{_}This little speech was delivered in rather an excited manner by Mr. Nixon.
10030-8{_}3702{_}Buntline said that he would write the drama that day and also select his company and have them at the theater for rehearsal next morning.
10030-8{_}3703{_}Nixon laughed at him, and said that there was no use of trying to undertake anything of the kind in so short a time--it was utterly impossible to do it.
10030-8{_}3704{_}Buntline, whose ire was rising, said to Nixon:  "What rent will you ask for your theater for next week?"
10030-8{_}3705{_}"Six hundred dollars," was the reply.
10030-8{_}3706{_}"Well, sir, I'll take your theater for next week at that price, and here is half of the amount in advance," said Buntline, as he threw down three hundred dollars on the stand.
10030-8{_}3707{_}Nixon took the money, gave a receipt for it, and had nothing more to say.
10030-8{_}3708{_}"Now, come with me boys," said Buntline; and away we went to the hotel.
10030-8{_}3709{_}Buntline immediately obtained a supply of pens, ink and paper, and then engaged all the hotel clerks as penmen.
10030-8{_}3710{_}In less than an hour after he had rented the theater, he was dashing off page after page of his proposed drama--the work being done in his room at the hotel.
10030-8{_}3711{_}He then set his clerks at copying for him, and at the end of four hours, he jumped up from the table, and enthusiastically shouted:  "Hurrah for 'The Scouts of the Plains!'
10030-8{_}3712{_}That's the name of the play.
10030-8{_}3713{_}The work is done.
10030-8{_}3714{_}Hurrah!"
10030-8{_}3715{_}The parts were then all copied off separately by the clerks, and handing us our respective portions Buntline said:  "Now, boys, go to work, and do your level best to have this dead-letter perfect for the rehearsal, which takes place to-morrow morning at ten o'clock, prompt.
10030-8{_}3716{_}I want to show Nixon that we'll be ready on time."
10030-8{_}3717{_}[Illustration: STUDYING THE PARTS.]
10030-8{_}3718{_}I looked at my part and then at Jack; and Jack looked at his part and then at me.
10030-8{_}3719{_}Then we looked at each other, and then at Buntline.
10030-8{_}3720{_}We did not know what to make of the man.
10030-8{_}3721{_}"How long will it take you to commit your part to memory, Bill?"
10030-8{_}3722{_}asked Jack.
10030-8{_}3723{_}"About six months, as near as I can calculate.
10030-8{_}3724{_}How long will it take you?"
10030-8{_}3725{_}answered I.
10030-8{_}3726{_}"It will take me about that length of time to learn the first line," said Jack.
10030-8{_}3727{_}Nevertheless we went to our room and commenced studying.
10030-8{_}3728{_}I thought it was the hardest work I had ever done.
10030-8{_}3729{_}"This is dry business," finally remarked Jack.
10030-8{_}3730{_}"That's just what it is," I answered; "jerk the bell, Jack."
10030-8{_}3731{_}The bell-boy soon appeared.
10030-8{_}3732{_}We ordered refreshments; after partaking thereof we resumed our task.
10030-8{_}3733{_}We studied hard for an hour or two, but finally gave it up as a bad job, although we had succeeded in committing a small portion to memory.
10030-8{_}3734{_}Buntline now came into the room and said:  "Boys, how are you getting along?"
10030-8{_}3735{_}"I guess we'll have to go back on this studying business as it isn't our _forte_" said I.
10030-8{_}3736{_}"Don't weaken now, Bill; you'll come out on the top of the heap yet.
10030-8{_}3737{_}Let me hear you recite your part," said Buntline.
10030-8{_}3738{_}I began "spouting" what I had learned, but was interrupted by Buntline:  "Tut!
10030-8{_}3739{_}tut!
10030-8{_}3740{_}you're not saying it right.
10030-8{_}3741{_}You must stop at the cue."
10030-8{_}3742{_}"Cue!
10030-8{_}3743{_}What the mischief do you mean by the cue?
10030-8{_}3744{_}I never saw any cue except in a billiard room," said I. Buntline thereupon explained it to me, as well as to Jack, who was ignorant as myself concerning the "cue" business.
10030-8{_}3745{_}"Jack, I think we had better back out and go to hunting again," said I.
10030-8{_}3746{_}"See here, boys; it won't do to go back on me at this stage of the game.
10030-8{_}3747{_}Stick to it, and it may be the turning point in your lives and lead you on to fortune and to fame."
10030-8{_}3748{_}"A fortune is what we are after, and we'll at least give the wheel a turn or two and see what luck we have," said I.
10030-8{_}3749{_}This satisfied Buntline, but we didn't study any more after he left us.
10030-8{_}3750{_}The next morning we appeared at rehearsal and were introduced to the company.
10030-8{_}3751{_}The first rehearsal was hardly a success; and the succeeding ones were not much better.
10030-8{_}3752{_}The stage manager did his best to teach Jack and myself what to do, but when Monday night came we didn't know much more about it than when we began.
10030-8{_}3753{_}The clock struck seven, and then we put on our buckskin suits, which were the costumes we were to appear in.
10030-8{_}3754{_}The theater was being rapidly filled, and it was evident that we were going to make our _début_ before a packed house.
10030-8{_}3755{_}As the minutes passed by, Jack and I became more and more nervous.
10030-8{_}3756{_}We occasionally looked through the holes in the curtain, and saw that the people were continuing to crowd into the theatre; our nervousness increased to an uncomfortable degree.
10030-8{_}3757{_}When, at length the curtain arose, our courage had returned, so that we thought we could face the immense crowd; yet when the time came for us to go on, we were rather slow in making our appearance.
10030-8{_}3758{_}As we stepped forth we were received with a storm of applause, which we acknowledged with a bow.
10030-8{_}3759{_}[Illustration: BEHIND THE FOOTLIGHTS.]
10030-8{_}3760{_}Buntline, who was taking the part of "Cale Durg," appeared, and gave me the "cue" to speak "my little piece," but for the life of me I could not remember a single word.
10030-8{_}3761{_}Buntline saw I was "stuck," and a happy thought occurred to him.
10030-8{_}3762{_}He said--as if it were in the play:  "Where have you been, Bill?
10030-8{_}3763{_}What has kept you so long?"
10030-8{_}3764{_}Just then my eye happened to fall on Mr. Milligan, who was surrounded by his friends, the newspaper reporters, and several military officers, all of whom had heard of his hunt and "Indian fight"--he being a very popular man, and widely known in Chicago.
10030-8{_}3765{_}So I said:  "I have been out on a hunt with Milligan."
10030-8{_}3766{_}This proved to be a big hit.
10030-8{_}3767{_}The audience cheered and applauded; which gave me greater confidence in my ability to get through the performance all right.
10030-8{_}3768{_}Buntline, who is a very versatile man, saw that it would be a good plan to follow this up, and he said:  "Well, Bill, tell us all about the hunt."
10030-8{_}3769{_}I thereupon proceeded to relate in detail the particulars of the affair.
10030-8{_}3770{_}I succeeded in making it rather funny, and I was frequently interrupted by rounds of applause.
10030-8{_}3771{_}Whenever I began to "weaken," Buntline would give me a fresh start, by asking some question.
10030-8{_}3772{_}In this way I took up fifteen minutes, without once speaking a word of my part; nor did I speak a word of it during the whole evening.
10030-8{_}3773{_}The prompter, who was standing between the wings, attempted to prompt me, but it did no good; for while I was on the stage I "chipped in" anything I thought of.
10030-8{_}3774{_}"The Scouts of the Plains" was an Indian drama, of course; and there were between forty and fifty "supers" dressed as Indians.
10030-8{_}3775{_}In the fight with them, Jack and I were at home.
10030-8{_}3776{_}We blazed away at each other with blank cartridges; and when the scene ended in a hand-to-hand encounter--a general knock-down and drag-out--the way Jack and I killed Indians was "a caution."
10030-8{_}3777{_}We would kill them all off in one act, but they would come up again ready for business in the next.
10030-8{_}3778{_}Finally the curtain dropped; the play was ended; and I congratulated Jack and myself on having made such a brilliant and-successful _début_.
10030-8{_}3779{_}There was no backing out after that.
10030-8{_}3780{_}The next morning there appeared in the Chicago papers some very funny criticisms on our first performance.
10030-8{_}3781{_}The papers gave us a better send-off than I expected, for they did not criticise us as actors.
10030-8{_}3782{_}The _Chicago Times_ said that if Buntline had actually spent four hours in writing that play, it was difficult for any one to see what he had been doing all the time.
10030-8{_}3783{_}Buntline, as "Cale Durg," was killed in the second act, after a long temperance speech; and the _Inter-Ocean_ said that it was to be regretted that he had not been killed in the first act.
10030-8{_}3784{_}The company, however, was very good, and Mdlle.
10030-8{_}3785{_}Morlacchi, as "Pale Dove," particularly fine; while Miss Cafarno "spouted" a poem of some seven hundred and three verses, more or less, of which the reader will be glad to know that I only recall the words "I was born in March."
10030-8{_}3786{_}Our engagement proved a decided success financially, if not artistically.
10030-8{_}3787{_}Nixon was greatly surprised at the result, and at the end of the week he induced Buntline to take him in as a partner in the company.
10030-8{_}3788{_}The next week we played at DeBar's Opera House, in St. Louis, doing an immense business.
10030-8{_}3789{_}The following week we were at Cincinnati, where the theater was so crowded every night that hundreds were unable to obtain admission.
10030-8{_}3790{_}We met with equal success all over the country.
10030-8{_}3791{_}Theatrical managers, upon hearing of this new and novel combination; which was drawing such tremendous houses, were all anxious to secure us; and we received offers of engagements at all the leading theaters.
10030-8{_}3792{_}We played one week at the Boston Theater, and the gross receipts amounted to $16,200.
10030-8{_}3793{_}We also appeared at Niblo's Garden, New York, the theater being crowded to its utmost capacity every night of the engagement.
10030-8{_}3794{_}At the Arch Street Theater, Philadelphia, it was the same way.
10030-8{_}3795{_}There was not a single city where we did not have crowded houses.
10030-8{_}3796{_}We closed our tour on the 16th of June, 1873, at Port Jervis, New York, and when I counted up my share of the profits I found that I was only about $6,000 ahead.
10030-8{_}3797{_}I was somewhat disappointed, for, judging from our large business, I certainly had expected a greater sum.
10030-8{_}3798{_}Texas Jack and myself longed for a hunt on the Western prairies once more; and on meeting in New York a party of gentlemen who were desirous of going with us, we all started Westward, and after a pleasant trip arrived at Fort McPherson.
10030-8{_}3799{_}CHAPTER XXIX.
10030-8{_}3800{_}STARRING.
10030-8{_}3801{_}Texas Jack and I spent several weeks in hunting in the western part of Nebraska, and at the end of our vacation we felt greatly re-invigorated and ready for another theatrical campaign.
10030-8{_}3802{_}We accordingly proceeded to New York and organized a company for the season of 1873-74.
10030-8{_}3803{_}Thinking that Wild Bill would be quite an acquisition to the troupe, we wrote to him at Springfield, Missouri, offering him a large salary if he would play with us that winter.
10030-8{_}3804{_}He was doing nothing at the time, and we thought that he would like to take a trip through the States, as he had never been East.
10030-8{_}3805{_}Wild Bill accepted our offer, and came on to New York; though he told us from the start that we could never make an actor out of him.
10030-8{_}3806{_}Although he had a fine stage appearance and was a handsome fellow, and possessed a good strong voice, yet when he went upon the stage before an audience, it was almost impossible for him to utter a word.
10030-8{_}3807{_}He insisted that we were making a set of fools of ourselves, and that we were the laughing-stock of the people.
10030-8{_}3808{_}I replied that I did not care for that, as long as they came and bought tickets to see us.
10030-8{_}3809{_}Wild Bill was continually playing tricks upon the members of the company, and it was his especial delight to torment the "supers."
10030-8{_}3810{_}Quite frequently in our sham Indian battles he would run up to the "Indians" (the supers), and putting his pistol close to their legs, would fire at them and burn them with the powder, instead of shooting over their heads.
10030-8{_}3811{_}This would make them dance and jump, so that it was difficult to make them fall and die--although they were paid twenty-five cents each for performing the "dying business."
10030-8{_}3812{_}The poor "supers" often complained to me about this, and threatened not to go on the stage and be killed again if that man Wild Bill did not stop shooting and burning their legs.
10030-8{_}3813{_}I would order Wild Bill to stop his mischief; he would laugh and then promise not to do it any more.
10030-8{_}3814{_}But it would not be long before he was at his old tricks again.
10030-8{_}3815{_}My company, known as the "Buffalo Bill Combination," did a fine business, all through the East.
10030-8{_}3816{_}Wild Bill continued his pranks, which caused us considerable annoyance, but at the same time greatly amused us.
10030-8{_}3817{_}One day at Titusville, Pennsylvania, while Burke, the business agent, was registering our names and making arrangements for our accommodation, several of us started for the billiard room; but were met by the landlord, who stopped me and said that there was a party of roughs from the lower oil region who were spreeing, and had boasted that they were staying in town to meet the Buffalo Bill gang and clean them out.
10030-8{_}3818{_}The landlord begged of me not to allow the members of the troupe to enter the billiard room, as he did not wish any fight in his house.
10030-8{_}3819{_}To please the landlord, and at his suggestion, I called the boys up into the parlor and explained to them the situation.
10030-8{_}3820{_}Wild Bill wanted to go at once and fight the whole mob, but I persuaded him to keep away from them during the day.
10030-8{_}3821{_}In order to entirely avoid the roughs, the members of the company entered the theater through a private door from the hotel, as the two buildings joined each other.
10030-8{_}3822{_}While I was standing at the door of the theater taking the tickets, the landlord of the hotel came rushing up and said that Wild Bill was having a fight with the roughs in the bar-room.
10030-8{_}3823{_}It seemed that Bill had not been able to resist the temptation of going to see what kind of a mob it was that wanted to test the pluck of the Buffalo Bill party; and just as he stepped into the room, one of the bruisers put his hand on his shoulder and said:  "Hello, Buffalo Bill!
10030-8{_}3824{_}we have been looking for you all day."
10030-8{_}3825{_}"My name is not Buffalo Bill; you are mistaken in the man," was the reply.
10030-8{_}3826{_}"You are a liar!"
10030-8{_}3827{_}said the bruiser.
10030-8{_}3828{_}Bill instantly knocked him down, and then seizing a chair he laid out four or five of the crowd on the floor, and drove the rest out of the room.
10030-8{_}3829{_}All this was done in a minute or two, and by the time I got down stairs, Bill was coming out of the bar-room, whistling a lively tune.
10030-8{_}3830{_}"Well!"
10030-8{_}3831{_}said he, "I have been interviewing that party who wanted to clean us out."
10030-8{_}3832{_}"I thought you promised to come into the Opera House by the private entrance?"
10030-8{_}3833{_}"I did try to follow that trail, but I got lost among the cañons, and then I ran in among the hostiles," said he; "but it is all right now.
10030-8{_}3834{_}They won't bother us any more.
10030-8{_}3835{_}I guess those fellows have found us."
10030-8{_}3836{_}And sure enough they had.
10030-8{_}3837{_}We heard no more of them after that.
10030-8{_}3838{_}Another incident occurred, one night, at Portland, Maine.
10030-8{_}3839{_}Bill found it impossible to go to sleep at the hotel on account of the continued talking of some parties who were engaged in a game of cards in an adjoining room.
10030-8{_}3840{_}He called to them several times to make less noise, but they paid little or no attention to him.
10030-8{_}3841{_}He finally got up and went to the room with the intention of cleaning out the whole crowd.
10030-8{_}3842{_}He knocked and was admitted; greatly to his surprise, he found the party to be some merchants of the city, whom he had met the previous day.
10030-8{_}3843{_}They were playing poker, and invited him to take a hand.
10030-8{_}3844{_}Bill sat down at the table, and said that, inasmuch as they would not let him sleep, he wouldn't mind playing for a while, provided they would post him a little in the game, for he didn't know much about it.
10030-8{_}3845{_}At first he didn't play very well, intentionally making many blunders and asking numerous questions; but when morning came, he was about seven hundred dollars ahead.
10030-8{_}3846{_}Bill put the money in his pocket, and just as he was leaving the room he advised them never to wake a man up and invite him to play poker.
10030-8{_}3847{_}[Illustration: LEARNING THE GAME.]
10030-8{_}3848{_}Wild Bill remained with me until we reached Rochester.
10030-8{_}3849{_}I met my family there, and having bought some property in that city, with the intention of making the place my home, I asked Bill not to cut up any of his capers, for I wanted the performance to go off smoothly, as I expected a large audience that evening.
10030-8{_}3850{_}He, of course, promised to behave himself.
10030-8{_}3851{_}When the curtain rose the house was crowded.
10030-8{_}3852{_}The play proceeded finely until the Indian fight in the second act, when Bill amused himself by his old trick of singeing the legs of the "supers."
10030-8{_}3853{_}After the curtain dropped, the "supers" complained to me about it.
10030-8{_}3854{_}Bill's conduct made me angry, and I told him that he must either stop shooting the "supers," or leave the company.
10030-8{_}3855{_}He made no reply, but went to the dressing-room and changed his buckskin suit for his citizen's dress, and during one of my scenes I looked down in front and saw him elbowing his way through the audience and out of the theater.
10030-8{_}3856{_}When I had finished the scene, and had retired from the stage, the stage-carpenter came up and said:  "That long-haired gentleman, who passed out a few minutes ago, requested me to tell you that you could go to thunder with your old show."
10030-8{_}3857{_}That was the last time that Wild Bill and I ever performed together on the stage.
10030-8{_}3858{_}After the evening's entertainment I met him at the Osborn House.
10030-8{_}3859{_}By this time he had recovered from his mad fit and was in as good humor as ever.
10030-8{_}3860{_}He had made up his mind to leave for the West the next day.
10030-8{_}3861{_}I endeavored to persuade him to remain with me till spring, and then we would go together; but it was of no use.
10030-8{_}3862{_}I then paid him the money due him, and Jack and myself made him a present of $1,000 besides.
10030-8{_}3863{_}Bill went to New York the next day, intending to start west from there.
10030-8{_}3864{_}Several days afterwards I learned that he had lost all his money in New York by playing faro; also that a theatrical manager had engaged him to play.
10030-8{_}3865{_}A company was organized and started out, but as a "star" Wild Bill was not a success; the further he went the poorer he got.
10030-8{_}3866{_}This didn't suit Bill by any means, and he accordingly retired from the stage.
10030-8{_}3867{_}The company, however, kept on the road, using Bill's name, and employing an actor to represent him not only on the stage but on the street and elsewhere.
10030-8{_}3868{_}Bill heard of this deception and sent word to the manager to stop it, but no attention was paid to his message.
10030-8{_}3869{_}Finally, Bill resolved to have satisfaction and he proceeded to a town where the company was to play; he entered the theater and took a seat near the stage, and watched the performance until the bogus Wild Bill appeared.
10030-8{_}3870{_}He then sprang upon the stage, knocked the actor clear through one of the scenes, and grabbing the manager by the shoulders he threw him over the foot-lights into the orchestra.
10030-8{_}3871{_}[Illustration: GETTING SATISFACTION.]
10030-8{_}3872{_}The other actors screamed and yelled "Police!"
10030-8{_}3873{_}The audience could not at first understand what it all meant, some of them supposing the affair to be a part of the play.
10030-8{_}3874{_}Wild Bill retired from the stage in good order, resumed his seat, and told them to go on with their show.
10030-8{_}3875{_}A policeman now appearing, Bill was pointed out as the disturber of the peace; the officer tapping him on the shoulder, said:  "I'll have to arrest you, sir."
10030-8{_}3876{_}"How many of you are there?"
10030-8{_}3877{_}asked Bill.
10030-8{_}3878{_}"Only myself," said the policeman.
10030-8{_}3879{_}"You had better get some help," said Bill.
10030-8{_}3880{_}The officer then called up another policeman, and Bill again asked:  "How many of you are there now?"
10030-8{_}3881{_}"Two," was the reply.
10030-8{_}3882{_}"Then I advise you to go out and get some more reinforcements," said Bill, very coolly.
10030-8{_}3883{_}The policemen thereupon spoke to the sheriff, who was dressed in citizen's clothes.
10030-8{_}3884{_}The sheriff came up and said he would have to take him into custody.
10030-8{_}3885{_}"All right, sir," replied Bill, "I have no objections to walking out with you, but I won't go with any two policemen."
10030-8{_}3886{_}At the court next morning Bill stated his reasons for having acted as he had done, and the judge fined him only three dollars and costs.
10030-8{_}3887{_}This was the last time that Wild Bill appeared on the stage.
10030-8{_}3888{_}He shortly afterwards returned to the West, and on arriving at Cheyenne, he visited Boulder's gambling room and sat down at a faro table.
10030-8{_}3889{_}No one in the room recognized him, as he had not been in Cheyenne for several years.
10030-8{_}3890{_}After losing two or three bets he threw down a fifty dollar bill and lost that also.
10030-8{_}3891{_}Boulder quietly raked in the money.
10030-8{_}3892{_}Bill placed a second fifty dollar note on another card, when Boulder informed him that the limit was twenty-five dollars.
10030-8{_}3893{_}"You have just taken in a fifty dollar bill which I lost," said Bill.
10030-8{_}3894{_}"Well you needn't make any more such bets, as I will not go above my limit," replied Boulder.
10030-8{_}3895{_}"I'll just play that fifty dollar bill as it lays.
10030-8{_}3896{_}If it loses, it's yours; if it wins, you'll pay me fifty dollars, or I'll know the reason why."
10030-8{_}3897{_}"I am running this game, and I want no talk from you, sir," said Boulder.
10030-8{_}3898{_}One word brought on another, until Boulder threatened to have Bill put out of the house.
10030-8{_}3899{_}Bill was carrying the butt end of a billiard cue for a cane, and bending over the table, he said: "You'd rob a blind man."
10030-8{_}3900{_}Then he suddenly tapped Boulder on the head with the cane, with such force as to knock him over.
10030-8{_}3901{_}With another sweep of the cane he tumbled the "look-out" from his chair, and then reaching over into the money drawer he grabbed a handful of greenbacks and stuck them in his pocket.
10030-8{_}3902{_}At this stage of the game four or five men--who were employed as "bouncers" for the establishment to throw out the noisy persons--rushed up to capture Bill, but he knocked them right and left with his cane, and seeing the whole crowd was now closing in on him, he jumped into a corner, and with each hand drew a revolver and faced the enemy.
10030-8{_}3903{_}At this moment the bar-keeper recognized him, and sang out in a loud voice:  "Look out boys--that's Wild Bill you've run against."
10030-8{_}3904{_}That settled the matter; for when they heard the name of Wild Bill they turned and beat a hasty retreat out of the doors and windows, and in less time than it takes to tell it, Wild Bill was the only man in the room.
10030-8{_}3905{_}He coolly walked over to Dyer's hotel, and retired for the night.
10030-8{_}3906{_}Boulder claimed that he had taken $500, but he really got only $200.
10030-8{_}3907{_}Boulder, upon learning that it was Wild Bill who had cleaned him out, said nothing more about the money.
10030-8{_}3908{_}The next day the two men met over a bottle of wine, and settled their differences in an amicable manner.
10030-8{_}3909{_}Poor Bill was afterwards killed at Deadwood, in the Black Hills, in a cowardly manner, by a desperado who sneaked up behind him while he was playing a game of cards in a saloon, and shot him through the back of the head, without the least provocation.
10030-8{_}3910{_}The murderer, Jack McCall, was tried and hung at Yankton, Dakotah, for the crime.
10030-8{_}3911{_}Thus ended the career of a life-long friend of mine who, in spite of his many faults, was a noble man, ever brave and generous hearted.
10030-8{_}3912{_}Jack and myself continued playing through the country after Wild Bill left us, and we finally closed our season in Boston on the 13th of May, 1874.  Business called me from Boston to New York, and after I had been there a few days, I met an English gentleman, Thomas P. Medley, of London, who had come to America for a hunt on the Plains.
10030-8{_}3913{_}He had often heard of me, and was anxious to engage me as his guide and companion, and he offered to pay the liberal salary of one thousand dollars a month while I was with him.
10030-8{_}3914{_}He was a very wealthy man, as I learned upon inquiry, and was a relative of Mr. Lord, of the firm of Lord & Taylor, of New York.
10030-8{_}3915{_}Of course I accepted his offer.
10030-8{_}3916{_}When we reached the hunting ground in Nebraska, he informed me, somewhat to my surprise, that he did not want to go out as Alexis did, with carriages, servants, and other luxuries, but that he wished to rough it just as I would do--to sleep on the ground in the open air, and kill and cook his own meat.
10030-8{_}3917{_}We started out from North Platte, and spent several weeks in hunting all over the county.
10030-8{_}3918{_}Dr. W. F. Carver, who then resided at North Platte, and who has recently acquired considerable notoriety as a rifle-shot, hunted with us for a few days.
10030-8{_}3919{_}Mr. Medley proved to be a very agreeable gentleman and an excellent hunter.
10030-8{_}3920{_}While in camp he busied himself in carrying wood and water, attending to the fire, and preparing and cooking the meals, never asking me to do a thing.
10030-8{_}3921{_}He did not do this to save expenses, but because he wanted to do as the other hunters in the party were doing.
10030-8{_}3922{_}After spending as much time as he wished, we returned to the railroad, and he took the train for the East.
10030-8{_}3923{_}Everything that was required on this hunt was paid for in the most liberal manner by Mr. Medley, who also gave the members of the party several handsome presents.
10030-8{_}3924{_}About this time an expedition consisting of seven companies of cavalry and two companies of infantry--to be commanded by Colonel Mills of the Third Cavalry, was being organized to scout the Powder River and Big Horn country, and I was employed as guide for the command.
10030-8{_}3925{_}Proceeding to Rawlins, Wyoming, we "outfitted," and other guides were engaged--among them Tom Sun and Bony Ernest, two noted Rocky Mountain scouts.
10030-8{_}3926{_}We there left the railroad, and passing through the Seminole range of the Rocky Mountains we established our supply camp at the foot of Independence Rock on the Sweetwater.
10030-8{_}3927{_}I was now on my old familiar stamping ground, and it seemed like home to me.
10030-8{_}3928{_}Fifteen years before, I had ridden the pony express and driven the overland stages through this region, and the command was going into the same section of country where Wild Bill's expedition of stage-drivers and express-riders had recaptured from the Indians a large number of stolen stage-horses.
10030-8{_}3929{_}Leaving the infantry to guard the supply camp, Colonel Mills struck out for the north with the seven companies of cavalry.
10030-8{_}3930{_}One day while we were resting on a prairie near the head of Powder river, a horseman was seen in the distance approaching us.
